PREFACE
This Master Thesis is the result of work conducted by Steffen Grundahl Thomassen and
Jonas Pedersen at the Department of DTU Wind and Energy Systems of the Technical
University of Denmark (DTU). The work was performed in fulfillment of the requirements
for obtaining the degree Master of Science in Electrical Engineering at DTU. The project
work have been carried out between the 24th of January and the 24th of June 2022 under
supervision of Mattia Marinelli (Associate Professor at DTU), Tatiana Gabderakhmanova
(Postdoctoral researcher at DTU), Jan Engelhardt (PhD student at DTU), Jan Martin
Zepter (PhD student at DTU), Zoltan Mark Pinter (PhD student at DTU), and Christian
Nielsen (Embedded Hardware Developer at Nerve Smart Systems ApS).
The Master Thesis counts for 30 ECTS.

==PAGE:7==
ABSTRACT
The transition from fossil fuels to renewable energy presents challenges for the power
grid system, where the intermittent production of renewable energy sources (RESs) and
the charging of electric vehicles (EVs) can potentially led to stability issues and network
overloading. To investigate potential solutions to these problems, a multi-purpose battery
energy storage system (BESS) has been installed on Bornholm as part of the H2020
INSULAE project. The system serves as a power buffer between the local distribution
grid, a photovoltaic (PV) generator and two EV high power charging (HPC) points. These
components together serve as a HPC station. The optimal sizing of the system components
is crucial to satisfy customer demand and mitigate unnecessary costs, resulting in more
attractive systems to deploy in the future.
As part of the INSULAE project, this thesis investigates optimal sizing of components in
HPC stations at different EV penetration levels. Models from previous works, representing
the HPC station on Bornholm are extended to make them scalable and to include a
wind turbine generator (WTG) as RES. Furthermore, new energy management system
(EMS) functionalities are included, such as so-called "hot swap" enabling switching between
battery strings during EV charging. EV charging profiles are developed to represent
charging behaviour at HPC stations, based on assumptions for EV charging behaviour.
The sensitivity analyses are performed using Monte Carlo simulation for three system
setups with a vast number of EV charging scenarios. First, a base model, consisting of two
battery strings and one charger (2S1C), used for verifying the used functionalities. Second,
a setup including three strings and two chargers (3S2C) is included to show the impact of
an extra charger and string. Lastly, 3S2C setups with RES connected (either PV or WTG)
(R3S2C) are covered. These differ from the other setups since the EMS control prioritises
RES import. All solutions found adhere to defined customer satisfaction criteria.
Results from the 2S1C and 3S2C setups show that the hot swap functionality is economically
beneficial, achieving 6-27%-points higher ten year return rates. The results comparing the
2S1C and 3S2C setups show 38-70% higher return rates when designing a 2S1C system for
low EV penetration levels. However, if the EV penetration increases beyond a threshold
for EV customer queuing chance and time, the return rates for the 3S2C setup are 31-52%-
points higher. For the R3S2C setups, the RES size in the solutions achieving the highest
return rates reaches the limit of the investigated range. Therefore, it has not been possible
to determine whether PV or WTG are most economically beneficial to include. The 3S2C
setup is generally found more economically viable than the R3S2C setups with 10-40%
higher return rates. For the environmental impact considering investment cases, R3S2C
presents an acceptable solution without high economical compromises, especially for lower
EV penetrations. Lastly, different sensitivity cases for the HPC station are investigated
showing how changes in system cost estimations, the distribution of EVs, and installing
the system in different places on the distribution grid impact the solutions. Generally EV
demand is found to have the largest impact on the return rate of the system.

==PAGE:21==
1
INTRODUCTION
The increase in renewable energy sources (RES) and green actions taken by consumers
are a response to the dangerous levels of carbon dioxide released to the atmosphere [12].
This transition from "black" to "green" energy presents challenges e.g. how fast the build
out can be done for RES, changing habits and implementation of new technologies to the
power grid. This chapter will in section 1.1 briefly explain some of the key figures in the
green energy transition including the growth in energy consumption, the additions of RES
in the grid worldwide, and the role of electric vehicles (EVs). Then the role of high power
charging (HPC) stations in the EV expansion will be presented in section 1.2, where the
different charging station types will be explained. Hereafter, a brief introduction to the
H2020 INSULAE project is presented in section 1.3. Finally, this chapter includes the
thesis objective in section 1.4 and thesis outline in section 1.5.
1.1
The green energy transition
The access to electricity is an essential part of life for the majority of peoples in the
modern society, whether it being used for e.g. lighting, heating, machinery, or public
transportation systems [13]. As a result the electricity consumption and production have
increased globally. This increase is evident in the long time scale with the exponential
growth from approximately 5.600 TWh in 1800 to approximately 173.000 TWh in 2019
[14], but also in the more recent years the electricity consumption has increased, as shown
in Figure 1.1 from the Electricity Market Report from 2022 from IEA [1].

Figure 1.1. Historical and expected change in global electricity demand for the years 2015 - 2024

1
==PAGE:22==
1. Introduction
Here it is observed that there has been continuous net increase in energy demand globally
the last years, except for 2019 and 2020 due to the global Covid-19 pandemic, which
caused a decrease in demand. The predictions for the near future are also that the energy
demand will increase, however the demand growth is not predicted as high as in 2021,
where recovery from Covid-19 meant extraordinary temporary growth.
In recent years the buildout of renewable energy has been in focus. The added focus on the
green energy comes from a number of areas including energy agreements around the world,
lower costs on RES, and to make Europe independent of the gas from Russia due to the
Russian invasion of Ukraine [2]. Around the world, countries have agreed to significantly
reduce greenhouse gas emissions. Some of the targets include to at least lower the emissions
by 55% from 1990 levels as per the new 2030 European Union Emission Target [15] and
net-zero emissions by 2050 in line with the Paris Agreement on Climate Change [16]. On
May 18th 2022 the Danish, German, Belgian, and Dutch governments met in Esbjerg. Here
they pledged to expand the combined North Sea offshore wind capacity of the four countries
to 65 GW by 2030 and 150 GW by 2050 in a joint declaration, known as the Esbjerg
Offshore Wind Declaration [17]. Furthermore, the prices of RESs have been decreasing
the in the last ten years, making new investments in green energy more attractive. The
global weighted-average levelized cost of energy (LCOE) of utility-scale RES for newly
commissioned projects between 2010 and 2020 decreased by 85% for photovoltaic (PV)
generation, 56% for onshore wind, and 48% for offshore wind [18]. The political willingness
and lower prices have contributed to an increase in global renewable energy projects as
seen in Figure 1.2 from the Renewable Energy Market Update report from 2022 from IEA
[2].
IEA. All rights reserved.

Figure 1.2. Renewable net capacity additions, 2019-2021 [2]
It shows that the addition of RESs have continuously been growing, despite the pandemic-
induced construction delays, supply chain challenges, and record-high raw material and
commodity prices [2].
The green transition is strongly connected to the 2030 Sustainable Development Goals
(SDG), which were adopted by all United Nations Member States in 2015. The SDG gives a
shared plan for peace and prosperity for people and the planet, at the present moment and
2
==PAGE:23==
1. Introduction
into the future. In particular the goals related to the green transition are: (7) Affordable
and clean energy, (9) Industry innovation and infrastructure, and (13) Climate action.
But other SDG are also indirectly affected from the green transition e.g. better health
through higher air quality, economic growth, and global partnerships from international
green projects [12].
A substantial part of the green transition includes decarbonizing the transport sector, as it
is presently dominated by internal combustion engine (ICE) based vehicles. The polluting
emissions from the ICE vehicles have negative consequences on human health and the
ecosystem. The transport sector is responsible for approximately 24% of the total carbon
dioxide (CO2) that comes from combustion of fossil fuel. An option to counter this CO2
footprint from the transport sector is the inclusion of EVs. The advantages of battery EVs
are including but not limited to: zero tailpipe emissions, easy to charge the battery when
parked or at home, regenerative braking, and low cost of operation. Disadvantages include
that the driving range for a single charging is still limited for some models, and the models
are in general still more expensive to buy than similar ICE models [19, 20]. Issues are also
present regarding the mining and transport of the rare-earth materials used in the EV
batteries, since the mining for li-ion batteries materials is mainly concentrated to Australia
(41%) and Chile (34%) globally [21]. This results in a limitation to the decarbonizing of
EVs, as the materials have to be transported to the location they are used. The share
of EVs in the transportation sector is continuously increasing and by 2030 it is expected
that the sales of EVs will exceed those of ICE vehicles [22]. This thesis will cover some of
the challenges arising from including EVs into the transport sector, more specifically the
charging stations.
1.2
The role of high power charging stations in the electric
vehicle expansion
EVs are required to be charged to continue driving, similar to ICE vehicles require refuelling.
Even though the technology for EV batteries is improving, "range anxiety" is still present,
meaning that the drivers fear not having sufficient energy in the vehicle for the journey
and nowhere to charge when desired. So for the EVs market share to keep increasing,
it is required to fulfill customer desires for travel ranges and recharging times. As ICE
vehicles have been used as transportation for generations, the recharge time of EVs are
often compared to the refueling time of these [23]. Proper charging infrastructure that is
able to compete with refueling stations for ICE vehicles presents a bottleneck for widely
adopting EVs into the transport sector. A solution to counter range anxiety and fulfill
customer needs can be to develop EV fast-charging infrastructure in similar locations as
existing filling stations for ICE vehicles, especially in areas where longer driving distances
are necessary e.g. highways [19]. Furthermore, charging of EVs also presents a challenge
related to power system operation. As the charging stations are essentially relatively large
unscheduled loads in the power system, it is important to ensure that charging EVs does
not compromise the stability of the system or cause network overloading [22]. Therefore, it
is important to minimize impact on the grid where the charging stations are connected.
Different charging station types exist, satisfying different charging needs. The types can
3
==PAGE:24==
1. Introduction
be divided into four charging modes as per ICE 61851-1 [24]: Mode 1 and 2 are simple
wall plug charging methods connecting to the AC supply network with 16 or 32 Ampere,
respectively.
They represent the slowest modes of charging and are usually used for
over-night charging of EVs. Mode 3 charging is the connection of EVs through electric
vehicle supply equipment to the AC supply network. Here the limit is 63 Ampere and
this method is commonly used for public charging stations where customers can normally
charge their vehicles within a few hours. Mode 4 refers to a DC charging connection. The
power levels in mode 4 are usually capable of fully charging connected EVs in 20 to 30
minutes, depending on the charging capability and battery size of the EV. This mode is
normally noted ultra-fast charging or high-power charging. Throughout this report it will
be referred to as HPC [23].
The charging modes 1-3 are acceptable for many use cases as studies have shown that daily
travel range in 80% of the cases are less than 50 km [23]. This is well within the capabilities
of what can be charged during an over-night or parking charge. Furthermore, this charging
method also has low power requirements from the grid, if charging occurs outside peak
hours. However, for longer traveling distances or in special cases it is required to charge
mid-travel. Here the mode 4 charging method using HPC presents a viable solution to
satisfy customer needs and consequently achieve higher uptake of EVs in the transport
sector [23]. As the power demands from HPC stations can be very high, it is important to
consider solutions where the grid integration does not present problems such as overload,
increase in peak load, and voltage instability [20]. A solution to mitigate the high power
demand from the grid is to deploy a battery energy storage system (BESS) at the charging
station. This thesis will investigate sizing of the components in HPC stations, including a
BESS. This is described in more depth in section 2.1.
1.3
H2020 INSULAE
The H2020 INSULAE project investigates how decarbonizing islands can lead to cost
effective energy systems for the 15 million people in EU living on islands. The energy
systems of the islands are generally small and isolated microsystems, which compared
to the mainland results in their energy systems often being three to four times more
expensive, having two to three times higher average carbon intensity, up to ten times
higher losses, and more dependent on fossil fuel imports [25]. Therefore, the main goal of
the INSULAE project is to encourage decarbonizing the EU islands through innovative
solutions developed and demonstrated on the three Lighthouse Islands; Unije, Bornholm,
and Madeira. The aim is to use the results to validate an Investment Planning Tool that
can then be used on the Follower Islands which include Menorca, Norderney, Galante, and
complex of Chios-Inousses-Psara.
The scope of this project is to model different configurations of HPC systems including their
control, and to determine optimal system component sizing though sensitivity analyses.
The objective is to determine the best economical combination of system component
sizes for the various configurations of the HPC station taking into account location, EV
penetration levels, and RES production from PV and wind turbine generator (WTG).
4
==PAGE:25==
1. Introduction
1.4
Thesis objective
A multi-purpose BESS from Nerve Smart Systems ApS (NSS) is installed on the Danish
Island of Bornholm. The purpose of the BESS is to function as an energy buffer for HPC
of EVs. The reconfigurable BESS is connected to the local distribution grid and a RES.
The optimal sizing of the system components in the HPC station is crucial to satisfy
customer demand and avoid unnecessary costs. Here optimal sizing refers to performing
sensitivity analysis of predetermined inputs. This project will determine the optimal sizing
of the HPC station’s system components considering specific applications including EV
fast charging, solar, and wind harvesting. An existing model of the system on Bornholm
will be used and developed to fit the sizing purposes. This includes scaling of the system
components and necessary considerations for control in different configurations. Also
improvements to the control will be suggested. Basic financial aspects including initial
cost, revenue, and future predictions will be considered in the analysis. For replicability
and scalability purposes, aspects that need to be considered when installing the system
elsewhere and in other setups are then investigated. These includes penetration levels and
size distributions of EVs.
During this thesis, the following research questions will be investigated:
• What model elements are required to determine the optimal size of a battery-buffered
high power charger?
• How can the optimal system sizes of a high-power charging station with battery
storage be determined, considering the high uncertainty of electric vehicle charging?
• Which factors have the strongest influence on optimal sizing of the system
components?
• How will different system configurations influence the optimal sizing?
• How does including renewable influence the performance and profitability of the
system?
1.5
Thesis outline
This thesis is arranged in eight chapters based on the above-mentioned objectives. First, the
theoretical background is presented, covering the required knowledge about the charging
stations, battery, scaling methods, and the reference system on Bornholm. This is followed
by how the model is developed to fit the objective of sizing the system components of in
the HPC station by sensitivity analysis. Here the changes made to the original model are
presented and discussed. The different configurations that are desired to be investigated
are then described and the changes required to the initial model developed are presented.
Hereafter, the Monte Carlo simulation approach is introduced and how it is being applied
in this thesis is explained. The results of Monte Carlo simulation for the model developed
in the different configurations are then summed up and discussed, including a thorough
description of the design criteria. Finally, the results of this thesis are conveyed in a
conclusion. An overview of the chapters is shown below:
• Chapter 2 starts with the theoretical background necessary to understand this
thesis’ investigation. This includes an introduction to EV charging stations and the

2
THEORETICAL BACKGROUND
This chapter will explain the theoretical background conducted in this thesis, which will
give a thorough understanding of the different important aspects considered. This includes
theoretical background knowledge for the technical solutions, considerations for ways to
conduct the investigations, and an explanation of the physical system investigated, which
is located on Bornholm.
First, a description of charging station systems is presented in section 2.1. This includes the
explanation of different technical solutions and their advantages and disadvantages. This
section also covers an investigation of available EV charging data, to determine if relevant
data can be found. Hereafter, the battery degradation is explained from a literature review
in section 2.2. This is presented to establish which factors influence degradation of the
BESS. This is followed by the considerations put into the sizing investigation method
used in this thesis, which is covered in section 2.3. This compares the used sensitivity
analysis method with the mathematical optimization method and explains why this has
been chosen. Lastly, the physical setup on Bornholm from which the investigations are
made and the technology used is covered in section 2.4.
2.1
Charging station systems
Charging stations give the EV users the possibility of charging their vehicles to continue
driving. A charging station is a physical construct consisting of a single or multiple
charging points. The electric energy required to fill the battery of the EV is delivered
through the charging point, either from conductive- or inductive charging technology.
Conductive charging technology is well developed and used, whereas inductive charging
technology is mostly still on research level. Therefore, this thesis will only consider the
conductive charging technology. Charging with this method is both efficient and reliable.
For conductive charging there are on-board and off-board infrastructures. The on-board
chargers are located internally on the EVs and are subject to limitations in cost, space,
weight an size. They are therefore generally used for relatively slow charging sessions
(Mode 1-3), as explained section 1.2. The off-board chargers are located at e.g. parking
lots, highways etc. and are therefore not limited as much to these constraints. Therefore,
these can be used for HPC, as explained in section 1.2. How much power can be delivered is
depending on the cables and charger of the charging station, and on the charging capability
of the battery of the EV connected [19].
This section will dive into the some of the different topologies and technologies for HPC
stations focusing on DC link configurations. First, the difference of charging systems with
and without BESS will be highlighted, showing the potential benefits of including a BESS
to the charging station. Then the battery technology used by Nerve Smart System ApS
7
==PAGE:28==
2. Theoretical Background
(NSS) will be briefly explained and compared to the conventional BESS used for charging
stations. Hereafter, the potential benefits of including a RES to the charging station will
be explained. Lastly, an investigation of available EV charging data will be presented.
2.1.1
With or without BESS
Different topologies exist for charging stations, each having their own advantages and
disadvantages. This section will cover the difference between either including or excluding
a BESS to the HPC station. How the topology of a conventional charging station looks
like with or without the BESS is shown in Figure 2.1.
~

3ph AC 
grid
Battery energy 
storage system
EV high power charger
DC/DC
DC/DC
DC bus
EV high power charger
DC/DC
DC/DC
RES: PV or WTG
Figure 2.1. System topology HPC DC station with and without BESS
The HPC station consists of a grid connection through the AC/DC grid converter, a DC
bus, a BESS, HPC points for EV charging, and DC/DC converters for the BESS and the
chargers. The BESS and RES are marked with blue and green to indicate that these are
optional. To install the BESS in a conventional system also requires a DC/DC converter
to control the power flow in and out of the battery. The topology shown in Figure 2.1 is
modular, since the DC bus allows the several subsystems to be incorporated. This can be
additional chargers, BESS or even RES as explained in section 2.1.3. Thus this topology is
used in different configurations of EV chargers [20].
Assuming that the charging station is unidirectional, meaning that there is no vehicle to
grid power flow, the power flow will be from the AC grid to the BESS or the EV HPC.
When power is flowing from the BESS to the EV HPC point, the BESS can assist in
adding the difference between the required charging power from the EV and the delivered
power from the grid in cases of high power requests from EV. The BESS is charged in the
periods where no EV charging sessions are active. Since the power request hereby can be
reduced from the grid, the connection can be reduced in configurations including BESS.
How the charging stations, especially HPC stations are interacting with the grid is important
since they can have negative impact on various parts of the power grid. Some of these
impacts are briefly presented here. Since many distribution systems are working on the
verge of instability excluding EV loads [19], grid stability can be affected due to the
8
==PAGE:29==
2. Theoretical Background
high power demand of the EV chargers. The EV charging stations can also influence the
supply-demand balance of the grid, as the EVs connecting to the stations are uncontrolled
loads that can connect at any time of the day. When considering the HPC stations, the
high magnitude of the loads can therefore have severe impact if not dealt with accordingly.
Furthermore, the grid assets such as transformers, transmissions lines, and switchgear
protective devices can suffer decreased lifetime, due to the added thermal loading on these
devices from the higher power flow. This higher power flow, resulting in higher RMS
value of the current drawn also add additional grid losses. Lastly, the grid voltage can be
affected by the charging stations, potentially causing voltage deviations, depending on the
EV charging penetration levels [19].
As the HPC stations present a viable option to mitigate the problems related to EV
chargings, as explained in section 1.2, their impact on the grid need to be considered.
Integrating BESS to the HPC stations present a solution for the power system operators,
where the uncontrolled peak demands can be significantly reduced. Adding a BESS to the
HPC station also improves the potential use of RESs, since the intermittent power of the
RES can be stored [20, 23]. Other configurations also exist that present even lower impact.
An example of such a configuration and a different battery technology solution will be
described in the next section.
2.1.2
Nerve Smart Systems BESS vs. conventional BESS
Different technologies and topologies can be used for HPC stations. This section will cover
the NSS technology and a topology that can be beneficial for reducing the grid impact of
the HPC stations.
The NSS technology enables dynamic voltage control of the DC-link by actively engaging
the desired number of battery cells in the BESS. This effectively eliminates the need for
some of the converters in the HPC stations. Further explanation of the NSS technology
can be found in section 2.4.1 and in Ref. [3, 4, 8, 26]. The conventional HPC topology
and the HPC topology based on NSS technology are shown in Figure 2.1 and Figure 2.2
respectively.
It is seen that the DC/DC converters in the conventional system, which are required for
the components attached to the DC-bus including BESS and EV charger, are excluded due
to the NSS technology enabling dynamic DC-link voltage control of the BESS. The the
battery strings of the NSS controlled BESS can connect to one individual component each
at a time. This means that if there are four connections, two EV chargers, RES, and grid,
as in Figure 2.2, the EMS needs to prioritize which elements to connect to. For instance,
if two EVs arrive they will most likely occupy two BESS strings, whereas whether the
remaining string should connect to the RES or grid is a predefined decision making of the
EMS.
The NSS topology in Figure 2.2 also presents an option to reduce the negative impacts of
the HPC stations on the power grid compared to the conventional topology in Figure 2.1.
The impacts are reduced as load from the EVs are decoupled from the grid. The BESS
is working as a power buffer interposed between the grid and the charging EVs, meaning
that the power rating of the grid converter can be reduced [20]. The lower demand also
9
==PAGE:30==
2. Theoretical Background

baery

Baery energy storage system

EV high power charger

RES: PV or WTG
Figure 2.2. System topology HPC DC charging station with Nerve Smart Systems ApS technology
enables the possibility of connecting the HPC station to the low-voltage distribution grid.
On the other hand, it is also necessary to size the battery to be able to provide the full
power and energy of the charging sessions, as it does not only have a supporting function
anymore. The exclusion of costly DC/DC converters made possible by the NSS technology
is beneficial in terms of financial aspects as well as associated losses [23]. The topology
with the BESS functioning as a power buffer interposed between the grid and the charging
EVs with the NSS technology will be investigated in this thesis.
2.1.3
Hybrid systems (Including RES)
Since the HPC stations based on DC-link topologies are modular as described in section
2.1.1, it is also possible to add RES in the NSS topology resulting in a hybrid system. This
section will describe some of the advantages and challenges of such hybrid system.
The RES input to the HPC station serves as an additional way to import green energy
to the BESS excluding expenses of importing electricity from the grid. It can in some
occasions also help charging the BESS faster than systems without RES, depending on
the EMS control. The topology of a hybrid system based on the Nerve Switch technology
and with the BESS functioning as a power buffer interposed between the grid and the
charging EVs is shown in Figure 2.2. This shows that the RES consisting of either PV
or WTG is connected in the same manner to the busbar system as the EV HPC points.
Therefore, the use of RES in this topology also depends on the amount of available battery
strings in the system. Since some of the energy required for charging EVs can be drawn
from the RES, it can potentially lower the power drawn from the grid. This may result
in the lower rated power for the grid inverter of the system and making the installation
more environmentally friendly at the same time [20]. Since the RES power from both PV
and WTG are fluctuating depending on the natural phenomena of solar and wind, the
intermittency in the provided energy may present some challenges to the system. These
impacts will be investigated in this thesis in section 6.2.
10
==PAGE:31==
2. Theoretical Background
Another contributor to why it is interesting to investigate implementing RES in the HPC
stations is the decreasing prices on RESs. As mentioned in section 1.1, the global LCOE of
utility-scale RES for newly commissioned projects between 2010 and 2020 fell by 85% for
PV, 56% for onshore wind, and 48% for offshore wind [18]. This tendency is expected to
continue both for installation and operation, meaning that the potential financial benefit
of implementing RES to the HPC stations increases [27]. This will also be investigated in
this thesis by looking at 2030 predictions for costs of PV and WTG to give an indication
of future plausible benefits in section 6.4.1.
2.1.4
Available electrical vehicle charging data
Since charging EVs is the objective of the HPC station, it is important to consider the
charging profiles of the EVs. Even though the popularity of EVs in the transport sector
is growing, it is still difficult to find the required data to generate satisfactory charging
profiles, especially for the newest HPC technology. This is because HPC is a new technology
that has not been implemented in many places yet, and also due to the charging data can
have financial value for companies. Hence, EV charging data is often kept secret by the
HPC manufactures.
This thesis does not include E-buses and trucks as their charging behaviour is considered
to vary from that of the passenger EV drivers use of HPC stations. They will likely charge
at dedicated power charging stations when idle. Therefore, these are not part of the
required data. Furthermore, the plug-in/out times of the EVs are considered identical
to arrival/departure time. In real life this assumption is not correct, as it would mean
that the EVs charge from the second they arrive to the charging station, until the second
they leave again. However, for the charging profiles required for this thesis the focus is on
charging energy over a period of time, so it does not alter the actual energy required by
the arriving EVs.
This section will attempt to cover if any published EV charging data fits the scope of
this thesis. Here it is important to note that this thesis will focus on HPC capable of
charging with 300 kW. This large charging capability will likely have an influence of how
the charging station will be used. Therefore, potential useful data will have to include
HPCs in this range to represent this specific charging behaviour.
Ref. [22] from 2021 provides a comprehensive collection of 50 unique data sources for EV
integration studies. In the review paper the data sources are categorized as either surveys,
ICE/EV trials or charger trails. The data sources fall into following availability categories.
• Publicly available, where the data is accessible without any action required
• Available upon request
• Not publicly available
• Conditionally available, where research cooperation may enable sharing of the data
• Unknown
These availability categories highlight the trend that information on charging profiles often
is kept secret by the HPC manufactures due to their financial value. This of course limits
the amount accessible data.
11
==PAGE:32==
2. Theoretical Background
It is also important to underline that correlation is important. For instance, energy,
power, and duration need to arise from same data, since they are the product of charging
sessions. Otherwise, it will display unreal tendencies. This means that one cannot mix these
parameters from different data sources. Since the arrival times are not directly influencing
the power profile of the charging session, these can be determined independently from the
energy, power, and duration if necessary.
The type of data sources usable for this thesis’ investigation needs to be determined;
surveys, ICE/EV trials or charger trails. The three different types are here briefly explained
and usability is discussed. To conduct surveys, an interaction between interviewers and
participants is needed. This can be either written or oral. The benefits of such studies
are that specific questions can be answered. Therefore, such information can be good to
determine e.g. driving behaviour and obtain qualitative behaviour in general. The main
drawback for the scope of this thesis is that the information is not actual data from either
EV or charging station. Therefore, the charging behaviour from surveys would not give a
precise objective picture. Hence, the surveys will not be used.
For ICE and EV trials, the data collection is gathered by the controller area network
(CAN)-bus of the vehicle. This type of data is good to determine driving patterns and
vehicle information including start/end time, location, and travel distance. In some cases
it also provides additional information regarding EV location and technical parameters as
battery temperature, voltage, current, temperature, and auxiliary power usage. As this
information is on the EV and not on the charger, they are not relevant since charger is
the scope of this thesis. Normally, tens to hundreds EV participate in the trials, so the
amount of data is scarce to create distributions from. Hence, this type of data is not ideal
for this study either.
The last type of data sources is the charger trials, in which the data are collected using the
metering devices of the chargers. This means that data regarding plug-in/plug-out time
and charging power can be extracted. From the power and the charging duration (plug-out
time minus plug-in time), the charged energy can then be derived. This kind of data
are useful for countries that are preparing infrastructure for transportation electrification,
to understand charging needs of the users. The participants in such trials are tens to
thousands of public chargers, which generally gives a comprehensive data set to create
probability distributions from. Therefore, the charger trials are the most ideal data type
for this thesis objective of sizing the system components of a HPC station. One of the
issues with both ICE/EV and charger trial data is that these are often gathered for specific
projects and often not accessible to the public [22].
The last thing to notice for defining the usability of the data is the age of the data and
the values included. Some of the available data set are more than ten years old. The
development in both the charging stations’ charging capabilities and the EVs’ battery size
and charging capabilities means that the data sources are only relevant for a short period.
New data from today will most likely be significantly different from that of users driving
in five to tens years from now. The variables included in an ideal profile are defined in
Ref. [22] as battery capacity, charging power, plug-in state of charge, plug-in/out time, and
charged energy. In this thesis the values for the charging profiles required are as minimum
arrival- and departure time and two out of the three of the power related parameters:
12
==PAGE:33==
2. Theoretical Background
charging power, energy charged, and charging duration. Therefore, only data less than five
years old that contain these minimum requirements for variables will be considered.
Summing up all the limitations presented in this section for data sources only one out of
the fifty studies is potentially relevant. This is a German study from 2019 to 2020 in Ref.
[28] based on public available data [29]. This data set contains 26.951 public charging
points. However, only eleven of these have charging power of P > 100 kW included and
the most frequently occurring rating above 100 kW is P = 175 kW. This data source is not
considered useful because of the low amount of connection points with P > 100 kW and
that the rating is most frequently at P = 175 kW, which is only approximately half of the
300 kW desired in this thesis.
More work is required to determine the charging profiles, since none of the data sources
found in Ref. [22] have been proved to be well suited to represent the EV charging profiles
for this thesis’ investigation. Therefore, more recent EV charging data will be presented
and discussed from two different data sources, the NSS system installed in Bornholm
and charging trial data from charging stations in the Netherlands in sections 3.2 and 3.3,
respectively. Also an alternative approach to define charging profiles based on assumptions
will be presented and discussed in section 3.3.1.2, inspired by Ref. [30]. In these sections
the best suited EV charging profile distributions will be chosen for the future investigations
in this thesis.
2.2
Battery degradation
The heart of the investigated HPC station are the BESS and EMS of the BESS. To justify
the control schemes of the EMS, the battery degradation metrics need to be investigated,
which will be carried out in this section through a literature review.
The battery chemistry utilized in the investigated BESS developed by NSS, is lithium
iron phosphate (LFP). Therefore the focus will solely be on the LFP chemistry. From the
literature the two main operating factors influencing the lifetime are calendar aging and
cycles [31, 32, 33, 34, 35].
In Ref. [31] a literature review on calendar aging degradation factors for different chemistry
were conducted.
The main factors influencing the calendar aging were found to be
temperature and SOC. Among the investigated chemistry, the LFP chemistry was found
to be least affected by temperature and state of charge (SOC). A clear tendency shows
that increasing temperature and SOC will increase the degradation, with an optimal
operating temperature around 25 ◦C. The SOC degradation contribution approximately
followed a power regression curve with a power coefficient less than one. Furthermore,
the temperature was found to have more impact on calendar aging compared to SOC.
This tendency also appeared in Ref. [32, 33, 35]. Among the found literature investigating
calendar aging, the only SOC dependent experiment lasting for more than twelve months
was Ref. [32], which lasted for 48 months. Here the results showed a tendency of largest
degradation for 50% SOC and lower degradation for both higher and lower SOC values.
The variations in some of these results were fairly large and it was the only experiment
found showing this tendency.
13
==PAGE:34==
2. Theoretical Background
In Ref. [32] and [33] the capacity fade for cylindrical LFP cells after 9 months of storage at
50% SOC and at 40 ◦C were found to be 6% and 8%, respectively. In Ref. [35] the capacity
fade was 5.5%, but at 45 ◦C, 50% SOC and 8 months of storage. The difference in results
might come from the fact that batteries at different capacities were investigated and that
the tests were not carried out in the exact same way.
Cycle degradation experiments of LFP cells were carried out in Ref. [34] and [35]. The
temperature dependency through 1C charge and discharge cycles of LFP cells were
conducted in Ref. [34]. From these measurements a significant increase in cycle degradation
appeared at temperatures towards −18 ◦C and 40 ◦C with lowest degradation at 25 ◦C.
The tendency of optimal operating cycling temperature also appeared in Ref. [35].
A C-rate dependency test was also conducted in Ref. [34], which in combination with
increasing temperatures during higher C-rate cycles of 5C, 10C, and 15C showed excessive
degradation of the cells.
Furthermore at high C-ratings, the results showed a faster
degradation during charging compared to discharging. The depth of discharge (DOD)-cycle
behavior of the LFP chemistry was also investigated in Ref. [34], where an optimal DOD
was found around 40 %. This behavior was considering equivalent full charging cycles. In
Ref. [34] and [35] the capacity fade through 1C cycles at 20-25 ◦C was found to be 8.5% at
2800 cycles and 20% at 2900 cycles, respectively.
Summing up:
As the exact degradation values found in the literature varies, a numerical degradation
dependency will not be used to predict the lifetime of the BESS. Instead the SOC, current
and cycles will be monitored to make an indication of the battery degradation in different
simulation case studies. From the literature review presented in this section the following
assumptions will be considered throughout the EMS development for a BESS containing
LFP cells.
• Cells should be kept at approx 20-30 ◦C to limit the calendar and cycle degradation.
• Higher SOC values accelerate the calendar degradation.
• The higher the current rate, the higher the impact on the cycle degradation of the
BESS.
• The BESS degrades faster when charging compared to discharging at same current
rate, affecting the cycle degradation.
2.3
Sensitivity analysis versus mathematical optimization
The key aspect of optimization is simply optimal thinking and it is therefore present in
the industry as well as in the daily life. Taking the number of constraints into account,
optimization aims to achieve the best outcome of a particular objective. It allows to
make sensible choices for a decision maker based on an objective foundation, formally
known as optimization problems [36]. The optimization problems can range from simple
cases of how a person chooses to spend money on different groceries to very complex
problems with a vast amount of variables. The optimization problem of this thesis is
how to size the components of the HPC station to fulfill success criteria as defined in
section 6.1.3 without over-sizing the system and thereby adding unnecessary expenses. As
14
==PAGE:35==
2. Theoretical Background
most optimization problems, the one in this thesis contains three main elements: The first
element is the decision variables which are the decisions that are optimized in the model.
For the HPC model, these include the parameters BESS size, the inverter size, number of
chargers, number of battery strings, EV penetration levels, and potentially the RES size
in cases where such is included. The second element is the objective function, which is a
numerical measurement for how well the system is performing with the decisions chosen.
For the HPC model, this can for example be to maximize the economical return rate, the
system efficiency, or the self-sufficiency of the system. The last element is the constraints.
These are the limitations of the decision variables. These limitations are also necessary
in order to model a realistic case, taking into account interaction between variables and
their reasonable sizes. For the HPC model constraints relate to the intervals that will be
investigated for the decision variables [36].
In this section two potential ways of optimizing the size of the HPC components will be
presented: sensitivity analysis with parameter sweep and mathematical optimization. Both
solution methods will be shortly explained and their pros and cons for this thesis objective
will be pointed out. Finally, the solutions methods will be compared and the best suited
solution method will be chosen for the future investigations.
Sensitivity analysis
Sensitivity analysis based on simulations uses a fixed relation between input and output
through differential equations. One set of input data is given to the model and associated
results are obtained. It means that in the case of having a model already present, it is
possible to acquire knowledge of how this solution depends on data, assumptions, and
combinations of the system component sizing. Sensitivity analysis allows to estimate
the effects of changes in the parameter values to optimize the objective function [37].
Furthermore, it is also an effective way to compare different scenarios to each other where
one parameter in the model changes.
For sensitivity analysis it is important to define which parameters should be changed and
the resolution of these parameters. It is then possible to explore how sensitive the objective
function of the model is to these parameters. Furthermore, if enough combinations and
a large enough interval is swept for the various parameters, it enables the possibility of
finding the local optimum for the desired result. This simplicity of the sensitivity analysis
as a tool to finding the optimal solution is both an advantage and disadvantage of this
solution method. The advantage is that it is usable for investigations with a limited number
of parameters with limited interval sizes. For these cases it is a strong tool that allows
to define e.g. assumptions, resolutions of parameters swept, their respective impact on
the system, check solutions, and replicate results. Additionally, it is a solution method
where it is possible to debug the model without too many difficulties as everything is
well-defined. Lastly, it is a broadly used method that reveals how outcomes respond to
parameter changes [38]. The disadvantage of the sensitivity analysis is that for some cases
it becomes excessively compute heavy. Varying one parameter at a time, while holding
the others fixed means that all possible combinations of these parameters will have to
be investigated, resulting in large amount of nested values. In large models with a vast
amount of decision variables with large constraints, this is not a beneficial solution method.
15
==PAGE:36==
2. Theoretical Background
For this thesis’ objective of optimally sizing the HPC system components in different
configurations, there are relatively few parameters. Therefore, the sensitivity analysis can
serve as a valid solution method and it is possible to keep an overview of the parameters
at the same time.
Mathematical optimization
Mathematical optimization quantifies the process of optimal thinking.
Thereby, the
decision maker will make decisions based on an objective numerical metric.
Taking
the three elements into account; the decision variables, the objective function, and the
constraints, most optimization problems can be defined in a generic form:
max
x1,...,xn f(x1, ..., xn)
(2.1)
s.t.(x1, ..., xn) ∈A
(2.2)
where in Equation 2.1 the objective is to maximize the objective function f(x1, ..., xn) for
n decision variables. This could for example be the maximum return rate for the HPC
station depending on the sizes of the system components. The "subject to" part of the
formulation in Equation 2.2 means that the decision variables chosen must satisfy the
constraints within the feasible region A. The optimal solutions satisfy the constraints to
maximize the objective function and they are typically denoted
(x∗
1, ..., x∗
n).
(2.3)
How values are found in Equation 2.3 for Equation 2.1 is basically the foundation for
the generic optimization problem [36]. Various optimization methods exist for different
optimization problems, including both classic and heuristic optimization methods. They
aim to find solutions to the decision variables in a number of different ways. One of the
advantages of using mathematical optimization is that solutions can be found for complex
objectives with many decision variables. The drawbacks of mathematical optimization can
include that the parameter tuning is challenging as they are usually problem dependent.
Furthermore, the iterative solution methods may present difficulties reaching similar results
if it is desired to validate the findings. Lastly it can be difficult to determine a stopping
criteria for the iterative problem as the optimal solution is unknown [39].
Choosing a solution method
From the solution methods presented, sensitivity analysis parameter sweep is chosen for this
thesis’ objective of optimal sizing the HPC system components in different configurations.
It is considered feasible to use for the investigation as only a few parameters will be swept,
meaning that the amount of nested values for decision variables will be limited. The
sensitivity analysis presents a simple solution method with clearly defined ranges and
assumptions for the parameters investigated. Furthermore, the results presented from
the simulations can be processed and displayed via Monte Carlo simulations including
success criteria, as explained in chapter 5. Therefore, it represents a good solution method
for the scope of this thesis. As presented, other methods also exist that would optimize
certain decision variables towards the objective function of a mathematical model subject to
16
==PAGE:37==
2. Theoretical Background
constraints that define the solution space. But the added complexity and added assumptions
in the uncertainties associated with such methods are undesirable for the scope of this
thesis.
2.4
Reference battery energy storage system
The BESS investigated in this thesis refers to a physical system developed by NSS as a part
of the INSULAE project and is physically installed in Bornholm. This system is mentioned
since some of the models used in this thesis build on models developed for the system
installed in Bornholm [3, 4]. The models used in this thesis contain updates covered in
section 3.4. This section will therefore give an introduction to the Nerve Switch technology
developed by NSS and the Bornholm setup.
2.4.1
Nerve Smart Systems technology
NSS develops BESS for various applications [26]. These BESSs are controlled by their
developed battery management system (BMS) called the Nerve Switch. The Nerve Switch
enables dynamic reconfiguration of battery cells within a BESS [40]. This provides active
control of the DC voltage which can limit the need of DC/DC power equipment for
various DC applications, such as PV energy harvesting and EV DC charging. The DC
voltage granularity of the NSS BESS depends on the battery cell voltage and system
impedance. This project assumes the DC voltage granularity of the NSS BESS to be
sufficient for the used applications. The Nerve Switch technology is explained more in
detail in Ref. [3, 4, 8, 26].
2.4.2
Bornholm System
The setup on Bornholm is a mobile container consisting of a NSS BESS of 311 kWh, two
175 kW EV chargers, and two 33 kW inverters [3] as shown in Figure 2.3.
Figure 2.3. Picture of the setup on Bornholm
The BESS is connected to a 60 kW PV system and a grid connection of 43 kW (phase
current of 63 A) [3]. The NSS BESS contains three dual strings that each consist of two
sub strings. The two sub strings can be connected in either series or parallel. Each sub
string consists of six reconfigurable battery modules. These battery modules are controlled
by the Nerve Switch. Each module has twenty-seven cells. The cells used in the Bornholm
17
==PAGE:38==
2. Theoretical Background
system are 100 Ah prismatic LFP cells from Sinopoly [3, 4, 8]. If "high voltage" applications
are connected, e.g. a 800 V EV, PV array, or grid inverter, then the dual string connects
the two sub strings in series. If "low voltage" applications are connected, e.g. a 400 V EV,
then the system changes to two parallel sub strings. The actual system is currently located
at Campus Bornholm in Rønne. More detailed information about the Bornholm system
can be found in Ref. [3].
2.5
Chapter summary
This chapter has explained the theoretical background of the work that is investigated in
this thesis. First, charging station systems have been presented in-depth including the
explanation of pros and cons of different technical solutions. This also determined that
no satisfactory EV charging data set is available at present time, since these data are
financially important for companies to keep secret and the HPC technology is still new.
Then the battery degradation was explained from a literature review, establishing which
factors have an influence on the degradation of the BESS in the charging stations. Here
it was also concluded that the battery degradation parameters will only be monitored in
this thesis’ investigations. Hereafter, possible investigation methods that could be used
in this thesis were explained, comparing the used sensitivity analysis method with the
mathematical optimization method. It was here concluded why the sensitivity analysis
has been chosen. Lastly, the physical setup on which the investigations are made and the
technology used were explained.
18
==PAGE:39==
3
MODEL DEVELOPMENT
To give a thorough understanding of the model developed for this thesis, this chapter
presents the different considerations for this extension. First, an overall description of the
model built in previous works in Ref. [3, 4, 8] is presented in section 3.1. This is followed
by an in-depth explanation of the considerations for creating the EV charging profiles used
for the investigations in section 3.2 and 3.3. Lastly, this chapter shows what is upgraded
from the previous models [3, 4, 8]. These aspects include parts of the model that have
been changed to enable the sizing of the BESS by sensitivity analysis, additional EMS
functionalities, as well as economic considerations.
3.1
Description of the original model
The original simulation model represents the container system installed on Bornholm as
described in section 2.4. The system consists of two EV chargers, a PV array, a grid
inverter, and three dual BESS strings. A block diagram of the model build in Matlab
Simulink in previous works from Ref. [3] is presented in Figure 3.1.
Figure 3.1. Conceptual block diagram of original system model [3]
The model includes the physical elements of the system as well as controls for energy
and power exchange. Also the auxiliary power consumption and the thermal dynamics
are included in the model. It is worth noting that implementation of the dual string
configuration is not needed in the model, since the simulation is only focusing on power
19
==PAGE:40==
3. Model Development
and energy levels. Therefore, only an efficiency curve for 800 V has been implemented.
This efficiency curve is the same if 400 V is demanded as the dual strings then would be
parallel connected. All applications connected to the system are assumed to be either
800 V or 400 V.
The EMS controls the inverter power setpoint depending on PV and EV forecast, which
in the end ensures correct connection between the three dual BESS strings and the EV
chargers, PV, and inverter [3, 4]. The inverter power setpoint is controlled through a
droop controller. In this EMS control it is not possible to swap between strings while
charging EVs, meaning that if the battery string connected to a charging EV depletes, the
charging session is interrupted. The model includes a dynamic thermal model considering
the physical dimensions of the container system, where the auxiliary power is depending
on temperature thresholds in the system. More detailed information about the original
simulation model can be found in Ref. [3, 4, 8]. The required aspects of the model have
been updated in this thesis to size the system, which will be explained and reasoned in
section 3.4.
3.2
Charging data analysis for Bornholm
As charging EVs is the objective of the HPC station, charging profiles for EVs is a necessary
input for this thesis’ sizing investigation. Therefore, it is important to consider the quality
of the data fed to the model. This section will present the charging data from the NSS
system on Bornholm to determine if this data can be used as an input to the model. The
data are available as part of the INSULAE project. The data are extracted from the actual
NSS BESS with two chargers and three battery strings and show the charging sessions of
this test system. The system is located on the relatively small island of Bornholm. This
means that the charging sessions will reflect the charging patterns of this location, which
are likely to be different from the ones expected at locations where the HPC station would
be more frequently used e.g. along highways and densely populated areas. Furthermore,
charging at the station is currently free, which is likely impact the charging behavior of
the EV users. It is also worth noticing that the system has only been operating from June
2021 and the data obtained are from the period June 7th, 2021 to March 3rd, 2022. This
results in only 416 sessions for arrival time, charged energy, and charging duration. The
raw data include observations with extremely low energy demand. These observations
have been removed, as they are assumed to be results of unsuccessful attempts of charging
using the system. Therefore, all observations less than 2 kWh have been removed as seen in
Figure 3.2. The 2 kWh threshold is used as it is equivalent to approximately 10 km driving
range [41]. The data exclusion is done based on the assumption that EV users would not
use a charging station, especially a HPC station to charge less than 10 km driving range
for their vehicle. Therefore, these sessions are considered unsuccessful attempts of charging
using the system.
Cleaning up the data results in 257 usable observations, which is too low for determining
representative probability distributions. However, to clarify if any trends might still be
present, probability distributions for arrival time, charged energy, and charging duration
in Figure 3.3 are constructed based on the provided Matlab scripts. Those scripts were
already used in Ref. [4] for processing EV profile data set from the Netherlands [42, 43].

21
==PAGE:42==
3. Model Development
From the blue graphs in Figure 3.3 there are no distinct tendencies for neither charging
duration nor charged energy. The distribution for the arrival time does not show any clear
trends either, only that the charging sessions are mainly initiated in the daytime. The
probability distributions with 20 bins are made to see if a tendency could be found if the
data is coarsely divided into intervals and fitted. It can be observed that some tendencies
are present. The arrival times are during the day hours with the highest probability around
14.00-15.00. The charging duration probability for the Bornholm system is highest around
10 min and the charged energy distribution has the highest probability at 5 kWh decreasing
until around 50 kWh.
Overall, the distribution from Bornholm still shows signs of low amount of data, with
the fluctuations throughout the distributions, even after dividing the data into 20 bins.
Based on the location, small amount of usable data resulting in unclear trends, and free
EV charging, the Bornholm data are not considered sufficient for this investigation.
3.3
Electric vehicle charging profiles
This section will describe the considerations behind the creation of the EV profiles that
are fed into the simulation. As these profiles are the energy demand from the HPC station,
they are important to define and consider what they represent. Section 3.2 showed that the
data from the Bornholm system were not satisfactory, so alternative EV charging profiles
will be considered. Firstly, two EV profiles based on different locations and with different
energy demands and charging duration will be described in section 3.3.1. The first profile
is based on data acquired from the Netherlands [42, 43], while the second one is based on
traffic flow from England with EV charging profiles created from assumptions for arrival
time, energy demand and charging duration. Then the creation of the profiles will be
described in section 3.3.2, including the important assumptions that these are based on.
Lastly, an example of a randomly generated EV profile will be presented and discussed.
To strive for good quality profiles, the steps presented aim to comply with the guidelines
and limitations for data usage in EV studies, as presented in Ref. [22].
3.3.1
EV probability distributions
This section will describe the creation of arrival time, energy demand, and charging duration
probability distributions. This will be done first for data acquired from the Netherlands
and later for an approach based on assumptions for traffic, EVs etc.
3.3.1.1
Charging data for EVs from the Netherlands
The EV charging data from the Netherlands are based on a fast charge definition of charging
at any station that provides at least 43 kW of power. The data have been extracted from
the EVs, so the charging capacity is defined from the charging capabilities of the vehicles,
not by the power the chargers can deliver. The data have been measured at the vehicle,
thereby excluding losses in charging stations. In Ref. [43] the average charging power was
41 kW, however the highest charging power during a session recorded was 148 kW. This
implies relative small fast charging stations, but the data set likely also includes chargers
capable of 150 or 175 kW [43].
22
==PAGE:43==
3. Model Development
The comprehensive data set is based on 1 million charging session in the Netherlands in
2019. This results in probability distributions for arrival time, charging duration, and
energy charged, respectively as shown in Figure 3.4.
Figure 3.4. Probability distributions based on Netherlands EV data for a) arrival time, b) charging
duration, and c) charged energy [4]
The distribution of charging sessions for fast chargers over the day is mainly around the
center of the day, namely from 08:00 AM to 08:00 PM. The energy demand has the majority
of the distribution below 20 kWh and the charging duration is mainly distributed below 30
minutes [43].
All in all, the data set from the Netherlands is an empirical analysis that shows actual
charging behaviour based on large data quantities. The data represent real behaviour
in the given locations. However, they also include a variation of charging powers under
the term fast charging. This range includes charging powers for AC larger than or equal
to 43 kW and DC charging powers of at least 50 kW. It is important that definitively no
charging stations above 175 kW are listed in the data set [43]. For the scope of this project,
this may represent a charging behaviour that does not correspond to what a HPC station,
capable of charging EVs with up to 300 kW, would experience. The arrival time of EVs
are relatively evenly distributed throughout the middle of the day. This behaviour might
also be different for a HPC station, where a high power would result in a significantly
shorter charging duration if the same energy is required. This could potentially result in
different charging patterns from the consumer, which would be able to use the charging
stations more like a filling station for ICE vehicles. Therefore, both pros and cons are
present for the data from the Netherlands in regards to this study. To address this, the
probability curves from this data for arrival time, charging duration, and energy demand
in Figure 3.4 will be compared to other ways of estimating the EV profiles, before the best
suited solution is used for further investigations.
23
==PAGE:44==
3. Model Development
3.3.1.2
Charging profiles for EVs based on highway usage and assumed EV
charging distributions
At the present time it is concluded that there are not enough available data for HPC
stations capable of charging EVs with up to 300 kW to do an empirical analysis. This is
based on extensive research presented in sections 2.1.4, 3.2, and 3.3. Also attempts have
been made to acquire data from HPC manufacturers1.
Therefore, another approach is suggested to estimate the charging distributions for such
chargers. The ability to explicitly determine a specific scenario for investigation is prioritised
in these estimations for clarity. This has been assumed of higher importance than actual
data acquisition, since this was not possible for HPC stations. Therefore, this section
will also go further in depth explaining the process of creating such distributions. It is
based loosely on the same approach as in Ref. [30] where the distributions are divided into
sub-elements. These sub-elements are battery capacity, charging power, plug-in/out SOC,
plug-in/out time, and charged energy [22].
Arrival time probability distribution
For this project it is assumed that the HPC stations will be used similar to the filling
stations for ICE vehicles, as the charging power is large, resulting in relatively fast charging.
Therefore, it is a beneficial option for charging the EV mid journey. From this, arrival time
for EVs at the HPC stations is assumed to be proportional to probability distribution of
cars driving by. As an example if one hundred cars drive by at 10.00 and twenty cars drive
by at 12.00, the HPC station visits will be five times more likely at 10.00. This assumption
goes well in hand with many EV users are early adopters, so their driving and charging
patterns may not have changed significantly yet. The impact of this assumption should be
carefully studied in the future, as EV market penetration likely increases [22].
Traffic flow data from the government-owned company "National Highways" in England
has been used to make a distribution of the arrival times for the HPC station [44]. Data
from a full year are processed to define clear tendencies. Thereby not looking at different
seasons nor individual days in the week like different behavior e.g. Tuesday compared
to Friday. The traffic flow is chosen for vehicles passing between the junctions J16 and
J17 on the A1(M) motorway between the large city Leeds and the capital London. This
location is chosen to represent a potential point of interest to install a HPC station, since
the A1 is a trafficked road in England. The data have been prepared for both southbound
and northbound directions, to include the potential difference between traffic to or from
London. The total carriageway flow as a function of the time of day in southbound
direction2 is shown in Figure 3.5. Total carriageway flow is defined as number of vehicles
in 15 min intervals. It shows peaks in traffic flows at 07:00-09:00 and around 16:00, which
corresponds well to people driving to and from work respectively. Furthermore, the period
from 05:00-09:00 seems to be divided into two separate tendencies. To try to clarify this,
1Email correspondences with author of Ref.

manufactures from Fastned (support@fastned.co.uk, Feb 2022), Setec (info@setec.com, Feb 2022),
Tesla (greve_sales@tesla.com, Feb 2022), Grasen (sales@grasen.com, Feb 2022), and WyeEVcharger
(support@wyeevcharger.com, Feb 2022)
2for A1(M) between J17 and J16

==PAGE:45==
3. Model Development
the data has been divided into weekdays (Monday-Friday) and weekends (Saturday-Sunday)
as shown in Figure 3.5. The weekdays and weekend days are represented by the red and
blue dots, respectively.

weekend days. A two week interval for both southbound and northbound as in Figure 3.7a

It is seen from Figure 3.7a and Figure 3.7b that there are differences between the direction
of the traffic. The southbound direction has the highest probability in the morning time for
weekdays, whereas this is in the afternoon time for the northbound direction. This indicates
that more people drive towards London in the morning (southbound) and out of London in
the afternoon (northbound). Some differences are also present in the weekend distributions.
Similar for both the southbound and northbound direction is that the distributions are
mainly concentrated around morning and afternoon peaks for the weekdays and more
evenly distributed throughout the day for weekends. Both the northbound and southbound
arrival distributions can be chosen for simulation scenarios later in the investigation.
Charged energy and charging duration probability distributions
To estimate probability distributions for charged energy and charging duration without
actual empirical data for HPC stations, a set of assumptions is defined:
1. The EVs connecting to the chargers are categorized into three different charging
size definitions; small, medium, and large. Small EVs are defined as vehicles able
to charge with an average power of P≤50 kW, medium EVs are capable of charging
with average 50 kW<P<125 kW, and large EVs are defined as the vehicles that can
charge with P≥125 kW in average. The SOC interval for the average charging values
is 20 to 80 %. This SOC interval is used considering the practical condition of
26
==PAGE:47==
3. Model Development
general charging behavior where charging power significantly decreases beyond 80 %
as generally seen from charging profiles [10].
2. The charging curves and battery capacity of the EVs that will be used in the small,
medium, and large categories are chosen from the top thirty EVs sold in January-May
2021 [45]. It is desired to have three different EV models for each category to limit
charging curve biases from a single EV model. The chosen models are defined from
the most sold vehicles in each category. They are shown with their respective battery
capacity and average charging power in Table 3.1. The values for charging curves are
found in Ref. [10]. The values for available battery capacities are found in Ref. [9]
3. To determine the demanded energy for an EV charging session, the arrival and
departure SOC are estimated. It is assumed that arrival SOC are Weibull distributed
with the scale and shape factor set at 24 and 2.5, respectively in this study. This
represents arrival SOC with a peak around 20 % where EV users are most likely to
plug in their EVs according to Ref. [30]. The departure time is also assumed Weibull
distributed with the scale and shape factor set at 80 and 25, respectively in this
study. This is an attempt to represent general charging behavior where charging
power significantly decreases beyond 80 % and therefore EV users are assumed to
disconnect the charging around this SOC value. The higher shape factor for departure
SOC compared to arrival SOC is chosen to reflect the assumption that EV users
unplugging from the charging station close to 80 % SOC, whereas arrival SOC values
may vary more. The Weibull distributions for arrival and departure SOC are shown
in Figure 3.9a, based on 100.000 randomly generated observations.
4. Required SOC is relating to the energy required in a given charging session. It is
defined based on the assumption that SOC is linear with energy as seen in Ref. [46]
and illustrated in Figure 3.10. This is not a perfect linearity, but compared to the
other uncertainties in the investigation it is not considered critical. The required
SOC distribution is shown in Figure 3.9b.
With the assumptions defined the charging profiles can be estimated. The charging profiles
for the chosen EVs in Table 3.1 have been added from Ref. [10] and the averages of each
categories have been found in Figure 3.8. The charging profiles are a collection of multiple
sessions with ideal circumstances (optimal battery temperature and low SOC) [47]. The
average is also calculated for the usable capacities found in Ref. [9] for each category. Only
two large EV charging profiles have been taken into account, since only these two were
present on the top 30 list of sold EVs in January-May 2021 in Europe [45]. Therefore, the
population of a potential third large EV for determining average charging profile for this
category is considered very small and irrelevant.
The EV charging profiles for the different categories are clearly different from each other
with the large EVs having significantly higher charging power throughout the SOC range
compared to the medium EVs and small EVs. The same is present for the medium EVs
compared to the small EVs. The average of each category have been found to determine
the distribution of the charged energy demand. The average charging power and usable
battery capacity for the categories are seen in Table 3.2. In this investigation the energy
demand for charging a given EV is based on the required SOC for a charging session. This
makes the energy demand depend on the SOC distributions for arrival and departure of
the EVs as shown in Figure 3.9a.

Echarged,x = EVcap,x · SOCreq
(3.2)
Where Echarged,x denotes the charged energy for a given category, EVcap,x denotes the

required SOC distribution in Figure 3.9b. Calculating the energy values like this is possible
with the stated assumption of linearity between SOC and energy as shown in Figure 3.10
for two common lithium-ion battery types.

Charged energy as a distribution is desired to create the probability distributions that will
be fed into the simulation model. Figure 3.11 shows that the charged energy of the small
EVs are lowest distributed around 18 kWh, the medium EVs in the middle around 35 kWh,
and the large EVs are highest around 50 kWh. Those are direct consequences of EVs with
higher charging capabilities also generally comes with a large battery capacity.
The duration of a charging session is based on the charged energy in Figure 3.11 and the
mean of the power in the required SOC range from the charging profiles in Figure 3.8. It
is calculated as
tcharge,x = Echarged,x/Pavg,x · 3600
(3.3)
where tcharge,x denotes the charging duration in seconds for the given EV charging category
and Pavg,x denotes the average power in the required SOC range for the given EV charging
category. The charging duration distribution is shown in Figure 3.12.
