All in all there are significant differences between the probability distributions for the
Netherlands data, Bornholm data, and the probability distributions based on assumptions
rather than empirical data.
First of all, it has been concluded that the Bornholm data do not present sufficient number
of observations to determine tendencies in neither the arrival time, charging duration nor
energy demand, see Figure 3.3. Also the location and the zero charging cost adds to the
conclusion that the data cannot be used for the investigations in this project.
Differences are also present, when comparing the probability distributions based on the data
from the Netherlands in Figure 3.4 and the probability distributions based on assumptions
in Figure 3.13. The distributions based on the data from the Netherlands show tendencies
that are expected from charging stations with lower charging capabilities, since the average
charging power is 41 kW. The amount of charged energy is also relatively low around
10-20 kWh, which is the amount expected to be charged for EVs with quite small battery
sizes. The arrival time distribution shows times based on several different chargers placed
at various locations, which makes it hard to determine if this is actually representative
for locations where it would be feasible to install HPC stations. On the contrary, the
estimated probability distributions show higher average charging powers for medium and
large categories of EVs at approximately 85 kW for the medium EVs and 165 kW for the
large EVs. They have corresponding high charging energy at around 35 kWh for medium
EVs and 50 kWh for large EVs. The arrival times are based on a heavy trafficked location,
assumed to be financially beneficial to install HPC stations. The EV profiles are based on
the assumption that EV users will use the HPC stations similar to the filling stations for
31
==PAGE:52==
3. Model Development
ICE vehicles.
The main positive feature of the charging data from the Netherlands is that it builds on
actual collected data with a large amount of observations. So therefore the data set is good,
but may not represent the charging behaviour that this study is interested in investigating.
For the estimated probability distributions it is the opposite. Here the data are hard to
validate since many uncertainties are present in the assumptions, but on the other hand
the behaviour based on the assumptions is closer to what this project expects from HPC
stations.
Conclusively, it has been decided to use the estimated probability distributions to generate
EV profiles for the investigations in this project. There are a lot of unknown variables in
how HPC stations will be used in the future, but the estimated probabilities gives the
possibility to clearly define which scenarios are investigated. Also, the parameters can
easily be changed to accommodate new EVs on the market and more detailed charging
behavior information, when this becomes available. So to have a clear definition of the EV
profiles in this project, the approach of the estimated probability distributions has been
chosen seen in Figure 3.13.
3.3.2
Creation of random EV profiles based on probability distributions
In this section the creation of randomized EV profiles as a time series will be described.
These EV profiles will be generated based on the estimated probability distributions for
arrival time, charging duration, and charged energy in Figure 3.13. This is done based
on the provided Matlab scripts. Those scripts were already used in Ref. [4] for creating
random EV profiles from the Netherlands data set. Modifications have been made to the
script to accommodate the three different sizing categories for charging implemented in
this project. Changes have also been made to the script to represent a system with only
one charger instead of the two chargers as in the original script.
A number of inputs are defined for the generation of EV profiles. Firstly, the number of
EVs to visit the station during a day is defined, meaning that different controlled scenarios
for the system can be investigated, e.g. few EVs arriving or stressed situations with large
quantities of EVs arriving. Secondly, the number of days is defined in the script, which
allows for a longer period of time to be investigated. Furthermore, it can also be used to
include/exclude different behaviours depending on days of the week etc, if this is desired.
Since three categories of EVs are defined, the distributions of the EVs arriving from the
the three different categories are specified in Table 3.2. The EV fleet distribution is loosely
determined by number of sold EVs from Table 3.2. The distribution of small EVs using the
HPC station is assumed low, since the charging capabilities of the station is large and price
for charging at HPC stations is larger than other charging options, e.g. home-charging
or charging stations with lower power capabilities [48]. The small EVs would not benefit
from using HPC stations, as the EVs would limit the power transfer to approximately
50 kW as seen in Figure 3.8. Uncertainties are present for the distributions, since there are
a lot of unknown variables in how HPC stations will be used and who will use them in
the future. Therefore, the priority in this project is to be able to clearly define a scenario.
More detailed knowledge of the charging patterns will be easy to apply to this distribution
32
==PAGE:53==
3. Model Development
in the future. The distribution is implemented in the script as
X
EVcat,x = EVcat,small + EVcat,medium + EVcat,large = 1
(3.4)
where EVcat,x denotes the share of each EV category summing up to 1. The actual number
of EVs in each category is determined on the distribution for the total amount of EVs
during the total time period. Therefore, considerations regarding the distributions need to
be kept in mind if low amount of EVs and few days are given as inputs to the simulation.
The distribution of the initial investigation is set to EVcat,small = 0.18, EVcat,medium = 0.69,
and EVcat,large = 0.13. This is based on the assumption that small EVs are three times
less likely to use the HPC station.
The EV profiles are generated from charging duration and energy probability distributions
as shown in figure Figure 3.13. To generate a single EV time series profile, a randomised
point on each probability curve is chosen with charging duration and charged energy for
the same EV category. The point from the arrival plot, determines at what time the EV
starts charging. A combination of the duration and energy plot, determines the amount
of power and the amount of time the power is charging the EV. The maximum power is
set to 300 kW to comply with the NSS system as seen in Appendix B. When multiple EV
profiles throughout a day are desired, a probability of overlapping EV profiles is present.
To prevent overlapping EV profiles, the overlapping EV profile is queued to arrive after the
first EV profile is finished by adjusting the arrival time. This will impact the probability
curve of the arrival time, but considering the probability of the duration is highest at
approximately 20-25 minutes, delaying concerning EV profiles will not change much in the
arrival probability and will actually introduce an estimation of queuing phenomenon. This
is only valid for fairly low amounts of EVs as the chance of queuing on another queue gets
higher with higher EV penetration levels.
An example of randomized EV profile for fourteen days, with fifteen EVs on average per day,
and the distribution equal to EVcat,small = 0.18, EVcat,medium = 0.69, and EVcat,large = 0.13
is shown in Figure 3.14.

Figure 3.14. Example of randomly generated EV profile time series. Distribution of EVs; small:

As seen in Figure 3.14, the charging power varies significantly from lower charges around 30-
50 kW and up to over 250 kW in a few cases. This is the desired result from the probability
distributions with varying category sizes for charging capabilities. The charging values
for power and duration around the highest probability will be reflected many times in the

3. Model Development
time series, but it will also include a few observations for the low and high extremities.
This concludes the considerations in the creation of the randomized EV profiles time series
that will be fed to the simulation model.
3.4
Model extension
This section will give an explanation of the upgrades made to the model used in Ref. [3, 4, 8].
The upgrades are made for the model to comply with the sensitivity analysis for sizing of
the BESS components and to include additional functionalities into the model that are
desired.
First the changes made to the EMS and the inverter setpoint are described in section
3.4.1 and 3.4.1.4. The changes include both additional functionalities in the EMS and
changes made to the inverter setpoint to more correctly represent the real system from NSS.
Thereafter, the scaling method for the BESS is discussed in section 3.4.2. This is to explain
how the battery sizes will be scaled in the investigations of this thesis. Next, how the
thermal model is included will be presented in section 3.4.3 followed by the considerations
for battery degradation in section 3.4.4. The last part in section 3.4.5 will describe how the
economic considerations are included with respect to both system costs and the operational
economy of the system.
3.4.1
Energy management system
In this section the considerations to upgrading the existing EMS in Ref. [3, 4, 8] will be
presented. Changing the EMS from the previous work is desired in this project to start the
scaling investigation with a simple and sturdy EMS. Hence, this is initially designed for a
simple two strings one charger (2S1C) system, which is explained in detail in section 4.1.
It is possible to make the EMS more advanced later in the investigation, to include other
functionalities. First, the main objective of the EMS will be presented in section 3.4.1.1.
Secondly, an implemented functionality for sharing the power between the battery strings
at high power demands will be explained in section 3.4.1.2. Lastly, this thesis implement
a so-called "hot swap" functionality, allowing for dynamic swap between battery strings
during charging will be described in section 3.4.1.3.
3.4.1.1
The main objective
The main objective of the initial EMS is keeping the SOC of the BESS within set operational
limits of 10-90% and as close to the upper operational limit as possible. This is prioritised
to be able to charge as many EVs as possible. A flowchart of this decision making can be
found in Figure C.6 in Appendix C. Keeping the SOC within these desired limits, can
be explained looking into the open circuit voltage of the battery cells as function of the
SOC in Figure 3.15. At lower SOC values it can be seen that the characteristic has a steep
drop and at higher SOC the characteristic increases significantly. The voltage is relatively
steady within the 10-90%. Hence this is a preferable operational region to keep a fairly
constant voltage.
34
==PAGE:55==
3. Model Development
0

Figure 3.15. Open Circuit Voltage vs. State of Charge for a single Lithium Iron Phosphate
battery cell [5]
The SOC lower and higher operational limits of 10% and 90% are also used for the values
for triggering the protection system in this investigation. The flowchart for this control
can be found in Figure C.5 in Appendix C. Previously, in Ref. [4] there was a buffer range
between the protection limit and the SOC limit. This limits the available capacity in the
BESS strings and the buffer has been removed, assuming the EMS will operate the BESS
within the SOC operational limits. The protection signal is also fed to the EMS in this
thesis, which enables the EMS to keep track of the SOC limits.
3.4.1.2
Charging power sharing between strings in two strings one charger
setup
The EMS has also been upgraded to include sharing the power demand from EVs with high
power requests. This means that if the EV charging profiles have power request higher
than the implemented BESS capacity size dependent string limit, the power will be shared
between the two strings in the BESS. This feature has been implemented in the EMS to
reflect the reality of the system as seen in data sheet in Appendix B. In the data sheet it
can be seen that the maximum string limit is 150 kW, and that it can only be charging
with up to 300 kW when both strings are connected to the same charger. If one battery
string drops below the SOC limit, the other string will continue to charge for the rest of
the charging duration of that EV request as shown in Figure 3.16. This limit is calculated
at a specified voltage, which is assumed to be where the string is operating.
In Figure 3.16 the EV power request is seen in the top plot. This includes the request sent
from the EV (P_EV) and the power request per string handled by the EMS (P_EV_string).
It can be seen that for power requests above the string limit, P_EV_string = ½ P_EV.
This is since it is divided between the two strings as seen in the middle plot. This is valid
while SOC is larger than the SOC limit for both battery strings. When the SOC falls
below the limit as observed around 32 min in the lower plot, the power delivered from the
remaining string will be equal to the maximum string limit. The flowchart for this control
can be found in Figure C.1 and C.4 in Appendix C.

3.4.1.3
Hot swap functionality
A feature named hot swap is implemented in the EMS. This is an extension to the EMS
functionality from the prototype on Bornholm and previous works [3, 4, 8]. This enables
dynamic switching between battery strings during EV charging, if the connected battery
string drops below the SOC limit. Hot swap is implemented to investigate the potential
benefit of dynamic switching during EV charging. If hot swap is enabled and the string
drops below SOC limit, the charging request can be successfully completed by dynamically
switching to the other available battery strings, assuming it have available energy. The
hot swap will only trigger once per charging session to not continuously discharge both
battery strings.
An upper hot swap SOC limit has also been created. This changes the strings if the battery
string that is not connected to the EV during charging reaches the upper SOC limit. The
potential benefit of this, is that additional energy can be charged to the BESS during
charging of EVs, instead of stopping power transfer if the string not used for EV charging
reach the upper SOC limit. The flowchart for this control can be found in Figure C.3 in
Appendix C.
Lower- and higher-limit SOC hot swap functionalities are displayed in Figure 3.17. It
should be noted that positive power is discharging the BESS and negative power is charging
the BESS. A 100 kW inverter is used in this example. The power request from the charging
station, battery string power, and SOC are plotted for the hot swap functionality. To
display both the lower- and higher limit SOC hot swap in the same plot, a relatively low
36

power is requested for a long period of time with high initial SOC for both strings. In the
beginning of the EV charging period battery string 1 charges the EV until around 10 min,
where the strings swap meaning battery string 1 will be charged from the grid and battery
string 2 is charging the EV. This is because of the SOC of battery string 2 reaching the
reference at this time. Even though the SOC of string 1 is reaching the SOC reference, it
will not swap over again, since each of the hot swaps will only trigger once every charging
session to limit continuous switching. This is displaying the higher-limit SOC hot swap.
Battery string 2 keeps charging the EV until approximately 68 min, where the SOC of
battery string 2 reaches the lower limit, which triggers the lower-limit SOC hot swap. As
a result battery string 2 will be charged from the grid and battery string 1 will charge the
EV. The hot swap functionalities are hereby confirmed from the behaviour in Figure 3.17.
This concludes the initial changes made to the existing EMS in Ref. [3, 4, 8]. Since
an optimal EMS depends heavily on the system configuration and the overall desirable
behaviour, the specific EMS for each system configuration in chapter 4 will be described in
further detail.
3.4.1.4
Inverter setpoint
This section describes changes to the existing inverter setpoint in Ref. [3, 4, 8]. The
inverter setpoint has been changed to reflect the actual behavior of the NSS system. In
the previous model, it was implemented as a droop controller. In a droop controller the
response depends on the difference between the monitored and the reference value. In this
37
==PAGE:58==
3. Model Development
case, it meant that the magnitude power transfer to the battery strings from the grid was
dependent on how much the SOC was varying from the SOC reference.
The inverter control for the NSS BESS is a simple "switch". This means that when the
SOC is lower than the SOC reference, rated inverter power is requested from the grid. For
the simple model only import from the grid is considered. This is possible to change for
future investigations to also include export to the grid. In this project the inverter setpoint
has been implemented as shown in Figure 3.18. The string signals s1 and s2 are compared
Figure 3.18. Implementation of inverter setpoint in the simulation model
with the inverter signal number "4". The inverter setpoint implemented in the model is
based on logical operators, since the value for the inverter setpoint is binary chosen to
either 0 (no charge) or 1 (full charge).
The difference between the previously implemented droop controller inverter setpoint in
Ref. [3, 4, 8] and the newly implemented on/off controller is highlighted in Figure 3.19.
The test scenario in Figure 3.19 shows a EV charging session from 0-50 min. Hereafter, a

initially react with full active power capability. However, after the SOC gets closer to
the 0.8 pu reference, the droop controller lowers the power request from the inverter as it
can be seen from around 60 min in Figure 3.19. This consequently means that the BESS
system will be charged faster with the newly implemented on/off controller. This concludes
the upgrading of the inverter setpoint to correspond more realistically to the actual system
from NSS.
3.4.2
Scaling methods
The original model explained in section 3.1 contained three dual BESS strings of 104 kWh
each. To find an optimal size of the system, scaling of the BESS is necessary. Therefore,
this section will describe considerations and assumptions behind scaling the BESS. First,
three different ways to scale the BESS are discussed in section 3.4.2.1. Hereafter, the
efficiency impact of the BESS scaling method used will be described in section 3.4.2.2.
3.4.2.1
BESS scaling
Three ways of scaling the BESS are visualized in Figure 3.20. The first one from the left
depicts the 100 Ah prismatic LFP cells added in series [3]. The middle depicts varying the
cell capacity and keeping the amount of cells constant. The last one depicts adding strings
in parallel, so keeping the amount of cells and capacity within a string constant and add
extra strings in parallel.
Adding 
cells in
series
Lower : 70 Ah 
Reference : 100 Ah
Upper: 130 Ah
Cell Ah scaling
Adding strings
Figure 3.20. Scaling of BESS
Adding cells in series will increase the amount of unused cells, which will add extra
resistance to the system from the extra Nerve Switches, bus bars etc. needed. Adding cells
in series will load the cells more compared to adding more capacity to each cell at the
same current level, since the C-rating of the cells increases with larger capacity. Adding
extra capacity to each cell will therefore load the cells more "optimal" compared to adding
cells in series. Extra cell capacity will also decrease the cell resistance [49], which is the
majority of the resistance for a battery cell controlled by the Nerve Switch [5] and vice
versa for lower cell capacities. Adding cells in series will give more freedom to balancing
39
==PAGE:60==
3. Model Development
SOC and permanently bypass potential damaged cells and still be able to provide the
demanded voltage level. The efficiency curves of the BESS string will be affected by adding
cells in series or increasing the capacity. On the other hand the efficiency curve will not
be affected by adding extra strings. Scaling the system by adding cells in series or extra
capacity will give the possibility to scale the system capacity with a higher resolution
compared to adding extra strings. The physical dimensions of the cells potentially change
if the cell capacity is changed. This potentially requires the Nerve Switch design to be
updated, whereas adding the cells in series or extra strings will use the existing design.
As a detailed system sizing is desired in this thesis, scaling with extra strings will not be
considered in the scaling process. Increasing the overall BESS capacity will be the only
option if scaling is carried out by adding cells in series, assuming the amount of cells used
in the original model to be a minimum for the Nerve Switch to function. Therefore, to be
able to increase and decrease the BESS size, the BESS scaling method used throughout the
investigations in this thesis will be carried out by keeping the amount of cells constant and
changing the cell capacity. From an optimal sizing perspective the step size of scaling the
cell capacity should be as low as possible, but considering the various LFP cell capacities
available on the market, the step size in this thesis will be 10 Ah [50, 51, 52, 53]. To keep
this process as simple as possible, the maximum required voltage from the BESS controlled
by the Nerve Switch including the extra gap is assumed to be 1000 V. Doing this will
directly convert the kWh capacity to Ah with a factor of 1000 in difference, assuming
perfect SOC balancing.
3.4.2.2
Efficiency curves
The original battery model from Ref. [3] consists of a power input, which through a loss
model and SOC estimation resulted in a SOC and power of the battery pack. These blocks
are shown in Figure 3.21. The SOC model is kept unchanged, whereas changes to the
Figure 3.21. Block diagram of battery model used [3]
loss model are updated to account for scaling of the system. This is required since the
Ah scaling method introduces changes to the battery efficiency. The original loss model
is shown in Figure 3.22, where the efficiency is solely a function of battery power. The
efficiency curves for the assumed desired voltage level of 800 V have been provided from
Ref. [5]. To account for the impact of the scaling method used, BESS capacity dependent
updates to the efficiency curves must be made, which are covered in this section.
The efficiency curve in Ref. [5] takes the power dependent voltage drop across the battery
40
==PAGE:61==
3. Model Development
Figure 3.22. Block diagram of the loss model from Figure 3.21 [3]
resistance and Nerve Switches etc. into account when calculating the required amount of
engaged cells to provide the desired pack voltage. To account for the sizing method, the
battery resistance is assumed to vary with Ah capacity following the trend expressed in
Equation 3.5 [54]. This states that if the capacity is doubled, then the resistance will be
halved and vice versa.
Rnew = Rold
Ahold
Ahnew
(3.5)
This approach is implemented in the model resulting in updated efficiency curves to also
depend on battery capacity. These have been calculated based on the method presented
in Ref. [5] and are provided by the supervisors. The efficiency block diagram updates in
Matlab Simulink are seen in Figure 3.23 to now also include a battery capacity dependency.
Figure 3.23. Block diagram of the loss model including capacity dependent efficiency

in Figure 3.24. Here the sign convention of the power describes whether the battery is

==PAGE:62==
3. Model Development
charged or discharged. Positive power indicates discharging and negative power indicates
charging of the BESS. The efficiency is found to increase for larger battery capacities and
decrease for smaller capacities. This concludes how the scaling method has been chosen
and what impact this method has on the efficiency of the BESS.
3.4.3
Thermal system
As mentioned in section 2.2 the temperature is found to be one of the controllable
parameters, which has the largest impact on the battery degradation. Therefore the
thermal management system is an important part of the BESS.
In the previous project [3], a detailed dynamic thermal model was developed based on the
physical dimensions of the system installed at Bornholm. The model included thermal
capacitance of different system components, air flow etc. The temperature from the thermal
model was monitored as a system performance parameter in Ref. [4] and [8]. Furthermore,
the temperature was used to define when and how the cooling system was operating, which
depended on temperature threshold and fan control.
This thesis focuses on scaling the system and investigating different topologies, thus
changing the physical dimensions of the thermal parameters obtained in Ref. [3]. Therefore,
simplified modifications to the thermal model will be made, which will be described in this
section. To minimize the degradation and complexity of the simulation, the thermal model
is assumed to maintain a steady temperature at approximately 20-25 C◦, which was found
to be the temperature at which the battery was operating optimal [34, 35].
Since a first order thermal model would dissipate the power right when its needed, the
auxiliary power of the heat pump, might be modelled momentary larger than in reality.
As the grid connection is assumed to have a certain size, the potential charging power
through the inverter will be limited by the auxiliary power. Therefore, during EV charging
the grid power used to charge unused strings will be limited, effectively increasing the
BESS’s charging time. This can potentially have an impact on the momentary system
performance if multiple EVs are connecting right after each other. As this does not reflect
the reality, another approach is implemented in the model. This can then potentially be
used for future investigations of the thermal impacts. The thermal impacts solely consider
the energy, not the thermal impact on e.g. resistances etc.
To counter the problem of momentary larger heat pump power than in reality, an alternative
thermal model will be presented in this section.
This thermal model builds on the
assumption that a constant power buffer accounts for the thermal losses. This power buffer
has been set to 3 kW to account for the rated power consumption of the heat pump in
Appendix A, the water cooled system, which accounts for the losses associated with cooling
down the HPC cable, and control room auxiliary consumption. This buffer is implemented
in the model so that the available grid connection is 3 kW larger than the rated power of
the inverter size investigated.
This thermal model has the advantage over the previously presented model that it will
not limit the power import of the converter while EVs are charging. However, it neither
completely reflects reality, as the calculated costs in the model will be slightly higher than
42
==PAGE:63==
3. Model Development
necessary due to the larger grid connection size. The slight increase in the cost value is
considered better than the disturbance of important power. Furthermore, since it is not
within the time frame of this thesis to continuously consider the physical dimensions for a
more in depth thermal model, the thermal model considering constant power buffer will
be used in this thesis’ investigation of sizing the HPC station.
3.4.4
Battery degradation
As mentioned in section 2.2 some battery degradation parameters will be monitored. The
battery degradation parameters considered in this thesis only concerns SOC, cycles, and
loading current. The temperature is an important degradation parameter, but is assumed
to be covered by thermal management system. Therefore, the thermal impact will not be
the scope of this thesis. It should be clearly noted that these parameters will not be used to
estimate the lifetime of the battery. Therefore, the dynamic impact of the state of health
is not considered. The battery degradation metrics will rather be used for comparison
between setups to indicate whether a specific system configuration or operation potentially
will degrade the battery faster. The comparison will simply be by comparing numerical
values with no weighted difference in lifetime degradation.
State of charge
The SOC is averaged for each BESS string over the entire simulation period to give an
indication of how the SOC is behaving. Then the average of these will be calculated and
used as an indicator for the entire system. From the literature review in section 2.2, higher
SOC have shown to degrade the battery faster than a lower SOC, which essentially is what
the SOC measurement will be used for in the degradation comparison. Some studies have
shown that the battery cell resistance varies for different SOC values [54]. The impact of
these will not be considered in this study.
Cycles
The cycle estimation used is the same as in the previous model [3]. It is based on the total
energy throughput of each string divided by the energy capacity and then halved to get
full cycles equivalent to one full discharge and one full charge. The cycles for each string is
then averaged. These average cycles will be used as a cycle degradation indicator for the
entire system. More cycles increases the cycle degradation of the batteries.
Loading current
As mentioned in section 3.4.2.1 the scaling method used is in this thesis keeps the amount
of battery cells constant and changes the cell capacity. This will have an impact on the
loading of the batteries. For instance the same numerical loading current of two different
Ah batteries will have different percentage loading, assuming the exact same chemistry
and battery type. Therefore, a current in per unit with respect to the BESS capacity
will be calculated. Since this project is mainly focusing on power and energy levels, some
assumptions need to be made to make this calculation. From previous battery model in
43
==PAGE:64==
3. Model Development
Ref. [3] a terminal power of the battery strings is provided. To calculate the current from
this power the operating voltage and Ah capacity of the cells are needed.
The Ah capacity of the cells will be based on the assumption that the amount of cells is
assumed to provide a maximum DC voltage of 1000 V, including the extra capacity needed
to operate the Nerve Switch. From this assumption a simple relation between kWh and
Ah capacity is obtained. From storage capacity and maximum DC voltage the Ah capacity
(capkAh) can be found:
capkAh = capkWh
Vdc-max
(3.6)
Which is equivalent to the C-rate of the cell, found by:
IC−rate = capkAh
1 hr
(3.7)
Then based on the operating voltage (Vdc-operate), assumed to be 800 V (section 3.4.2.2),
and the terminal power (Pterm) of the battery, a numerical loading current (Il) for the
setup can be found:
Il =
Pterm
Vdc-operate
(3.8)
From the Ah capacity and the loading current a per unit loading current (Il−pu) can be
found:
Il-pu =
Il
IC−rate
(3.9)
To monitor how Il−pu is behaving during charging and discharging the BESS, it will be
divided into two, one for charging currents and one for discharging currents. These values
will the be averaged individually for each string during loading, meaning all zero values
will be removed. These will then be averaged depending on the amount of strings to get a
picture for the entire system.
Monitoring the current in this way cannot give a proper indication of the degradation. To
explain this, a scenario will be used with two EVs at same voltages and energy request but
different current demand. In this scenario the EV with the low current demand will have
a larger impact on the total average current than the other, even though they request the
same energy. Therefore, the current will only be used as a degradation indicator between
configurations. Simply if Il−pu is larger for one system, then this is likely to degrade faster.
3.4.5
Economy
This section will describe where the financial values have been acquired and how they will
affect the economic output of the system. The economy is divided into two main sections.
Section 3.4.5.1 describes the costs associated with buying the system, which will vary with
sizing of the various components. Section 3.4.5.2 describes the operational economy of the
system, such as electricity prices and income from charging vehicles and is only considering
data from Denmark converted to EUR.
3.4.5.1
System Components
The main system components are the BESS strings, grid inverter, and EV chargers. The
purpose of this section is to narrow the cost of a BESS system with EV chargers into three
scalabe parameters, listed below.
44
==PAGE:65==
3. Model Development
1. Amount of EV hyper chargers [kEUR/pcs].
2. Energy size [kEUR/kWh]
3. Power size [kEUR/kW]
The cost of a HPC station from Nerve Smart System is 168 kEUR3. This setup contains
two 100 kWh BESS strings, two EV hyper chargers of 150 kW each, and two 50 kW grid
inverters. The cost of this is denoted as ΛBESS+2×HPC. The price of the same BESS
system excluding the EV chargers is 128 kEUR, where the cost is denoted as ΛBESS. It
should be noted that this price is excluding the grid connection cost, which will be described
in Equation 3.12. This overall system cost will be used as a reference to determine the
BESS components cost in this section.
EV high power charger cost
To determine the cost per EV charger, Λcharger, the difference between the before mentioned
systems from NSS is used and shown in Equation 3.10.
Λcharger = ΛBESS+2×HPC −ΛBESS
2 × HPC
= 168 kEUR −128 kEUR
2 × HPC
= 20 kEUR/HPC
(3.10)
This is implemented in Simulink as a constant depending on the amount of EV chargers
used in the setup.
BESS sizing cost
The cost of a BESS consists of various parts, such as BMS, EMS, battery cells, grid
inverter, assembly etc. These components add cost to the overall system depending on
how the system is scaled. E.g. if the energy capacity is increased and power capacity is
kept constant, extra battery cells and BMS are required. This will impact the overall price
depending on the amount of extra energy capacity, whereas the cost of power equipment
would remain constant and vice versa.
To estimate the price of individual system components, the cost and performance database
from Pacific Northwest National Laboratory (PNNL) have been used [11]. Here individual
system component cost of various energy storage systems have been investigated, such as
different chemistries for BESSs, compressed air, pumped hydro, and hydrogen. As the
chemistry used in this thesis is LFP, only the system cost of this chemistry is considered.
Various system sizes have been investigated in Ref. [11], with power capacity ranging from
1-100 MW and energy capacity ranging from 2-800 MWh. Only the system with a power
capacity of 1 MW and energy capacity of 2 MWh will be used to estimate the individual
system cost. This exact system from Ref. [11] has been chosen since the power and energy
ratings were closest to the NSS system and have the same energy to power ratio of two. An
overview of the system specific component price distribution for a LFP BESS estimated
from PNNL is presented in Table 3.3. These estimations include a 2020 estimation, which

The block definitions from PNNL [55] is summarized here:
The "Storage Block" consists of battery cells, BMS, and battery module. The "Balance of
System" consists of cabling, heat pumps etc. The "System Integration" consists of assembling
the components to a functional system. The "EPC" consists of engineering, procurement,
and construction cost. The "Development" consists of power purchase agreements etc. The
"Communication and control" mainly consists of EMS. The "Power Equipment" consists of
converters and protection components. Lastly, the "Grid integration" consists of connecting
the BESS to the grid.
As the overall system prices provided for the NSS system exclude the cost of "Grid
Integration", this will also be excluded from this system cost estimation. Furthermore
the "Development" part will be excluded as no signification power agreement is assumed
necessary for the load size investigated in this thesis. The NSS BMS is assumed to be
more expensive than conventional BMSs, therefore 15% is added to the "Storage Block".
These estimations are calculated and summarized in Table 3.4. It should be noted that
the cost in Table 3.4 is now overall prices in [kEUR] and not [EUR/kWh] or [EUR/kW].
This is to show the actual cost distribution of the system components, which will be used
to estimate the specific component cost of the NSS system.
From the estimated percentage distributions in Table 3.4 and the NSS system cost, of
128 kEUR, an estimation of NSSs specific system component cost can be found. This is
summarized in Table 3.5. The 2030 projection of the NSS system cost is based on the
relative difference between the 2020 and 2030 cost in Table 3.4 from PNNL. These 2020
and 2030 cost projections will be compared on a system level basis to investigate the
feasibility of building such systems in the near future.

cost is assumed to be covered as a part of the higher component cost found in Table 3.6.

The total amount of energy delivered to the EVs is heavily depending on the charging
behavior, which is described in section 3.3. A brief comparison of the current EV charging

Electricity Prices
There are mainly two ways of paying the electricity bill, through either a fixed or variable
EUR/kWh price. The fixed price is normally locked for a certain period, usually of several
years, whereas the variable is changing throughout the day [60]. Only the variable pricing
scheme will be considered in this thesis and it is based on the distribution tariffs and spot
prices presented in this section.
To understand the variability of the variable electricity price, the different parts of the
electricity price should be introduced.
Consuming industry customers
The electricity bill for consuming industry customers in Denmark mainly consists of three
parts [61], which are visualized by the green solid arrows in Figure 3.25.
• Transmission tariff
• Distribution tariff
• Energy usage - Spot price

Household customers also have to pay electricity taxes and 20% VAT on the total electricity
bill. The VAT can be fully refunded for industry customers. The electricity tax for
household customers is approximately 0.121 EUR/kWh. This can almost be refunded for
industry customers except for 0.054 cEUR/kWh [62] and has therefore been neglected.
The transmission tariff, Λtrans−tariff is paying for transporting the electricity through the
transmission system, which in Denmark is owned by Energinet. The transmission tariff is
a fixed cost and the average from 2016 to 2019 have been found to be [63]:
Λtrans−tariff = 0.011 EUR/kWh
(3.11)
This is implemented as a constant in Simulink.
The distribution tariff can either be fixed or varying throughout the day and depends
on how the customer is connected to the distribution grid [6]. Two types of customers
are connected to the 400 V network, Blow and C. The C customer is the most common
connection to the 400 V grid, such as households, connected downstream the 400 V network
of a 10/0.4 kV transformer. The Blow customer is also connected to the 400 V network but
directly at the low voltage side of a 10/0.4 kV transformer. Since the HPC system can be
installed both as a Blow and C customer in different use cases, both will be included in
this thesis. These differences are depicted in Figure 3.25.
The connection cost is different for Blow and C. The first 25 A cost 2103 EUR for both Blow
and C [64], which corresponds to P3ph = 3 · 25 A · 230 VLN = 17.25 kW, assuming a power
factor of 1. Above 25 A an extra 156 EUR/A and 163 EUR/A must be paid for Blow and C,
respectively. Each ampere in a three phase installation accounts for 3·1 A·230 V = 0.69 kW.
Therefore, extra power capacity cost approximately 226 EUR/kW and 236 EUR/kW for
Blow and C, respectively. The grid connection cost, Λgrid is summarized in Equation 3.12.

3.5
Chapter summary
This chapter has presented the different aspects for developing a model to enable the BESS
sizing sensitivity analysis. The previously used model has been briefly explained giving
an understanding of the foundation for the developed model in this thesis. Thereafter,
EV charging profiles from Bornholm and the Netherlands were presented, discussed, and
compared with EV profiles based on assumptions for arrival time, duration, and charged
energy. It was concluded that the EV profiles fed to the model will be the one based on
assumptions. This approach was shown to be the most desired, since acceptable EV data
for HPC stations are not present to this date and it is possible to clearly state where the
values used for the assumptions originate from. Lastly, this chapter showed the various
aspects of the previously used model that has been upgraded. Inclusion of additional
functionalities to the EMS, as well as economic considerations have been made. All in all,
a base model has been developed enabling the sizing of the BESS by sensitivity analysis.
Therefore, it is now possible to specify the system configurations desired to investigate and
the resultant changes required to the model to make these.
53
==PAGE:74==
4
SYSTEM CONFIGURATION
This chapter will explain the system configurations investigated in this thesis, focusing on
the differences between the different typologies, assumptions, and their control. In section
4.1 the base model consisting of a "two strings one charger" setup (2S1C) is explained. The
explanation includes the topology of the system, the important assumptions considered
and the EMS functionality. Hereafter, the "three strings two charger" setup (3S2C) is
described in section 4.2. The system topology and the assumptions are also included for
this configuration along with the changes made to the base model to obtain this 3S2C
configuration. Lastly, a configuration consisting of the "three strings two charger model
including RES" (R3S2C) is presented in section 4.3. Also for this configuration, the system
topology and the assumptions are included. This section also includes the definition of
RES profiles for PV and WTG, as well as the forecasts considered, and the RES costs.
The last part of the section covers the EMS functionality of this configuration.
4.1
Base model: two strings one charger
The system configuration for the 2S1C model will be explained in this section. It sums
up the considerations put into developing the fundamental model, from which other
configurations later can be established. First, the objectives of the base case along with the
system topology are presented in section 4.1.1. Hereafter, the assumptions for the 2S1C
configuration are summed up in section 4.1.2. Finally, section 4.1.3 recaps the priorities
and basic EMS functionalities of the base model.
4.1.1
System topology
In this thesis the base model has been created for multiple purposes: To confirm that all
functionalities in the simulation model work as intended, including inputs and outputs
as described in section 3.4. Additionally, the base model is created to ensure that the
parameter sweeps are giving expected results so that the objective of this thesis, sizing the
HPC station from sensitivity analysis, can be performed through Monte Carlo simulation.
This will be described in section 5.2 and 5.3, and how the results are processed is thoroughly
explained in section 6.1. Therefore, the topology is simplified to lower the complexity
level compared to that of the models from Ref. [3, 4]. Lastly, the base model is made to
examine if the simple system is economically feasible compared to the other topologies.
The topology for the base model is shown in Figure 4.1.
The base model consists of two battery strings, one EV HPC point, and an inverter
connection to the distribution grid. The EMS system determines how the two battery
strings connect to the grid and HPC point, respectively. The BESS capacity size and the
54
==PAGE:75==
4. System Configuration
~
baery

baery
string 1

3ph AC
400Vgrid

EV high power charger
Figure 4.1. System topology of the "two strings one charger" system.
inverter power capacity are the parameters that are swept in the sensitivity analysis at
different EV penetration levels.
4.1.2
Assumptions
Assumptions are made to limit the scope of this thesis’ investigation considering the limited
time frame of the project. These assumptions will be described in this section for the base
model. The assumptions fall within five categories: simulation parameters, thermal model,
EV profiles, economy, and the BESS.
4.1.2.1
Simulation parameters assumptions
The simulation model developed in Matlab Simulink is subject to a number of assumptions.
These assumptions are mainly made to keep the simulation time within a realistic time
frame and to keep the detail level of the investigation to a sensible level. It is not desired
to go into detail with the system on a cell level, as this thesis focus on the sizing of the
components. Therefore, the simulation will represent the system on a power and energy
level, instead of including voltages and currents, as described in section 3.1.
The dynamics of the system are considered beyond the scope of this investigation. Therefore,
only the large time scale with steady state values are used. This results in the simulation
time step used is set to sixty seconds. This assumption leads to simulation times that are
more realistic, since it is desired to simulate multiple days. The sixty seconds simulation
time step is not considered to cause significant lack of information, as it is steady state
values that are of interest.
4.1.2.2
Thermal model assumptions
The thermal model consists solely of a power buffer of 3 kW, as described in section 3.4.3.
It is implemented in this way to make the sizing of the system possible without having
to include physical dimensions for the different sizes of the BESS, as the more detailed
thermal model in Ref. [3, 4] depends on the physical parameters. The thermal model in
this thesis is assumed to always keep the system operating at optimal thermal conditions.
55
==PAGE:76==
4. System Configuration
4.1.2.3
EV profile assumptions
The EV profiles fed to the system are also subject to a number of assumptions. For the
arrival time distribution it is assumed that the HPC stations will be used similar to the
filling stations for ICE vehicles and the distribution throughout the day is based on traffic
data from National Highways, as explained in section 3.3.1.2.
A number of assumptions are also present for the charged energy and the charging duration
of the EV charging profiles. These are all well described in section 3.3.1.2. The assumptions
include:
• The EVs are defined as three different sizes; small, medium, and large, based on their
charging power capabilities.
• The charging curves that will be used for each size are determined based on sold EVs
in 2021.
• The arrival and departure SOC of the EVs are estimated based on Weibull
distributions.
• The SOC is assumed linear with the energy in the battery of the EVs.
The relatively large number of assumptions is a consequence of the fact that it has not been
possible to find satisfactory EV charging data for HPC stations as explained in section
2.1.4. The assumptions that are made makes it possible to create usable EV profiles for
this thesis objective, which can also easily be altered to fit future investigations.
EV charging profiles are generated based on the assumption that EV owners will be more
prone to charge at HPC stations when they drive. The driving behavior considered in
this thesis follows the traffic flow at a highway in England as explained in section 3.3.
Therefore, to get a realistic perspective in loading of the system during week and weekend
days the simulation will be executed throughout an equivalent fourteen day period.
4.1.2.4
Economical assumptions
The economical considerations for the components in the HPC station and electricity prices
are kept simple in this thesis and exclude discount rates. The operation and maintenance
(O&M) costs are assumed to be covered in the higher component cost found in section 3.4.5.
These assumptions are included to limit the complexity of the economical investigation, as
this is not the main objective this thesis’ investigation. Therefore, rather than giving exact
values, the economical results found in this thesis serve to compare different sizes, controls,
topologies, and EV penetration levels. This gives an understanding of how the sizing of
components and level of EV penetration influences the overall economy of the system.
4.1.2.5
Battery energy storage system assumptions
To focus on the sizing scope of this thesis objective, assumptions are also made for the
BESS. The battery degradation including SOC, cycles, and loading currents are only
monitored throughout the simulation, as discussed in section 2.2 and 3.4.4. This effectively
means that the thesis will not attempt to determine the lifetime of the system and how it
is affected by the battery degradation parameters, as this would require a study by itself.
56
==PAGE:77==
4. System Configuration
Instead the monitored battery degradation values serve to compare pros and cons of the
system operating at different sizes and controls schemes.
For the BESS, it is assumed that the power limit per battery string is 150 kW for the
reference case as mentioned in 3.4.1.2. It is scaled linearly with the energy capacity size
of the BESS. This way of scaling the power limit per battery string is a result of the
assumption that the BESS size in this thesis is scaled with Ah at fixed amount of cells,
as described in section 3.4.2. Therefore, the larger BESS size results in a larger nominal
current and thereby larger power limit per string at the same voltage.
Also, it is assumed that the SOC is linear with the energy in the BESS based on Figure 3.10
in 3.3, resulting in linear relation between SOC and energy. This simplifies how to keep
track of the energy in the BESS, by monitoring the SOC.
4.1.3
Energy management system for base model
The main priority of this EMS is to successfully charge as many arriving EVs as possible.
Therefore, the EMS in the base model will continuously try to charge the battery strings
to the max SOC limit and not export to the grid. It assigns the arriving EVs to the highest
SOC string to increase the chance of getting as many successful EV charging sessions as
possible. Furthermore, the EMS will charge the available string with the highest SOC
from the grid. This is to increase the chance of at least one string having enough energy
stored for the next EV charging session. Lastly, the hot swap functions that allow the
strings to swap in the middle of the charging sessions are also included to investigate the
potential benefits. The EMS for the base model configuration is described in depth in
section 3.4.1 and in the flowcharts in Appendix C.
4.2
Three strings two chargers model
The system configuration for the 3S2C model will be explained in this section. It sums
up the considerations put into developing the model from the 2S1C model to the 3S2C
model. The system topology is presented in section 4.2.1. Hereafter, the assumptions for
the 3S2C configuration are summed up in section 4.2.2. Lastly, section 4.2.3 explains the
changes made from the 2S1C model to the 3S2C model including EV profiles, general
considerations, as well as the changes implemented to the EMS.
4.2.1
System topology
The 2S1C model as described in section 4.1 is developed to include an extra battery string
and an additional HPC point as shown in Figure 4.2, creating the 3S2C topology .
Besides the added string and charger, the topology is the same as described for the base
case. An inverter connects the system to the distribution grid and the parameters swept for
the sensitivity analysis are BESS capacity size, inverter power capacity, and EV penetration
levels. As the system changes from two to three battery strings and an extra charger is
added, the complexity to the system increases, in particular for the EMS.
57
==PAGE:78==

Figure 4.2. System topology of the "three strings two chargers" system.
4.2.2
Assumptions
The assumptions for the 3S2C system are including the same as described for the 2S1C
system in section 4.1.2. On top of that, an additional assumption is made to the creation
of EV charging profiles for the 3S2C model: if two EVs with overlapping charging sessions
arrive, both with charging power demands larger than the power limit of the battery
strings, the charging power of the EV arrived last is limited to the battery string limit until
the end of the other charging session. This is presented in more depth in section 4.2.3.1.
4.2.3
Changes made to base model
Changes are made to the 2S1C model to develop it to the 3S2C model. These changes are
presented in this section and include: EV profiles, changes necessary for upgrading the
model from "two-string" to "three-string", and the EMS.
4.2.3.1
EV profiles
This section presents the changes made to the EV profiles fed into the 2S1C model as
described in section 3.3, to represent the needed behaviour of the 3S2C model. Two
different scenarios for overlapping EV charging session will be presented, highlighting
the pros and cons of each scenario for the scope of this thesis’ investigation. Lastly, the
approach found most sensible for feeding the 3S2C model will be chosen.
The main difference in the EV charging profiles between the 2S1C model and the 3S2C
model is that multiple EVs can arrive at the same time in the 3S2C model. This does
not only present additional challenges to the system performance, but also how the EV
profile inputs are made. In the case of overlapping charging sessions in the 2S1C model,
the EV arriving last was queued to begin after the first session finished. For the 3S2C
setup, the two chargers allow for two EVs to arrive in the same charging session interval.
In case of more than two EVs arriving, the additional EVs will be queued to begin their
charging session when a charger becomes available. It is also possible that two EVs arrive
with overlapping charging sessions, where both charging power demands are larger than
the power limit of the battery strings. It is important to notice that this is a rare case
58
==PAGE:79==
4. System Configuration
with the distribution chosen of 18% small, 69% medium, and 13% larger EVs. The chance
of getting overlapping charging session are found to be 0.14% for 8 EVs/day and 0.23%
for 16 EVs/day. These have been calculated based on generated EV profiles for 10.000
simulations each throughout 14 days1. This means that throughout a fourteen day period
there is a 15% chance for one occurrence of overlapping charging session with power larger
than the string limit for 8 EVs/day2. For 16 EVs/day the chance was found to be 51%.
Figure 4.3 illustrates the scenario of two overlapping charging sessions, both with charging
power demand larger than the string power limit. Two scenarios for how to handle these
cases are also presented here (1) limiting the power of latest arriving EV and (2) queuing
latest arriving EV to after the charging session of the first EV is completed.

Figure 4.3. Scenarios of two overlapping charging sessions with power above string limit. (1)
Limiting power (2) Queuing
The benefit of (1) is that this is more likely to represent the actual situation of two cars
arriving with large power demands. They will likely both connect the vehicles to the HPC
station, even though the power capacity will not amount to the desired value, resulting in
lower energy amount charged at the end of the charging session for the latest EV to arrive.
The drawback of this proposed solution is that the requested energy from the last EV to
arrive will not be the same as the delivered energy. This drawback is a consequence of
how the EV profiles are created for the model as a predefined input, where the charging
duration cannot be altered during simulation. This behaviour happens even though there
is still energy left in the battery, resulting in a negative impact on values for the system
performance.
The advantage of (2) is that it will not change the delivered energy from the requested
energy of the EV, as the charging session will be moved. However, this does not necessarily
reflect real behaviour as explained. Furthermore, this proposed solution will contribute to
skewing the actual arrival time distribution shown in Figure 3.13, as the arrival times will
be changed. This is also more likely to cause additional queuing compared to (1), since
the strings are occupied for longer time.
Based on the pros and cons discussed for the two solutions to handle the scenario of
overlapping charging session, both with charging power demand larger than the power
limit of the battery strings, solution (1) is chosen and implemented in the generation of
1So for 8 EVs/day x 10.000 simulations x 14 days = 1.120.000 random generated EV profiles, where
1.531 EV profiles were overlapping with power larger than the power string limit. For 16 EVs/day x 10.000
simulations x 14 days = 2.240.000 random generated EV profiles, where 5105 were overlapping with power
larger than the power string limit.
2So for 8 EVs/day 1.531 cases appeared which throughout 10.000 simulations corresponds to 15.31%
59
==PAGE:80==
4. System Configuration
EV charging profiles. The main reason for this is to not alter the arrival distribution.
Furthermore, as these scenarios are rare, the negative impact on values for the system
performance will have a negligible influence on the system, but solution (1) will likely
reflect the reality most of the two proposed solutions. The EV charging profiles for two
chargers are conclusively completed as inputs to the 3S2C simulation model.
4.2.3.2
Scaling up the model from 2S1C to 3S2C
This section describes changing the simulation model from the 2S1C model to the 3S2C
model. Figure 4.4 shows the Matlab Simulink model of the 3S2C model based on previous
works from Ref [3, 4, 8].
Figure 4.4. Model blocks introduced to the 2S2C model to get the 3S2C model (marked in blue)
[3, 4, 8]
The changes made from the 2S1C system are highlighted with blue. One of the main
changes to the operation of the system is that the added charger will enable two charging
sessions ongoing at the same time, as shown on the left hand side in Figure 4.4. For the
economics of the system, this can increase the income from charging more EVs, but at
the same time the change also adds 20 kEUR in extra costs to the system as explained in
Equation 3.10 in section 3.4.5.1. Seen from the system performance point of view, the extra
charger can potentially stress the system additionally by enabling multiple EV requests at
the same time.
The added battery string on the right hand side in Figure 4.4 adds extra battery capacity
to the system as well as one more string for the EMS to operate as highlighted in the
bottom of the figure (Signal3). The added battery string can increase the income due to
the potential of charging more EVs without depleting the strings of energy. On the other
hand, this will also add to an increase in system costs as explained in section 3.4.5.1. Lastly,
the added complexity of the 3S2C configuration compared to the 2S1C configuration, also
introduces new considerations for the EMS, as it has three strings in the algorithm to
choose from instead of two and two EV chargers.

==PAGE:81==
4. System Configuration
4.2.3.3
Changes to the EMS
This section describes the changes made to the EMS when upgrading the 2S1C base model
into the 3S2C model. These changes include simulation explanatory examples for the
power sharing and hot swap functionalities.
Charging power sharing between strings in three strings two chargers model
The power sharing between strings for the 3S2C setup is following more or less the same
ideology as for the 2S1C setup. In the rare case of two EVs with power capacity larger than
the string limit connecting in the same time slot an additional functionality is included.
The charging power of the last connected EV is limited until the first connected EV
disconnects as discussed in section 4.2.3.1. This is visualized by an example in Figure 4.5.
It should be noted that positive power is discharging the BESS and negative power is
charging the BESS. A 100 kW inverter is used in this example. Here the EV charging
power of charger 2 will be limited while both EVs are charging as seen at 11-26 min. When
the EV disconnects at charger 1, then two strings are available to deliver the demanded
power for charger 2, assuming their SOC are within the operational limits. Therefore, the
power, noted as P_EV2_string, is decreasing at 26 min since the requested is divided to
two strings, namely string 1 and 3.

==PAGE:82==
4. System Configuration
How frequently this occurs have been investigated generating EV profile samples over a
fourteen day period and then repeated 10.000 times for 8 and 16 EVs per day. This has been
carried out using the base case distribution of 18% small, 69% medium, and 13% large EVs.
Here 0.13% of the cases for 8 EVs/day and 0.23% of the cases for 16 EVs/day experienced
overlapping charging sessions larger than the string limit. The amount of EVs/day is
reflecting the amount of EVs the system is experiencing. Since two EVs with charging
capacity larger than the string limit happen rarely, the negative impact of implementing
power sharing in this way will have a negligible impact on the system performance.
Hot swap functionality in 3S2C model
The hot swap functionality for the 3S2C setup is similar to the functionality in the 2S1C
setup. The difference for the 3S2C setup is that it contains and extra string and EV charger,
which makes the control more complex. Overall the hot swap functionality contains two
features, one for upper limit and one for lower limit. The lower limit hot swap functionality
swaps a depleted battery string charging an EV to a string with sufficient SOC which
is not charging an EV. The upper limit swaps a fully charged string connected to the
grid with the string charging an EV which has the lowest SOC. Each of these features
are implemented to trigger maximum one time per charging session. Only one trigger of
hot swap is implemented to limit the amount of switching between strings. The hot swap
features will potentially increase the system performance, which will be investigated in the
results, section 6.1.5.
A case for the upper limit hot swap functionality is created specifically for visualisation
purposes and is visualized in Figure 4.6. At 25 min string 2 reaches upper SOC limit and
swaps with string 1, since this is the only EV charging string at this moment. At 39 min
string 3 is reaching the upper limit, but is not swapping with the EV charging string since
one swap have already have been carried out for this EV charging session. This concludes
the description of upper limit hot swap functionality of the three strings setup.
A case for the lower limit hot swap functionality is created specifically for visualisation
purposes and is visualized in Figure 4.7. At 13 min string 2 is reaching the lower SOC
limit. Since both of the remaining strings are charging the other EV, no hot swap is used.
At 23 min string 3 is reaching the lower SOC limit and swaps to string 2, since this is only
remaining string not charging EVs. At 30 min string 2 is reaching the lower limit again,
but is not swapping since hot swap has already been used for this EV session. Therefore,
the power of string 1 is changed to the string limit to deliver as much as possible to the
EV.
This concludes the presentation of the lower- and upper hot swap limit functionalities. It
should be noted that the EV charging sessions used in this section are overdimensioned and
only presented to highlight the hot swap functionalities. Updates to the EMS functionalities
for the 3S2C model are shown in Appendix D. These include functionalities for power
sharing and upper limit hot swap. The functionalities that are identical to the 2S1C model
can be found in Appendix C.

4.3
Three strings two chargers model with RES
The system configuration for the R3S2C model will be explained in this section. The
considerations put into including the RES to the 3S2C model will be explained. The system
topology is presented in section 4.3.1. Then the assumptions for the R3S2C configuration
are presented in section 4.3.2. Finally, section 4.3.3 describes the changes made to the
model to include RES to the 3S2C model including profiles, forecast, and costs for RES,
as well as the changes implemented to the EMS.
4.3.1
System topology
In this section the topology of the R3S2C will be explained. The topology of the system is
shown in Figure 4.8.
~

RES: PV or WTG
Figure 4.8. System topology of the "three strings two chargers and RES" system.
Compared to the system topology for 3S2C model presented in section 4.2, a RES, either
PV or WTG, is now added to the system. The RES input is an additional way to import
energy to the BESS. The RES input also results in one more option available in the EMS
algorithm, as it can be seen from the extra busbar in Figure 4.8 compared to that in
Figure 4.2. The model used is largely built on the previous works on the model described
in Ref. [3, 4, 8].
4.3.2
Assumptions
A number of assumptions are considered for the R3S2C. The assumptions are all connected
to the RES input and include assumptions regarding the RES profiles used to feed the
system, the RES forecasts, and the installation and O&M costs of the RES. The reference
RES size is assumed to be based on the connected PV array to the Bornholm system,
meaning that the scaling of the RES will be with respect to this value. These assumptions
will be presented in this section.
64
==PAGE:85==
4. System Configuration
4.3.2.1
RES profiles assumptions
The RES profiles created as input for the R3S2C model are for both PV and WTG based
on 1 year data, provided by the supervisors. The data contains a significant amount of
NaN values. To sort the data, it is assumed acceptable that if only NaN values appear
for five minutes or less, the data can be interpolated between the values before and after.
This is done as a compromise between getting more usable data and not changing the
behavior of the RES profiles. For future work, it is considered beneficial to find more than
the one year data. However, for an initial investigation of the influence of the RES input
to the HPC system, the data amount is considered acceptable. These considerations are
described further in depth in sections 4.3.3.1 and 4.3.3.2.
In order to understand how the performance of RES is affected by seasons, the RES inputs
were analysed for seasonal biases. For the RES profiles it is assumed that each season can
be represented by the fourteen closest days to the middle of each season. In this thesis the
seasons are represented by summer- and winter solstice and equinox. This is explained
thoroughly in section 4.3.3.1 and 4.3.3.2. This assumption may not entirely capture the
seasonal behaviour, as fourteen days for each season should represent the tendencies of
three months. Nonetheless, it is seen as a good indication for the scope of this project,
and it works well with the fourteen days simulation time as explained in section 4.1.2.
4.3.2.2
RES forecast assumptions
RES forecasts are included in the model for both PV and WTG. The forecasts help the
system to prepare and manage for the available energy from the RES input. In this thesis
the forecasts are considered to predict one hour ahead with no error. This assumption is
included to keep the RES inputs simple. In future work the forecast could include the
potential impact of uncertainties in the forecasts on the system. Additionally, a 0.1%
threshold for RES energy within the next hour is implemented. This means that if the
rated power capacity of the RES is 60 kW, then the energy delivered from the RES is only
considered useful if the forecast of the next hour is larger than 60 Wh. This is included due
to small power value fluctuation in the RES profiles, especially for PV during the night
time. The RES forecasts are explained in further detail in section 4.3.3.3.
4.3.2.3
RES cost assumptions
The RES cost estimations for PV and WTG are subject to a number of assumptions in
this thesis due to the global situation, available data, and since it is desired to estimate
future costs.
The RES costs for installation and O&M are based on 2020 estimations. This assumption
is included because of the financial fluctuations at the given time due to Covid-19 and
Russian invasion of Ukraine. The PV cost is assumed to be the average of PV cost in five
European countries. This assumption is considered the best possible estimation, based
on the available data [18]. Furthermore, the WTG costs are based on larger systems and
assumed to have the same kW-price for smaller systems. This assumption is based on
the fact that for larger WTG systems, costly system components as transformers would
be required. These components are not needed to connect it to the HPC station. The
65
==PAGE:86==
4. System Configuration
decreased cost per kW for large scale systems is therefore assumed to be the same as the
increase in costs for smaller system components. The RES costs for both PV and WTG
are described in detail in section 4.3.3.4.
The 2030 predictions are included to give an indication of how the costs of PV and WTG
systems will develop in the near future. They are based on predictions and will as a
natural consequence hereof be subject to uncertainties as described in section 4.3.3.4.
The uncertainties are present in both the cost estimations and the 2030 predictions are
considered acceptable for this thesis’ investigation of the potential benefits of connecting a
RES to the HPC station. These costs can be found in Table 4.1.
4.3.3
Changes made to 3 string model without RES
This section presents the changes made from the 3S2C as presented in section 4.2 to
the R3S2C. The main considerations are regarding the RES profiles, costs, and forecasts.
Finally, also changes to the EMS will be explained. In this thesis only the output of the
RES generation will be considered since this data is available, meaning that the internal
losses etc. will not be described.
4.3.3.1
Photovoltaic power profile
This section will describe the PV RES input for the simulation model. The RES input
serves as an additional way to import energy to the BESS. Adding RES to the 3S2C
system enables energy import to the BESS limiting the cost of importing energy from
the grid. Furthermore, in situations where maximum one string is in use for charging
EVs, the BESS can be charged faster with power from both grid and RES. Therefore, the
benefits of deploying RES is interesting to investigate from an economical and performance
perspective, to see if the investment of RES input for the system is financially sensible. The
installed PV system in Bornholm is assumed to be the reference RES size for this thesis’
investigation. The PV data considered in this thesis is provided by the supervisors and is
based on 1 sec measurements from a PV system located on Bornholm. This is presented in
Figure 4.9.

the PV data from Bornholm and investigate if the difference in PV and WTG seasonal
and daily distribution play a significant role. The low WTG data are scaled to match the
energy level of the PV data for the chosen dates based on the a scaling factor (SF). The
values for energy levels of PV and WTG are presented in Appendix H, resulting in a SF of
SF =
EPV,avg
EWTG,avg
= 2688.35 kWh
1088.27 kWh = 2.47
(4.1)
where EPV,avg denotes the average energy of the four chosen data time series for PV and
EWTG,avg denotes the average energy of the four chosen data time series for WTG. This
enables the scaling of the WTG data as per Equation 4.2.
PWTG,scaled = PWTG · SF = 10 kW · 2.47 = 24.7 kW
(4.2)
The scaled energy values for WTG inputs can be found in Appendix H.
70
==PAGE:91==
4. System Configuration
Scaling to match energy rather than power is chosen to represent the sizing choice of a RES
for the HPC station, which is likely to be based on how much energy can be provided to the
system, rather than the nominal power rating. If both units have the same power rating,
they most likely will not be reaching nominal powers for equally long period, resulting in
different overall energy productions. Therefore, scaling by energy will also be easier to
compare in the sizing choice of a RES for the HPC station.
Appendix H shows that the differences between the chosen days in the seasons for WTG
are relatively low. The maximum difference between seasons is a 54% energy difference
between summer and spring. However, this difference is sensitive to the chosen days.
The difference between summer and winter for PV is a factor fifteen for the days chosen.
Therefore, and because of the uncertainty of the obtained data, only the average value will
be used as input for the WTG, excluding potential seasonal biases as shown in Figure 4.14
where the x-axis notes the end of the respective day. Fall is the season that has the energy

4.3.3.3
RES generation forecast
Forecasts are included for both PV and WTG inputs. The forecasts for the RES inputs
serve to predict at what time the strings in the BESS should not be fully charged to enable
improved utilization of the RES energy. This can be beneficial to both lower import energy
costs and ensuring more green energy being used by the EV chargers. The forecasts are
assumed ideal and available one hour ahead, which results in the BESS having one hour to
achieve the desired energy state. The assumption that the forecasts are ideal is made to
simplify the implementation of the forecasts as well as investigating the best-case scenario
71
==PAGE:92==
4. System Configuration
benefits. Ongoing improvements in RES forecasting means that this assumption is not far
fetched either [67].
Since the power values are fluctuating in the RES inputs, the one hour forecast uses energy
for prediction. Figure 4.15 shows the RES forecast along with the RES power, and an
indicator for when the RES power is considered an usable input to the model in.
