threshold of 0.1% energy for one hour or with no threshold. The threshold is implemented
to neglect low energy values. The impact on how much energy the system will obtain from
the RES due to the threshold is neglected as it is at approx. 1% in the worst case scenario,
which is the winter season for PV as shown in Figure 4.15. Comparably, for the summer
period for PV and the average value for the WTG, the differences are approximately 0.02%.
4.3.3.4
Renewable Energy Sources cost estimations
This section contains cost information associated with connecting either PV or WTG to
the HPC station. The costs include the system installation costs as well as the O&M
of the system. To achieve high accuracy, the cost estimations of the RES need to be
estimated from project to project, as they are influenced by the location installed and
market tendencies. Available data mostly reflect utility sized systems in the MW range
[18]. Since the power size of the RES connected to the HPC station is in the low end of the
kW scale, this may introduce lower accuracy in the estimation. Even though uncertainties
are present, the RES costs have been included in this thesis to present an estimate and
show the influence on the overall economy in scaling the system.
72
==PAGE:93==
4. System Configuration
In 2020 the global COVID-19 virus outbreak influenced the world. The global economy has
been highly affected, which continues to have an influence on the PV and WTG system
costs. Furthermore, the recent Russian invasion of Ukraine have already had a large impact
on the global economy and the impact in the nearest future is still too early to assess.
The investment cost estimates for PV and onshore WTG have continuously decreased
from 2015 to 2020. The cost estimate in 2020 was 80% of that of 2015 for onshore WTG
and half that of 2015 for PV. But the cost estimates rose to 91.5% of the 2015 value
for WTG in 2022 and to 53.3% of 2015 for PV. However, the expected tendency is that
the cost will start decreasing again from 2023 [2]. This thesis will use economical values
from 2020 to estimate the costs of including RES to the HPC station. This assumption
is based on the fact that the cost tendency in 2021 and 2022 deviates from the expected
behaviour and that the cost estimates for 2023 suggest that the tendency prior to these
years will continue afterwards. The added costs to PV and WTG systems are because of
the large cost increase in many raw materials e.g. steel, polysilicon, copper, and aluminium.
Furthermore, the freight prices rose almost five-fold [2].
Photovoltaic costs
The cost estimation of installing a PV array to the HPC station is based on a total
installation cost. The total installation cost covers the entire system costs incl. module
costs, balance of system, installation etc. Since the PV array for the HPC station is
assumed to be in the kW-range, the estimated total installation cost values for commercial
systems (<1 MW) are considered instead of the cost for utility systems (>1 MW). The
commercial system are more expensive per kW than that of the larger utility scale, due
to the savings associated with large scale installations [18]. Since no country-specific
total installed cost values have been found for Denmark, the average of the total installed
costs from the available European countries in Ref. [18] is used for the PV system as in
Equation 4.3.
ΛPV,sys =
P ΛPV,sys,country
ncountries
= 1296 + 1092 + 1026 + 816 + 1485 EUR/kW
5
= 1143 EUR/kW
(4.3)
where ΛPV,sys denotes the estimated total installed cost for PV, ΛPV,sys,country denotes the
country specific total installed cost for PV, and ncountries is the total number of considered
countries. The yearly O&M costs related to the PV system are estimated based on that
of a utility-scale plant in US, since it was not possible to find these data for commercial
power range. The estimated O&M costs vary in the range between EUR 9.59/kW/year
and EUR 17.27/kW/year. The largest value in the estimated range is chosen to account
for some of the higher costs per kW for systems in commercial range. The O&M costs per
kW is higher for smaller PV systems since the main contributing factors are preventive
maintenance and module cleaning [18].
73
==PAGE:94==
4. System Configuration
Wind turbine generator costs
For estimating the costs of installing a WTG to a HPC station, the total installation costs
are used. This value includes both turbine, tower, electrical installation etc. The regional
costs have an influence on the estimated price, since the weighted-average total installed
cost for onshore wind in 2020 ranged from around EUR 996-3059/kW. So effectively a
factor of 3 in difference, solely based on the region. The total installed costs in Denmark is
chosen, which was approximately EUR 1800/kW in 2020 [18]. This value is representative
for large scale systems. However, it is considered acceptable for the kW range WTG for
the HPC station in this thesis. This is because, even though the large scale systems will
have savings per kW related to the larger size, the smaller turbine for the HPC station
will not have to include costly collector systems or system components as transformers.
For future work this estimate should be validated.
As for the system costs, the O&M costs of the WTG are also based on country-specific data.
Similar to the PV O&M costs, WTG O&M costs data are only valid for larger systems.
This value may therefore be in the low end, since larger turbines reduce the number of
turbines for a given capacity, which lowers the O&M costs. Therefore, more in-depth
investigations should be conducted for the O&M costs in a future HPC station with WTG
connected. The latest value available for Denmark is approximately 46 EUR/kW/year in
2019 [18]. This is chosen for the O&M costs of WTG for the investigations in this thesis.
Predictions for RES costs in 2030
As for the HPC system costs in section 3.4.5.1, a 2030 projection is included to investigate
the differences in the costs associated with installing RES at present date or in the future.
The projections are based on the predictions in Ref. [27] and values consider past inflation
rates in the Euro-zone. It assumes that at 2030, the costs related to PV decrease by 46.5%
and for WTG decrease by 13.7%. A summary of the economical values for RES connected
to the HPC station are presented in Table 4.1

changing prices. Nevertheless, the presented values in this section serve to give a good
understanding of the economical influence it will have to install a RES to the HPC station,
both for the present date and 2030. Additionally, these values will also impact the potential
differences between installing a PV or a WTG to the HPC station. This will be investigated
in section 6.2.
4.3.3.5
Energy management system
The majority of the EMS functionalities for the R3S2C model with RES are the same as
the 3S2C model. The main difference for the model including RES is the possibility of
exporting energy to the grid when RES power is produced. The RES power production is in
this thesis assumed sufficient if the one hour RES forecast is larger than 0.1% of the energy
delivered at rated RES power. For instance if a PV array with a rated power of 60 kW on
one hour average is generating more than 60 Wh then the RES power is assumed sufficient
to be harvested. This is implemented to limit the amount of system reconfigurations, since
small power variations close to 0 kW is present as shown in Figure 4.15. If a large RES
power threshold is chosen, less energy is harvested from the RES but a larger power is
provided to inject energy faster to the system to charge EVs and vice versa. To limit the
impact of using a RES threshold an energy export SOC limit (SOCexp-lim) is implemented
to only allow grid export if all available strings3 are between a specified SOCexp-lim and
the upper SOC operational limit as shown in Figure 4.16. To export energy to the grid
the RES forecast must be above the RES production threshold of 0.1%.
The decision of the magnitude of SOCexp-lim is a trade off between expected RES production
and potential EVs arriving. If the SOCexp-lim is close to the upper SOC operational limit,
many system reconfigurations will be present when exporting energy to the grid. On the
other hand, this will also result in more available energy to potentially charge EVs and vice
versa. To keep things simple and to limit the compromises of the two system functionalities,
a SOCexp-lim of 0.8 pu is chosen in this thesis. The area of allowing energy export to grid
is marked with the green area in Figure 4.16. When exporting energy to grid, the inverter
is matching the RES power production. This is assuming that the inverter knows exactly
how much power the RES is generating at a given time, which in reality comes with a
delay. A detailed flowchart of the extra EMS functionalities for the R3S2C can be found in
Appendix E. This concludes the EMS decision making when exporting energy to the grid.
To ensure as much energy harvested is from the RES as possible, charging the BESS
from the RES will be prioritized. Therefore, if the RES power production is above the
threshold and strings are available, the RES will always charge the BESS from the RES.
Since charging EVs is still a high priority, the highest SOC string is always charged first to
be able to provide energy to the EVs. So in case of multiple strings available the decision of
which string to charge from which sources need to be defined. Therefore, to decide whether
3Available strings are defined to be strings not used for EV charging.
75
==PAGE:96==

Figure 4.16. Overview of EMS decision making based on SOC levels for the "three strings two
chargers and RES" model.
the highest SOC string is charged from grid or RES, one hour RES forecast, RES rating,
and inverter rating are used as shown in the blue area in Figure 4.16. If the average power
of the one hour RES forecast is larger than the inverter rating, then the highest SOC
string is charged from the RES and other potentially available strings are being charged or
discharged from the inverter and vice versa. This concludes the priority of how the EMS is
deciding which string to charge in the R3S2C model.
To verify the functionalities of the EMS for the R3S2C model, an illustration of the
EV power, string power, and string SOC from the Matlab Simulink model is plotted in
Figure 4.17. The setup is configured to have three strings of 100 kWh each, a grid inverter
of 75 kW, and a PV with a rating of 60 kW. It should be noted that negative power is
charging the BESS and positive is discharging the BESS. The RES power is following a
day in June.
From 11:30 to approximately 12:25 the SOC and string power are found to fluctuate,
which is because the SOC of all strings are between the SOCexp-lim and upper SOC limit,
which can be seen in the bottom plot of Figure 4.17. When energy is exported to grid the
inverter is found to match the RES power as seen in the middle plot of Figure 4.17. At
approximately 12:25 an EV arrives at charger 1 with a power request of approximately
100 kW. Therefore, string 1 connects to the EV since this is the available string with
highest SOC. Here string 2 is exporting energy to grid matching the RES power and string
3 is importing energy from RES. At approximately 12:40 string 3 reaches the upper SOC
limit and takes over the charging of EV 1. String 2 has the highest SOC of the remaining
available strings and is prioritized to be charged from grid since the power level of the
grid is larger than the RES. String 1 is charged from RES until string 2 reaches the upper
SOC limit at approximately 12:50. Shortly after, the EV at charger 1 disconnects and the
inverter is charging the highest SOC string with full inverter power which in this case is
string 1. The remaining available string, string 3, is then charged from RES. The inverter
power rating is larger than the one hour RES forecast, therefore the highest SOC string

4.4
Chapter summary
All in all, the chapter has given an overview of the different configurations in this thesis’
investigation of the sizing of the system components in HPC stations by sensitivity analysis.
To give an overview, the overall system topologies have been presented for all the system
configurations investigated in this thesis. Furthermore, the relevant assumptions for each
of the system configurations have been explained to highlight the choices made and the
uncertainties they present. Lastly, differences in system configurations have also been
emphasized.
77
==PAGE:98==
5
MONTE CARLO SIMULATION
To obtain the study results in this project, Monte Carlo simulation will be deployed
on the models developed. The Monte Carlo simulation is basically running the same
simulation over and over again, repeatedly sampling from probability density distribution
functions to compute the statistics of interest [68]. The purpose is to mimic the operation
of complex systems, which in this case includes the randomness of the generated EV profiles
presented in section 3.3. This means that the extreme scenarios for power demand and
charging duration will also be included in the sizing of the HPC station system components
sensitivity analysis, even though they have a low probability to occur. Monte Carlo
simulation is favorable for this project, since the problem is hard to analytically solve and
experimentation is too time-consuming and immensely expensive. The results obtained
from the simulations are not exact results as they build on a randomly generated input.
Therefore, the Monte Carlo simulation is compute resource heavy, as a large quantity
of simulations are required to reflect the reality with satisfactory level of confidence.
Furthermore, it must be stressed that the Monte Carlo simulation results will only be as
good as the model and the inputs used. Therefore it is important that considerations have
been put into determining the inputs, as seen in section 3.3, 4.3.3.1, and 4.3.3.2, as well as
the modelling as seen in chapter 3.
An explanation of the success rate as one of the main outputs considered will first be given
in section 5.1. Secondly, the hierarchy of the parameters that are investigated will be
presented in section 5.2, followed by a description of the implementation of the Monte Carlo
simulation in section 5.3. Lastly, the number of simulations required to self-approximate
will be presented and discussed in section 5.4.
5.1
Success rate
To have tangible success criteria for the HPC station system component sizing sensitivity
analysis, success rates on both micro- and macro level are implemented as a measurable
result from the model. These are important to include to present a limit for how well the
system operates when sizing the HPC station system components. Alternatively, e.g. if
only the economy of system was measured as a criterion of success, the best solution could
be one that only had a low chance of actually charging the vehicle when arriving at the
HPC station. Naturally, this would not be a product EV users would use and therefore
not desirable to install economically either. This section will describe both the micro- and
macro success rate, where micro success rate (MISR) refers to the individual EVs level, and
macro success rate (MASR) refers to the population of EVs arriving at the HPC station as
displayed in Figure 5.1.
78
==PAGE:99==
5. Monte Carlo Simulation
Macro success rate
Micro
success rate
75%
99%
100%
Figure 5.1. Micro- and macro success rate
5.1.1
Micro success rate
MISR shown in Figure 5.1 covers the threshold of which the individual charging session is
considered successful. E.g. if the MISR threshold is set to 95% and the energy demand for
the charging session is 100 kWh, the session will only be considered successful if minimum
95 kWh is charged before the charger disconnects. It is implemented by comparing the
requested- and delivered energy for the charging session as showed in Equation 5.1.
MISR = Edel-session
Ereq-session
· 100
(5.1)
where MISR denotes the micro success rate, Ereq-session denotes the requested energy for
the session, and Edel-session denotes the delivered energy for the session. If the MISR is
higher than then defined MISR threshold, "XX", it counts as a successful session as shown
in
sessionsuccess,XX =



count + 1
MISR ≥XX
MISR < XX
(5.2)
where sessionsuccess,XX denotes the number of successful sessions for XX threshold. E.g.
if XX = 95% this means that all individual charging session with 95% or more charged
energy will be counted as successful sessions.
5.1.2
Macro success rate
MASR is an output that compares how many charging sessions have been ended to how
many of them where successful to a specified MISR threshold, XX, as in Equation 5.3.
MASRXX = sessionsuccess,XX
sessionsended
· 100
(5.3)
where MASRXX is the number % of charging sessions considered successful, XX refers
to the % threshold set for the MISR in Equation 5.1, sessionsuccess,XX is the number of
successful sessions considering XX MISR threshold, and sessionsended is the total amount
of ended sessions in the total EV population arriving to the HPC station as shown in
Figure 5.1. It is important to note that the MASR is not a part of the Monte Carlo
simulation method, as this is simply an output. It would therefore also be present if the
79
==PAGE:100==
5. Monte Carlo Simulation
simulation was performed once. The description of it is included here, since it is the main
output tracked from the Monte Carlo simulation.
By the use of the MISR and MASR the Monte Carlo simulation can compute the economy
for a specified charging success rate. This effectively means that for the objective of this
thesis, which is sizing of the components in HPC stations, the system needs to fulfill a
minimum requirement of operation quality for the EV customers. Both the MISR and
MASR thresholds can be lowered to create a system that is potentially going to be less
expensive to build, but results in lower chance for the arriving EV customer to have a
successful charging session. Or it can be set to a higher and more conservative value, which
on the other hand results in a higher chance for successful charging session, but also a
potentially more expensive system. The MISR and MASR values will be explained for the
results presented throughout this thesis.
5.2
Hierarchy of parameters to investigate
Since this thesis’ HPC station component sizing sensitivity analysis includes multiple
changeable parameters, it is desired to establish a hierarchy for the investigations. By
including a sweep of each of the parameters in the hierarchy it is possible to find the local
optimum combination of component sizing, taking into account the economy and input
scenarios.
The reasoning behind the hierarchy is simply; The parameters with largest impact will be
included in most scenarios as shown in Table 5.1. It should be noted that only two steps
are shown for each parameter in the table to simplify the illustration. In the actual Monte
Carlo simulation, more steps will be investigated.
Dist 1

nEVs 2

Table 5.1. Monte Carlo simulation parameters for investigation hierarchy
In the table above nsim denotes number of simulations, nEVs denotes the number of EVs
per day in the different scenarios, capkWh-bat denotes the storage capacity of the BESS in
the given scenario, invrating denotes the rated power of the inverter, and Dist denotes the
EV distribution investigated. The table includes the parameters swept for the base case.
This will be upgraded to also include RES scaling for the R3S2C topology.
The number of EVs are present in all parameter sweeps to determine the optimum sizing
of the HPC station components depending on the amount of EVs estimated to arrive a the
HPC station. Hence, it is included in the low end of the hierarchy. Next in the hierarchy is
the BESS size, since this is the most expensive component in the setup and it determines
the amount of energy possible to store in the system. Hereafter, the inverter rating is
included, since this will also have an impact on the price and performance of the system.
80
==PAGE:101==
5. Monte Carlo Simulation
Lastly, the EV distributions are included, as these can be investigated to set different stress
scenarios for the EV profiles.
5.3
Implementation of Monte Carlo simulation
This section will briefly describe how the Monte Carlo simulation has been implemented
for the sizing sensitivity analysis investigation. A flowchart showing the Monte Carlo
simulation process is displayed in Appendix I taking into account the hierarchy presented
in section 5.2. The input variables are initially loaded and determines the parameter
scenarios to be investigated for EV fleet distribution, inverter rating, battery capacity,
number of EVs, and number of simulations for each scenario. After the input variables
are loaded, the parameters are chosen according to the parameter hierarchy shown in
Table 5.1; first the EV fleet distribution is chosen, then the inverter rating followed by
the battery capacity. Lastly, the number of EVs is set. Subsequently, the required inputs
for the simulation are defined along with generating the random EV time series profile
based on the number of EVs and the EV fleet distribution. When the battery capacity and
EV loops for a specific inverter rating have been completed, the simulation parameters
will be momentary stored and distributed to the available cores and simulations will be
executed accordingly. Then the next parameter in the inverter loop is chosen and a new
set of simulations are performed. This specific combination of setting up the simulation
variables and running the simulations have been chosen as a balance between execution
speed, available memory, and cores.
In this way, the different parameters are nested within each other, which enables significant
amount of parameter scenarios to be investigated for the sensitivity analysis sizing of the
HPC station components. After all the parameter intervals are swept, the outputs from
the simulations are stored in a table and saved for later data processing. This concludes
the Monte Carlo simulation flow.
To explain the scale of the amount of simulation conducted, an example is here presented.
The distribution of the EV fleet is kept to a single value (18% small EVs, 69% medium
EVs, and 13% large EVs). The inverter rating is swept through three values: 40 kW, 70 kW,
and 100 kW. The battery size is also swept through three values: 50 kWh, 100 kWh, and
150 kWh. Lastly, the number of EVs are swept through three different values: 5 EVs/day,
10 EVs/day, and 15 EVs/day. Each scenario is simulated 10.000 times in this example.
This will generate a total number of simulations of:
nsim-tot = nDist-steps · ninv-rating-steps·
ncap-kWh-bat-steps · nnEVs-steps · nsim
= 1 · 3 · 3 · 3 · 10.000
= 270.000
(5.4)
where ntextsim−tot denotes the total number of simulations, nDist-steps, ninv-rating-steps,
ncap-kWh-bat-steps, and nnEVs-steps denote the scenarios for the different parameters, while
nsim are the number of simulations for each scenario. Equation 5.4 shows that vast amount
of data will be investigated in this project with the help of Monte Carlo simulation of
nested parameter sweeps. This is especially the case when higher resolution and thereby
81
==PAGE:102==
5. Monte Carlo Simulation
more steps are wanted for the swept parameters. To speed up this process the Monte Carlo
simulation are executed through DTU’s High Power Compute cluster.
5.4
Number of simulations required
As shown in Equation 5.4 the number of simulations for each scenario plays an important
role in the required compute power. Therefore, to limit the compute time and consequently
simulation time, this section will investigate the amount of simulations required to obtain
satisfactory results using Monte Carlo simulation for this thesis.
Obtaining satisfactory results using Monte Carlo simulation requires running a significant
amount of simulations due to the randomly generated input.
A few random based
simulations will give a few results which are also displaying correspondingly random
behaviour. However, since Monte Carlo simulation is making statistic on event observations
in large number of random simulations, the law of large numbers comes into play. In
theory this basically means that if the simulations are performed a certain amount of times
with the independent and identically distributed sequence of the randomly generated EV
profiles, the cumulative mean mn will at some point establish itself around the expected
value. The standard deviation, σ will be be smaller and smaller as well for larger number
of simulations, n with the factor √n. In practice it might occur that with a larger number
of simulations the mean is actually farther from the expected value than with a smaller
number of repetitions. In this case however, then it is bad luck, because the odds are in
favor of a mean closer to the expected value, with more simulations [69]. The uncertainty
of the results will be further discussed later in this section.
All in all, this underlines a key property of the Monte Carlo simulation: The simulations
are self-approximating. This means that the extreme cases with low probability will seldom
appear in the results [70]. The extreme scenarios can be investigated by displaying the
results as box plots, which includes both quartiles, median, and the extreme observations.
The accumulated mean of the success rate of vehicles charging at the station output is
used to show the number of simulations to convergence closely to the expected mean. The
accumulated mean is calculated as
mn = 1
n
n
X
i=1
xi
(5.5)
where mn is the accumulated mean at n number of simulations. xi is the statistical sample
of n different values. This is continuously being calculated throughout the number of
simulations.
From this the variance can be calculated as
σ2
n = 1
n
n
X
i=1
(xi −mn)2
(5.6)
where σ2
n is the variance at n number of simulations. As explained earlier, when the number
of simulations increases, the theory state that mn and σ2
n will move closer to the exact
values for m and σ2.
82
==PAGE:103==
5. Monte Carlo Simulation
The central limit theorem states that the sample means’ distribution approximates a
normal distribution when the sample size of independent random variables gets larger,
disregarding of the population’s distribution. This enables the calculation of the confidence
interval [m −e, m + e] at α of the estimated mn with the length of the interval equal to 2e.
e95%,n = 1.96 ∗σn
√n
(5.7)
where e95%,n is the 95% confidence interval, 1.96 is the α value at the 95% confidence
interval, and σn is the standard deviation at n simulations. This mean that mn has a 95%
chance of being in the interval [m −e95%,n, m + e95%,n] [70]. The calculation is initially
done with the base case values from Ref. [3]. The distribution of the EV fleet is 18%
small EVs, 69% medium EVs, and 13% large EVs. The inverter rating kept at 66 kW. The
battery size is set to 103.68 kW. Lastly, the number of EVs is set to a non-extreme value
of 10 EVs/day that neither stresses the system immensely, nor is unrealistically low.
Firstly, three simulations are repeated one thousand times in Figure 5.2a and Figure 5.2b
to confirm that the random input of the simulations is actually behaving randomly.

Figure 5.3 shows that the MASR is highest for 5 EVs/day as expected, as this will be
a less stressed scenario than that of 10 EVs/day and 15 EVs/day. From Figure 5.3 it is
hard to see how quickly the simulation repetitions converge towards the expected mean, µ.
Therefore a zoom in on the first 10.000 values is shown in Figure 5.4a along with the error
value from Equation 5.7 divided with µ in Figure 5.4b.
From Figure 5.4a it can be seen that the confidence interval quickly decreases in the first
initial number of simulations. The size of the confidence interval continues to decrease
when the number of simulations increases, but quite slowly. This is because the number of
simulations has to be multiplied by four to divide the confidence interval by two, as can
also be seen from Equation 5.7 [70]. To investigate this in more depth, Figure 5.4b shows
the error divided by the final cumulative mean value at 50.000 simulations, noted as the
expected mean, µ. This plot shows that in order to get a 95% confidence interval with
less than 0.1% deviation from the expected mean, the simulations need to be repeated
approximately 5.000 times. The 0.1% is chosen as a conservative compromise between
required number of simulations and good accuracy of the output values. The accuracy
in this project needs to be good enough to clearly define difference between the different
scenarios investigated. To be on the safe side and counter changes in the inputs and other
model topologies, 10.000 simulations for each parameter sweep will be used in this thesis to
solve the objective of HPC station component sizing sensitivity analysis for different cases.

5.5
Chapter summary
This chapter has explained how the Monte Carlo simulation has been used to obtain results
from the vast amount of simulations required in this project. The MISR and MASR
have been explained to clearly define the investigated outputs. Secondly, the hierarchy
of the parameters that are investigated was presented giving an understanding of the
process, followed by a description of how the the Monte Carlo simulation is implemented.
Lastly, the number of simulations required for the Monte Carlo simulation in this thesis to
self-approximate has been decided. Conclusively, 10.000 simulations will be performed for
each scenario investigated to ensure the reliability of the results.

SIMULATION RESULTS
This chapter presents the results of the Monte Carlo simulation. The simulation output is
analysed and discussed for the different investigated configurations. First the structure of
the analysis is described to give an overview.
Structure of analysis
An in-depth explanation of how the vast data are processed from the Monte Carlo simulation
results for the 2S1C model is included in section 6.1. This section defines the solutions
space for the results, which performance metrics are investigated, and the chance threshold
is introduced as a customer satisfaction criteria. The last part of the base model section
presents the way that results will be illustrated and analyses the 2S1C setup results for
the impact of maximum return rate, maximum efficiency, and the hot swap functionality.
Hereafter, the 2S1C setup is compared to the 3S2C setup to determine which system
performs best at different EV penetration levels in section 6.2. Here two comparisons are
shown, the first one comparing the 2S1C setup to the 3S2C setup, whereas the second
one compares two 2S1C setups against the 3S2C setup to represent a comparison between
systems with two chargers. The results for configurations including RES are presented
and analysed in section 6.3. Here the result are presented for the R3S2C setup for PV to
determine if the seasonal bias of solar energy potential influences the scaling of the system.
This section also includes a comparison between the 3S2C setup and the R3S2C setup,
with either PV or WTG. This is to explore the impact of including RES to the system and
if this is economically feasible. The last part of this chapter analyses the potential impact
of differences in prices between 2020 and 2030, changing the EV distribution, or shifting
from C customer to B customer in section 6.4.
In general different EMS types with different functionalities are investigated. An overview
of the EMS types investigated is provided in Table 6.1.

6.1
Base model: two strings one charger
This section presents how the results have been processed for the base model, as explained
in section 4.1. The configuration is a two string setup with a grid connection and a single
EV charger, denoted as 2S1C. The investigated parameter range and step size are presented
in table Table 6.2. The EMS type is 1 as shown in Table 6.1, which is prioritising EV
charging, having a constant SOC reference set to 0.9, and the hot swap is disabled. Hot
swap is initially disabled to show the data processing for the simplest case and allowing for
later comparison between including and excluding the hot swap functionality. The BESS’s
string size is swiped from 50 kWh to 150 kWh in steps of 10 kWh, effectively 11 steps. The
inverter size is swiped from 20 kW to 120 kW in steps of 10 kW, also resulting in 11 steps.
The amount of EVs per day is swiped from 4 to 20 EVs/day in steps of 4 EVs/day, adding
5 steps. As mentioned in the Monte Carlo section 5.4 each setup combination is simulated
10.000 times. This results in 6.050.000 simulations in total.

of feasible solutions, only this will be generated for other investigated setups.

This indicates that a system within the investigated ranges and the used EMS with hot
swap disabled will likely not be able to provide the customer satisfaction criteria presented
in this section for an EV penetration larger than 20 EVs/day with the used EV distribution.
If EV penetrations larger than 20 EVs/day is desired for the HPC station, the investigated
range should be extended.

6.1.2
Performance metrics
With the process of defining the solution space clarified, other system performance metrics
can be investigated within the solution space found in Figure 6.8. For all scenarios in this
section the same customer satisfaction criteria applies: 99% chance of getting 100% MASR
for a 95% MISR.
Different metrics will be monitored to cover various aspects of the HPC station. The
economical metrics will be investigated to determine the best financial combinations of
system components. For battery degradation the mean discharging and charging current
will be presented together with mean SOC and cycles. Lastly, system performance metrics
for the system and battery efficiency will be presented. All these performance metrics will
be specified within the solution space for MASR and MISR found in Figure 6.8.
Since outliers are present in the results the median is used to present the results. Therefore,
the performance metrics will only be used as an indicator of how the system performs and
will not accurately guarantee system performance in extreme scenarios.
6.1.2.1
Economical metrics
The economical perspectives are presented in section 3.4.5. In this section the results of
the economical perspectives will only look at the 2020 system cost estimations and variable
pricing scheme for C customers to show an example of how the values are processed for
all the cases. Initially, the economy for the full system range is presented. Hereafter, the
solution space is narrowed down to be within the specified criteria of 99% chance of getting
100% MASR at 95% MISR.
First, the overall system cost is shown in Figure 6.9. Here the system cost is found to
increase with string and inverter size. The system cost is found to increase more with string
size compared to inverter size, which makes sense considering the estimated component cost
in Table 3.6. It should be noted that the grid connection cost mentioned in Equation 3.12
is also included in the overall system cost.

The profit is based on the income from charging EVs and exporting electricity to the grid

ΛProfit = (ΛEV delivered + Λgrid export) −Λgrid import
(6.2)
Where ΛProfit is the profit in EUR, ΛEV delivered is the income from charging EVs, Λgrid export
is the income from exporting energy to the grid, and Λgrid import is the expenses for importing
energy from the grid. For this case, without RES generation, no export to the grid is
present. The electricity bought from the grid is assumed to follow the variable pricing
scheme from Figure 3.30 for C customers. The median of both the income and expenses are
used. They are then scaled up to ten years to get an idea of the profit over time, assuming
that the electricity price and income for charging EVs are unchanged in the future. This is
visualized for 4 EVs/day in Figure 6.10. The profit is found to increase with larger string
and inverter sizes caused by the larger chance of getting a higher MASR for larger string
and inverter setups as shown in Figure 6.4, because more EVs are being charged in these
cases, thus higher income.

6.1.2.2
Battery Degradation Metrics
A mean SOC value of all strings have been calculated for each simulation. The median of
this mean SOC value have been found and plotted for all the cases within the solution
space in Figure 6.13. The mean of the SOC have been found throughout the 14 day period
the simulation have been run. This means that it will be heavily affected by how the SOC
reference is controlled by the EMS, which for the EMS type 1 in this case is meant to be
kept at 0.9 pu. The SOC is found to decrease with lower inverter sizes, which might be
caused by longer charging time from the grid.

From a battery degradation perspective as mentioned in section 2.2 lower SOC values have
been found to lower the battery calendar ageing. So solely considering the SOC value, one
could think that the cases with higher EV penetration would lower the calendar ageing.
That is in some way true, at least the calendar aging contribution from the SOC will be
limited. That being said contribution from other degradation metrics, such as cycles might
have a more severe impact when increasing the EV penetration.
The cycles are measured for each string and one cycle is counting a full charge and discharge
cycle, so effectively an energy throughput of two times the energy capacity. This is then
counted throughout the simulation execution time of 14 days for each string. Again the

The cycles are found to increase with increasing EV penetration, which is expected since
more energy is demanded. The amount of cycles is also found to increase with decreasing
string sizes within the same EV penetration. This is expected behavior since lower energy
capacity for the same energy demand requires more full equivalent cycles. It should be
mentioned that how the BESS have been loaded during these cycles is not accurately
known, which will have an impact on the cycle degradation itself. But in general to limit
the cycle degradation of the system, lowering the amount of cycles would be beneficial.
The last two battery degradation metrics considered in this investigation are for the currents.
These are measured and averaged for charging and discharging currents throughout the
simulation execution time of fourteen days for values above zero. The units are converted to
per unit with respect to investigated capacity as explained in section 3.4.4. The conversion
to pu is used to compare loading between different capacities. This means that if a
100 kWh=100 Ah2 capacity is investigated, then 1 pu current equals 100 A and for e.g.
75 kWh=75 Ah capacity is investigated 1 pu=75 A etc. Once again the median of the 10.000
simulations for each case is used to investigate the tendency of the currents.
The discharging currents are visualized in Figure 6.15, which is found to increase with
smaller capacities. This tendency is expected since the population of the random generated
EV charging profiles is assumed to be large enough, thus getting the same numerical
loading for each case. Since the pu current is capacity dependent the current is increasing
with decreasing capacities.
The discharging current is found to be independent of the inverter size, which is expected
since the EMS investigated in this scenario is not exporting any power to the grid.
Furthermore, the median average discharging current is found to be independent on EV
penetration and inverter size, which indicates that the population used for generating
random EV charging profiles looks sufficient. It should be noted that this current is not
showing how the actual current is behaving, since charging two EVs requesting the same

6. Simulation Results
6.1.2.3
Efficiency performances
On a system level, the efficiencies are found in the same way as in the model provided from
DTU supervisors. The overall battery and inverter efficiencies are found in the same way, by
calculating the ratio between total energy losses and total energy throughput, respectively.
The overall system efficiency is taking into account the total energy delivered to the EVs,
exported to the grid, and imported from the grid or RES as stated in Equation 6.4. The
system efficiency furthermore takes into account the difference between initial and ending
energy in the BESS.
ηsystem-efficiency = EEV-delivered + Egrid-export + EBESS-end
ERES-import + Egrid-import + EBESS-init
(6.4)
The inverter efficiency is following the same efficiency curves as in Ref. [3]. Since the
EMS in the 2S1C base model is applying full inverter power when in operation, the overall
inverter efficiency will follow the efficiency defined at maximum power from the efficiency
curves in Ref. [3] and will therefore only take one value in all simulations regardless of the
energy throughput, 97.8% to be exact. Therefore, this figure have not been included for
this case3.
The battery efficiency curve is depending on how the system is scaled as explained in
section 3.4.2.2 and visualized in Figure 3.24. The overall battery efficiency is therefore
depending on both the string and inverter sizes. This is because the string charging power
depends on the inverter size and the string discharging power depends on the EV loading.
This is visualized in Figure 6.17.

the larger inverter size, since the EMS deploys full power when charging the BESS from
grid. Vice versa, the highest overall battery efficiency is found for the setup with largest
string size and smallest inverter size. The results in Figure 6.17 are found to overlap. This
indicates once again that the population of generated EV profiles are sufficient.

6.1.2.4
Summing up performance metrics
From these findings some trade off in the design priority must be made between battery
degradation, system efficiency, and economics. Considering the battery degradation and
system efficiency perspectives the best solution will be to choose the largest string size and
smallest inverter size. From an economical perspective it is desired getting as high return
as possible. The best solution within the solution space depends on the defined criteria for
MISR, MASR, and chance and the EV penetration level. Therefore, some performance
design priorities will be considered and compared in a later section.
6.1.3
Customer satisfaction criteria
All the results processed in this section are, until this point, only considering a criteria
with a 99% chance of getting 100% MASR at 95% MISR. These criteria have initially
been chosen to be able to present the results, but have not considered the behavior of
varying the chance threshold, MASR and MISR. Therefore, to show the impact of varying
the criteria, this section will investigate how the maximum return rate varies within the
investigated range. The maximum return rate has been chosen since this give a perspective
in the best available economical solution within the investigated range.
4Found in Figure J.2 in Appendix J
99
==PAGE:120==
6. Simulation Results
The first criteria investigation will look into how the return rate varies when swiping
through the chance threshold at 80%, 95%, and 100% MASR and 90%, 95%, and 99%
MISR. 100% MISR have been excluded since differences to 99% are insignificant for charging
durations lower than 100 min, due to the 60 sec simulation time step used and the 99%
MISR allows for unnoticeable differences between the requested and delivered energy for
the customer. Therefore, the 99% MISR is considered instead of the 100% MISR. A ten
year estimation of the return rate is used as explained in section 6.1.2.1. This is visualized
for 8 EVs/day in Figure 6.19, where the return rate is found to increase when the MASR,
MISR, and chance threshold are decreased.

Figure 6.19. Chance sensitivity of getting 80%, 95%, and 100% MASR at 90%, 95%, and 99%
MISR for 8 EVs/day with hot swap disabled.
When the criteria are slacked, the EVs are not being charged with 100% of their requested
energy, which results in slightly lower profit as seen in Table 6.4. On the other hand,
the solution space is increasing at slacked criteria, resulting in lower system cost5. Since
the relative magnitude difference in lower system cost is higher than difference in profit,
the return rate is increasing. A significantly larger increase in return rate is found when
decreasing the chance threshold from 100% to 99% compared to how the return rate is
developing for the remaining chance threshold investigated. This slightly looser chance
threshold criterion excludes some of the extreme scenarios from the data set, which
decreases the chance of over sizing the system. The return rate is also found to increase
when decreasing the MISR but with a lower magnitude compared to varying the MASR and
chance threshold. This tendency also appears for other EV penetration levels, which can
be found in Figure J.3 in Appendix J. It should be noted that since the chance threshold
is a minimum criteria the actual chance of getting the criteria is slightly higher than the
threshold, this is shown in Figure J.7 in Appendix J.
The solutions, when decreasing the MASR are summarized at 95% MISR and 99% chance
for 8 and 16 EVs/day in Table 6.4. In Figure 6.19 the return rate is found to increase by
approximately 70%-points when the MASR is decreased from 100%(blue) to 95%(orange),
5The system cost is found from a combination of string and inverter size, which can be found in
Figure J.4, J.5, and J.6, respectively in Appendix J.
100
==PAGE:121==
6. Simulation Results
which corresponds to 14%-points/MASR. From 95%(orange) to 80%(yellow) the return rate
is found to increase by approximately 80%-points, which corresponds to 5.3%-points/MASR.
Both at constant MISR and chance threshold. From this, the return rate is found to be
more sensitive to MASR variations for higher values, e.g. from 100% to 95% than from
95% to 80%. The sensitivity is not the same for all the EV levels investigated, which can
be seen in Figure J.3 in Appendix J. Here the return rate is found to be more sensitive
to changing the MASR for larger EV penetrations levels compared to lower. To show
this, Table 6.4 compares the solution, system cost, return rate, and profit for 8 and 16
EVs/day. For an EV penetration level of 16 EVs/day the return rate is found to increase
by 142%-points, when decreasing the MASR from 100% to 95%, which corresponds to
28.4%-points/MASR. From 95% 80% MASR, the return rate increases 102%-points, which
corresponds to 6.8%-points/MASR. Therefore, for 16 EVs/day the gains of decreasing the
MASR from 100% to 95% are 4.2 times as much compared to decreasing from 95% to 80%,
which is 2.6 for 8 EVs/day. This shows that the sensitivity of varying the MASR depends
on the EV penetration level investigated.

From these findings the return rate is found to be more sensitive to varying the MASR and
chance threshold compared to varying the MISR. However, before drawing any conclusions,
the effect of enabling the hot swap functionality will be investigated too. This will be
investigated in the same manner as in Figure 6.19, but instead divided up by the three
MISR levels to get a better visualization of how the hot swap functionality evolves. This is
visualized for 8 EVs/day in Figure 6.20, where larger return rates are found for the cases
with hot swap enabled. The same tendency appears for 4-20 EVs/day in Figure J.8 in
Appendix J.
The increase in return rate when enabling the hot swap functionality is found to be
significantly larger for the cases with 95% and 90% MISR compared to the cases with 99%
MISR. Therefore, if the desired criteria contains 99% MISR, the benefits of deploying the
hot swap functionality is limited compared to 95% and 90% MISR. The higher return rate
for the cases with the hot swap functionality enabled is achieved, since solutions with lower
system cost fulfills the same criteria6.
Summing up the criteria sensitivity analysis, varying the design criteria is shown to have a
significant impact on the return rate of the system. Furthermore, it is shown that choosing
6This is visualized in Figure J.9 in Appendix J.

Figure 6.20. Hot swap comparison for chance sensitivity of getting 80%, 95%, and 100% MASR
at 90%, 95%, and 99% MISR for 8 EVs/day.
the criteria for the system is a balance between customer satisfaction and economical
benefits. Since the customer satisfaction is hard to establish and beyond the scope of
this project, some conservative assumptions will be made, allowing for potential future
fine tuning. For all the cases presented in this section, the MISR is found to increase the
return rate with almost the same magnitude when decreasing from 99% to 95% as when
decreasing from 95% to 90%. As explained in section 5.1, the MISR is the lower limit of
the requested energy per charging session which is considered successful in the MASR.
Decreasing the MISR from 99% to 95% is assumed more acceptable for the customer
satisfaction than decreasing to 90%. Even though higher return rate is achieved for 90%
MISR, a MISR of 95% will be assumed appropriate for the investigations and sufficient for
maintaining the customer satisfaction.
Since the gain of decreasing the MASR is found to be dependent on the EV level and
do not follow a general tendency, the MASR will conservatively be kept at a 100% to
avoid compromising the customer satisfaction. This means that all the customers will
be guaranteed to get at least 95% of their requested energy. Since decreasing the chance
threshold to 99% is shown to have a significant impact on the return rate and excludes
extreme scenarios, 99% chance threshold will be assumed appropriate for maintaining
customer satisfaction. Therefore, the design criteria of having a 99% chance of getting
100% MASR at 95% MISR will be assumed appropriate for maintaining the customer
satisfaction. Choosing these criteria means that it is less likely to oversize the system. The
design criteria are shown to have a significant impact on the economics of the system and
should be kept in mind when sizing such a system.
6.1.4
Performance design priority
With the customer satisfaction criteria defined system performance priorities can be
investigated fulfilling the criteria of having 99% chance of getting 100% MASR at 95%
MISR. The first performance design priority will consider the cases for finding the maximum
return rate within the defined solution space. The second performance design priority
will consider the system efficiency and finding the cases with maximum system efficiency
within the solution space. The solutions and economical metrics for these design priorities

Figure 6.23.a presents the system efficiency which is increased by approximately 1.5%-

increase is due to higher efficiency for a larger string size and lower battery losses when
charging the BESS through an inverter with lower power capacity, which is the case for
the maximum efficiency priority. In Figure 6.23.b the actual chance of getting the defined
criteria is shown. This is to indicate if differences in priorities are resulting in significant
higher chance of achieving the defined criteria. Since the defined criterion of 99% chance
of getting the criteria, no significant difference are present, but if the chance criterion is
wished lower, then this might have an effect. In Figure 6.23.c the median of the average
SOC for the two strings is shown. Since the SOC is a product of the EMS control, which
for this case is trying to keep a constant SOC of 0.9 pu, significant differences are not
present. The average SOC is found to be approximately 0.003 pu less for the case achieving
the highest efficiency, which can be explained by the smaller inverter size. It takes longer
time for a lower power capacity inverter to charge the strings, which on average keeps the
SOC lower compared to a higher power capacity inverter. Figure 6.23.d shows the ten
year equivalent cycles, which is found to be lower for the cases achieving the maximum
8Actual numbers summarised in Table J.1 in Appendix J.
104
==PAGE:125==
6. Simulation Results
efficiency. The lower amount of cycles comes from the higher capacity. Figure 6.23.e and
6.23.f show the strings’ average discharging and charging currents. Both currents are in
pu with respect to their Ah capacities to show how the strings are loaded with respect to
their rated capacities. Both the discharging and charging currents are found to be lower
for the case achieving maximum efficiency for low EV penetrations, which is explained
by the larger string size of 150 kWh for maximum efficiency and 120 kWh for maximum
return at 4 EVs/day. The numerical discharging currents are the same due to the same
EV penetration, but the pu are different because of the variable string sizes. The charging
current varies with respect to a combination of inverter and string size.
From an battery degradation perspective, the degradation indicators for achieving maximum
efficiency are found to be likely to prolong the lifetime of the system compared to the case
achieving the maximum return. How much the lifetime potentially will be changed due to
the different performance criteria, is not considered in this thesis. But from these findings,
the system loading is found to be affected most among the investigated degradation
indicators. Therefore, a more thorough lifetime investigation of how much a difference
in degradation indicators have on the system will be beneficial to proper justify which
performance priority to be chosen.
With respect to the EV charging profiles9, EMS control10, and customer satisfaction
criteria11 investigated in this section, a narrowed range of possible system combinations
can be found based on the desired EV penetration level. The solutions achieving the
maximum efficiency within the solution space is limited by the investigated range and is
only shown to give an indication of which direction the system should be scaled in order to
prolong the lifetime. Therefore, the solutions suggested are values guided by the maximum
return rate curves in Figure 6.21.a and 6.23.b. If longer lifetime of the system is wished,
then the system is suggested to be scaled towards the maximum efficiency curve.
Since Figure 6.21 and Figure 6.21 gives a detailed overview and comparison of different
operation scenarios, only these will be presented and discussed for the cases to follow. The
remaining figures presented in this section will be generated for other investigated cases,
but only highlighted if significant different tendencies are present.

currents can decrease the lifetime of the system.

perspective and can provide the same customer satisfaction criteria with potential system
cost savings of approximately 10 kEUR for the 2S1C system. The battery degradation
indication metrics show that the solution with the maximum return rate with hot swap
enabled will potentially decrease the system lifetime. Since the degradation metrics only
are monitored in this thesis, the hot swap functionality is considered to have positive
impact due to the increase in the return rate. Therefore, the impact of the hot swap
functionality will also be investigated for other configurations.
6.2
Three strings model without RES
This section presents the results obtained from the 3S2C configuration as presented in
section 4.2. The investigated parameter range is the same as in the 2S1C model, summarized
in Table 6.2. The tendency for the customer satisfaction sensitivity analysis has been
found to be the same for the 3S2C model as found for the 2S1C model. Therefore, the
customer satisfaction criteria investigated in this section considers the same values as for
the 2S1C model, 99% chance of getting 100% MASR at 95% MISR. The figures presenting
this tendency can be found in Figure K.1, K.2, K.3, K.4, K.5, K.6, and K.7 in Appendix K.
The results for the 3S2C model have been processed in the same manner as presented
in section 6.1. The hot swap functionality was shown to have positive impact on system
performance of the 2S1C model in section 6.1.5. The same tendency appears for the
3S2C model, which can be found in Table K.2, Figure K.10 and K.11 in Appendix K.
Therefore, the hot swap functionality is included in all forthcoming results presented. The
comparison of the results from the maximum efficiency and the maximum return rate cases
are following the same tendencies for the 3S2C setup, as for the 2S1C setup. Therefore,
the results comparing the maximum efficiency and the maximum return rate have been
attached in Table K.1, Figure K.8, and K.9 in Appendix K. The only major difference is
that no solution within the swept parameter range fulfills the customer satisfaction criteria
for an EV penetration level of 20 EVs/day for the 3S2C model. This may be because the
2S1C setup has two strings per charger, whereas the 3S2C setup only has one and a half
string per charger. Since no clear tendency difference appeared in the before mentioned
cases, the first investigation for the 3S2C setup, will compare the difference between the
maximum return cases for the 3S2C and 2S1C setups. Only the maximum return rate will
be considered in this comparison, since the string size is found to choose the upper limit
of the investigated range for the cases achieving the maximum efficiency. Therefore, the
maximum efficiency cases are found to be biased by the investigated range.

14Actual numbers summarised in Table K.3 in Appendix K
108
==PAGE:129==
6. Simulation Results
has one more string, the average energy demand per string is lower than the 2S1C setup.
This can potentially prolong the lifetime of the 3S2C setup.
A difference between the 2S1C and the 3S2C setups is how the EV charging profiles are
generated. For the 2S1C setup EVs are queued if they arrive at the same time. For the
3S2C setup two EVs are allowed to connect at the same time and only queued if both
chargers are occupied, as explained in section 4.2.3.1. This difference in generation of EV
profiles might have impact on the comparison of these two setups, since this may create
different EV customer experiences.
To investigate this, the momentary average power demand from EVs are compared between
the two generations of EV profiles. This is carried out by generating EV profiles for
4-20 EVs/day throughout a fourteen day period 10.000 times, to reflect the scenario used
in the Monte Carlo simulation. It should be noted that this comparison therefore only
investigates how the inputs to the models are developing. Since the generated EV profiles
are not overlapping for the 2S1C model, the power from these profiles are averaged for
values larger than zero. The same is carried out for the generated EV profiles used in the
3S2C model. Here the momentary power demands have been added from both chargers
and then averaged for values larger than zero. The queuing time and queuing chance are
also determined and compared for the two configurations. The average power demand,
queuing chance and queuing time of the generated EV profiles are visualized in Figure 6.28
for the 2S1C model and the 3S2C model.

1 Charger
2 Chargers
Figure 6.28. Average power demand, queing chance and time comparison for the one charger
model and two chargers models.
The average EV power demand for the one charger EV profiles is found to be constant
at approximately 85 kW independent on the EV level, since EV profiles are not able to
109
==PAGE:130==
6. Simulation Results
overlap, thus not depending on EV penetration level. In the two charger EV profiles, the
momentary average power demand is linearly increasing with the EV level from 88 kW to
104 kW from 4 EVs/day to 20 EVs/day. This is since the chance of multiple EVs arriving
at the same time is larger for higher EV levels. Thus a higher chance of the system
experiencing a larger EV power demand. Therefore, the 3S2C system is found to be loaded
momentary more compared to the 2S1C system. This results in the battery potentially
being depleted more in high EV charging demand periods. It is possible that this is part
of the reason why the 3S2C system does not present solutions for 20 EVs/day.
Furthermore, the chance of queuing is found to linearly increase with EV penetration levels
for both the EV profiles for one and two chargers. For the one charger EV profiles the
chance of queuing is increased from 10% to 50% from 4 to 20 EVs/day, whereas the two
charger EV profiles are increasing from 0.5% to 10%. Therefore, the chance of queuing is
found to increase significantly more for one charger EV profiles compared to two charger
EV profiles. The queuing time is found to increase exponentially from 15 min to 30 min
from an EV penetration level of 4 EVs/day to 20 EVs/day for the one charger EV profiles.
Whereas the queuing time for the two charger EV profiles are increasing from 9 min to 11
min. Therefore, the queuing time for the one charger EV profiles is found to increase more
with EV penetration levels than the two charger EV profiles. The queuing chance- and
time might have significant impact on the customer satisfaction, which are not covered
by the MASR, MISR or chance threshold. Therefore, the 2S1C system is more likely to
achieve the defined customer satisfaction criteria for MASR, MISR, and chance threshold
with an overall smaller solution, compared to the 3S2C system. To be able to compare
the two setups more equally, it is possible in future work to implement a queuing time
limitation in the customer satisfaction criteria.
6.2.2
Three strings two chargers vs. 2x two strings one charger
The findings in Figure 6.28 show that there is a significant difference between the EV
customer experience for same the EV penetration level depending on the configuration.
Therefore, to also compare the configurations from the EV customers’ point of view, taking
queuing chance and time into account, an alternative comparison is proposed in this section.
From the results in Figure 6.27, it is desired to compare the 2S1C and the 3S2C setups
with respect to available chargers for same EV penetration levels, as shown in Figure 6.29a
and 6.29b.
Since the same amount of chargers are available, it will represent the same experience for
the EV customers arriving at the HPC stations, assuming a threshold of e.g. maximum 20%
chance of 20 minutes queuing time is acceptable. Therefore, this comparison is showing
desired scaling considerations for the system, if queuing chance- and time are considered.
However, it is important to notice that since original results from the 2S1C setup are
used, the results obtained have been found based on the EV profiles fed to the individual
system. This means that EVs profiles are not considering both system’s availability, which
may have impacts on the solutions proposed. Nevertheless, this comparison is considered
beneficial to illustrate the scaling of the two respective setups in Figure 6.29a and 6.29b
for the same customer satisfaction for queuing and considering same EV penetration level.
Therefore, based on the results from Figure 6.26 and 6.27, two systems from the solution

Figure 6.30. Solutions and economical comparison between "three strings two chargers" and 2×

This compares the two systems each containing two chargers, thus similar customer
experiences. A significant difference is that the EV profiles of the two systems of 2S1C
are momentary loaded with twice the power compared to that of a single 2S1C setup.
Since the two systems of 2S1C are assumed to operate independently, this comparison
is considered valid. It can be seen in Figure 6.30.c that the two systems of 2S1C are
55 kEUR and 66 kEUR more expensive compared to the 3S2C system for 8 and 16 EVs/day,
respectively. The string size and inverter size of the two systems of 2S1C are lower as
seen in Figure 6.30.a and b, respectively. However, since the there are four strings, two
inverters, and two grid connections present for the two systems of 2S1C, these values will
add up to a system cost higher than that of the 3S2C system. The profit is the same,
which results in 31%-points and 53%-points lower return rate for the two systems of 2S1C
15Actual numbers can be found in Table K.4 in Appendix K
111
==PAGE:132==
6. Simulation Results
compared to the 3S2C system for 8 and 16 EVs/day, respectively.
Conclusively, two different design cases can be considered when also including the queuing
shown in Figure 6.28 as a significant parameter. If a success threshold for queuing is
defined as e.g. less than 20% chance of queuing for less than 20 minutes, these cases
would be: (1) When designing a system for relative low EV penetration (4-8 EVs/day) it
is likely beneficial to invest in a single 2S1C system, as this is the solution that comply
with the assumed success threshold for queuing achieving a 38%-points and 70%-points
higher return rate for 4 and 8 EVs/day, as seen in Figure 6.26.f. (2) If the EV penetration
level is larger than 8 EVs/day and therefore exceed the assumed success queuing threshold
for a single 2S1C system as seen in Figure 6.28, it is most likely best to invest in a 3S2C
system. This results in 31 to 52%-points higher return rate than investing in two systems
of 2S1C, as seen in Figure 6.30.f. One could also argue for building the system with a
single 2S1C for high EV penetration levels, since the return rate is up to 136%-points
higher. However, this would likely lead to unsatisfied customers that would avoid using
the system and therefore consequently leading to loss in profit from charging EVs. The
3S2C setup may also have a longer lifetime than a single 2S1C due to the lower equivalent
cycles, as described in section 6.2.1.
As mentioned earlier it should be noted that the results for this comparison have been
found based on the EV profiles fed to the individual system for the 2S1C setup. Therefore,
the EVs profiles are not considering both system’s availability, which may have impacts on
the solutions proposed. Nevertheless, the comparison shows an important consideration
regarding customer satisfaction that needs to be considered when designing the system.
Consequently, it would be beneficial in future work to validate the tendencies found in
Figure 6.30. This could be done by modelling the two times 2S1C setup with a single EV
profile generated considering the same queuing method as for the 3S2C model. It could
also be beneficial to include queuing chance- and time as customer satisfaction criteria.
6.3
Three strings two chargers model with RES
This section presents the results for the R3S2C setup where RES is connected. Since the
RES is an extra component to the system, an extra parameter sweep is required which can
be seen in Table 6.5. This is the RES rating, which will be scaled with a scaling factor
from 0.6 pu to 1.4 pu in steps of 0.2 pu. The reference RES ratings for PV and WTG are
60 kW and 24.7 kW, respectively as explained in section 4.3.3.1 and 4.3.3.2. Therefore, the
numerical step size and range of the PV and WTG are different. This is to compare the
two sources on an energy level rather than power level. It should be noted that the PV
and WTG are only investigated individually connected to the system. The step size and
range for the other parameters are narrowed to limit the computing time when running
the Monte Carlo simulation as shown in Table 6.5.
In the RES models the EMS is able to import energy from both the grid and the RES.
Furthermore, the EMS considered in the R3S2C model is, beside from charging EVs,
prioritising to import energy from the RES. This means that when only one string is
available, power will be drawn from the RES if the one hour RES forecast is larger than
the threshold of 0.1% of the energy delivered at rated RES power. If the RES production

is not above the threshold, the power will be drawn from the grid through the inverter at
rated inverter power. This enables the EMS to export energy to the grid as explained in
section 4.3.3.5.
Since the RES is implemented, three extra metrics are considered including the RES
rating. These include the amount of energy exported to grid (Eexport) and the system
self sufficiency κ. The self sufficiency in this thesis is defined as how much of the energy
delivered to the EVs are harvested from RES. In Ref. [? ] a simplified formula is found to
calculate the self sufficiency. This formula is shown in Equation 6.5.
κ =
ERES-import
ERES-import + Egrid-import
(6.5)
Where ERES-import is the total energy harvested from the RESs and Egrid-import is the total
energy imported from the grid.
The customer satisfaction sensitivity analysis has also been made for the R3S2C setup
with PV during a summer period. The tendency is found to be the similar to the 3S2C
and 2S1C and is assumed to behave the same for other seasons and WTG. Therefore, the
customer satisfaction criteria are considered with same values as for the 2S1C and the
3S2C setups. The criteria are 99% chance of getting 100% MASR at 95% MISR. The
tendency for the PV summer case for the R3S2C setup can be found in Appendix L.
The energy production is seasonal dependent for PV as explained in section 4.3.3.1.
Therefore, a comparison of the seasonal solutions achieving the maximum return rate and
customer satisfaction criteria will be compared in section 6.3.1. This also includes an
explanation of how the solution for a full year is found. In section 6.3.2 the results from
the R3S2C model with WTG will be presented, which will be compared with the full year
PV solution and the 3S2C model.
6.3.1
With PV
This section will present the results from the R3S2C setup including PV. Results fulfilling
the customer satisfaction criteria are presented for all four seasons, since the potential for
solar energy harvesting is dependent on the seasons as shown in Appendix H. Also the
results for a full year are presented, which are based on all the result data for the four
seasons. This means that on average over a full year, the solutions meet the customer
satisfaction criteria of 99% chance of getting 100% MASR at 95% MISR. If an average of
the seasons for the PV input had been fed to the model instead, this would not include
the outliers present in each seasons. The results for maximum return rate are visualized in

Figure 6.31. Solutions and economical seasonal comparison of the "three strings two chargers"
with PV for max. return rate at different EV penetration levels.
Firstly, it is seen that there are no solutions present for neither 16 nor 20 EVs/day. This
may be influenced by the lower BESS string and inverter range investigated of 5 kWh and
5 kW, respectively17 and larger step size. These changes have been implemented to limit
the computing time when running the Monte Carlo simulation. That no solutions are
present for 16 and 20 EVs/day for the R3S2C, might also be influenced by the changed
’green’ EMS strategy to prioritize RES generation. To investigate the potential impact of
the EMS strategy deployed, Table 6.6 shows how much time only one string is available
during a day. This is shown to highlight the period where the decision is taken by the
EMS between using either the inverter or RES to import power. When more than one
string is available, both of the import options are utilized.
It can be seen in the table, the time only one string is available depends on the EV
penetration level. When the EV penetration level doubles, the time only one string is
available approximately triples. For low EV penetrations there are only short period where
the EMS is limited to one string and therefore chooses the RES to import. This is because
in these cases there are a low chance for two small EVs or a large EV arriving and thereby
occupying two strings. For larger EV penetration levels the chance of occupying two strings
increases, leading to more time that only one string is available. Even though the numbers
are still low compared to a full day of 1440 minutes18 resulting in 0.48-6.36% of the day, it
16Numerical values can be found in Table L.1 in Appendix L
17As shown in Table 6.5
181 day × 24 hours × 60 hours/minute = 1440 minutes

is important to stress that these minutes are most likely to be in periods where the energy
is needed to quickly restore the BESS for imminent arriving EVs. Therefore, it might have
an impact on the system performance and it would be beneficial to compare different EMS
strategies in future work, to determine if other solutions are better suited for purpose.
The different colored graphs in Figure 6.31 represent the seasons to show that scaling
of string and inverter size do not differ significantly depending on seasons, as seen in
Figure 6.31.a and .b, respectively. Therefore, the system cost does not vary notable
depending on seasons either. Figure 6.31.h and .i show that the larger potential for solar
energy harvesting for summer results in highest daily export and self-sufficiency, which
is 184 kWh and 58%, respectively for 4 EVs/day. On the other hand, the daily export
of energy and self-sufficiency values for winter are very low, approximately 14 kWh and
8%, respectively for 4 EVs/day. The amount of harvested RES energy compared to the
available is above 95% for all seasons and seasonal impact is low, as shown in the last row of
Table L.1 in Appendix L. Figure 6.31.g shows that the RES size is chosen as the 36 kW for
all the seasons. Since 36 kW is the lowest value in the investigated PV interval, this could
mean that the interval does not include values low enough to find an optimum size for PV.
The higher system and O&M costs of PV can also be an explanation, questioning whether
it is economically feasible to include the PV to the HPC station in general, considering
the EMS implemented and investigated parameter range. PV values exceeding the upper
investigation range of 84 kW could result in solutions for higher EV penetration levels,
but would most likely also be very expensive and therefore not result in desired return
rates. All in all, this means that the profit and therefore return rate is approximately
5%-points higher for summer than for spring and fall for 4 EVs/day and 7%-points higher
for 12 EVs/day. The increase is small as the export to grid income is from the spot price
of approximately 0.03 EUR/kWh, as described in section 3.4.5.
The battery degradation metric in Figure 6.32.a show that the system efficiency is slightly
higher for seasons with larger potential for solar energy harvesting. This is because of
the lower power transfer from RES compared to the inverter, at which the BESS has
higher efficiency as described in section 3.4.2.2. Also, the inverter will be used less for
importing energy, which results in lower converter losses. Only the output of the RES
generation is considered in this thesis, so the losses in the RES are not included in the
system efficiency as mentioned in section 4.3.3. The equivalent cycles increases for seasons
with larger potential for solar energy harvesting, as seen in Figure 6.32. This is because
more energy is exported to the grid. The larger amount of equivalent cycles adds cycle
degradation for these seasons and potentially lower the lifetime.

6.3.2
Comparison between WTG, PV, and no RES
In this section the results from the R3S2C setup including WTG will be presented. These
will be compared to the previous results for the 3S2C and R3S2C with PV. Only the full
year results are considered for the R3S2C with WTG. This is because the WTG input
is based on an average of the energy for all four seasons, since the seasonal biases in the
energy were comparably much larger for PV, as described in section 4.3.3.2 and shown
in Appendix H. The results for maximum return rate are visualized in Figure 6.33 and
6.3419 for solutions and economical aspects, as well as the battery degradation indications,
respectively. To be able to compare the different cases, these plots include the results from
R3S2C with WTG, R3S2C with PV, and 3S2C.
Figure 6.33 shows that the string size of the system is scaled identically for R3S2C,
independently of the RES being PV or WTG. The inverter size is slightly higher for the
configuration with WTG compared to that with PV, possibly to compensate for the lower
peak power of the WTG compared to PV. The largest difference between the system with
PV and WTG is seen in the different RES size. Both the systems choose the lowest possible
RES size. But since the RES size is determined by the amount of potential available energy,
the WTG is 15 kW and the PV is 36 kW, as shown in Table 6.5 and described in section
4.3.3. As seen in Figure 6.33.c the system cost of the system with WTG is approximately
10 kEUR lower than that of the system with PV. Even though the power specific cost is
higher for WTG (1800 EUR/kW) compared to PV (1443 EUR/kW) as described in section
4.3.3.4, the required size of the PV to acquire the same energy outweighs the lower power
specific cost. This results in an approximately 10 kEUR more expensive system when
including PV compared to WTG, based on the investigated range and EMS strategy.

In Figure 6.33.h and 6.33.i it is seen that extra 4-10 kWh is daily exported and a 1-6%-points
higher self-sufficiency is achieved with the WTG system compared to the PV system, which
is because more available energy is harvested for the WTG system20. The WTG system
can harvest more available energy because a significant amount of production is during
the night time. In this time the strings are unlikely to be blocked from charging arriving
EVs, since the arrival times of EVs are more likely in the daytime as described in section
section 3.3. No important differences are found in the battery degradation metrics in
Figure 6.34, when comparing the system with PV and WTG, respectively. The maximum
return rate solutions have been found with both RES sizes chosen as lowest possible value
in the interval. It is therefore not possible to draw conclusions as to which RES is most
beneficial. For future investigations is it desired to investigate a larger range with higher
resolution of RES sizes for both PV and WTG, to determine if optimum sizes can be
found.
As seen in Figure 6.33.c, when comparing the R3S2C systems for WTG and PV with the
3S2C system, the system costs are 30-65 kEUR higher throughout the entire EV penetration
level range for the R3S2C systems. The extra costs come from the system and O&M
costs of the added WTG or PV system. The slight increase to the profit of approximately
10 kEUR, as a result of the lower import and added exported energy, does not make up for
these higher RES costs. This results in an overall lower return rate of 10-40%-points when
including RES as seen in Figure 6.33.f.
