Figure 6.34. Battery degradation indicator metric comparison between "three strings two chargers"
and "three strings two chargers" with WTG and PV for max. return rate at different EV penetration
levels.
The only noticeable difference found in the battery degradation metrics when comparing
the 3S2C system to the R3S2C systems, is that the 3S2C system have a lower amount of
cycles, as seen in in Figure 6.34.d. This is because the extra energy exported to grid for
the R3S2C systems adds extra cycles. This might result in shorter lifetime for the R3S2C
systems due to battery cycle degradation, as described in section 2.2. The larger currents
in the Figure 6.34.e and f. are not showing the correct behaviour and are therefore not
analysed. The incorrect behaviour is because of the way the average currents have been
calculated, as they are affected by the implementation of RES. The discharging current
will both consider the charging of EVs and the export to grid, which have an impact on
the average calculations. Similarly the charging current will now both consider the import
from grid and the import from RES affecting the average calculations.
It is necessary to consider the difference in the priority of the EMS for the overall conclusion
that the 3S2C system is performing better than the R3S2C systems, resulting in more
economical superior solutions. The EMS prioritises harvesting as much green energy as
possible in the R3S2C systems, as described in section 4.2.3.3 and shown in Appendix E.
The 3S2C EMS prioritises charging as many EVs as possible, as described in section 4.2.3.3
and shown in Appendix C and Appendix D. The difference in the system configurations
means that the R3SC2 systems generally achieves approximately 20-40% self sufficient with
green energy, whereas the 3S2C system needs to acquire all the energy from the grid. As
only some of the overall production added to the grid is from RES [71], the energy used to
charge EVs with the R3SC2 is considered to be less harmful for the environment. Creating
a more environment-friendly system may be a driving factor in the business case investing
in a HPC station. If this is the case the R3SC2 systems can be superior to the 3S2C, even
though they result in a lower return rate. As seen in Figure 6.33.f the return rates for the
R3SC2 systems are close to that of the 3S2C for lower EV penetrations (4-8EVs/day),
with 10-20%-points difference. This means that at these EV penetration levels, the R3SC2
systems present solutions that are adding a high amount of self-sufficiency without lowering
the business case of investment severely. In general the systems achieve a higher return
rate at high EV penetration levels. At 4 EVs/day, the economical feasibility of the systems
is questionable since the return rates are between 93%-99%. A more detailed economical
118
==PAGE:139==
6. Simulation Results
assessment is required to eliminate the uncertainties related to these numbers.
The maximum self-sufficiency possible to achieve for the R3S2C system are 15-20%-points
higher than for the maximum return rate as shown in Figure L.8 in Appendix L. The
values for the RES sizes achieving the maximum self sufficiency cases are chosen as the
highest possible in the investigated range, since this comparison do not prioritise the
best economical solution. Therefore, the maximum self-sufficiency priority lowers the
return rate with approximate 20%-points through the entire EV penetration level range
compared to the maximum return rate priority. These numbers are directly depending on
the investigated range for RES sizes.
The conclusions presented in this section build on the limited investigated RES size range
and resolution, meaning that it is possible that better solutions could be found for the
R3SC2 systems. For future investigation, it would therefore be helpful to increase the range
and resolution of RES sizes investigated, as well as implementing different EMS strategies
to determine the advantages and disadvantages of utilizing the RES in alternative ways.
For future work, a weighted average of return rate and self-sufficiency could be considered
to choose the best suited sizing solution when comparing the R3S2C and 3S2C system.
It is also interesting to investigate the impact of future changes in prices, as described
in section 3.4.5 for the HPC station’s components and in section 4.3.3.4 for RES. This
is compelling as the 2030 predictions are decreasing the price of system components and
especially PV.
6.4
Sensitivity Analysis
This section covers sensitivity cases for the HPC station. These cases include a comparison
between 2020 and 2030 prices, an investigation of the impact of changing the EV distribution
and the economical difference between designing the system as B or C customers.
6.4.1
Sensitivity: 2020 vs. 2030
In section 6.3.1 the results showed no clear benefits or significant different solutions
of deploying a HPC system including RES with the 2020 cost projections, EMS, and
investigated range used. Therefore, this section will investigate the impact when the 2030
cost projections are used. The 2030 price projections for the BESS system are covered in
section 3.4.5 and in section 4.3.3.4 for the RESs. No clear trend in the electricity prices in
the past ten years have been present as discussed in section 3.4.5, except for 2020-2022
due to the global pandemic and ongoing war. Therefore, the electricity prices are assumed
the same for the 2030 projections.
The solutions achieving the highest return rate have been found to be the same for both
the 2020 and 2030 projections, within the investigated parameters, criteria, and EMS
used21. Therefore, all metrics, except for the system cost and return rate, are identical to
the results presented in Figure 6.33 and 6.34. Because the solutions are the same, only the
return rate and system cost will be investigated in this section.
21Solutions to 2020 vs 2030 comparison can be found in Figure L.6.

6.4.2
Sensitivity: EV distribution
This section investigates the potential impact of changing the EV distribution of the
randomly generated EV profiles. The two EV distributions investigated are: EV dist 1
(small EVs: 18%, medium EVs: 69%, and large EVs: 13%) is the distribution considered in
all previous studies in this thesis. EV dist 2 (small EVs: 5%, medium EVs: 69%, and large
EVs: 26%) represents a distribution where small EVs are less likely to use the HPC station.
These are instead substituted by large EVs, resulting in higher stresses on the system.
The system topology investigated is the R3S2C with PV for summer season. The R3S2C
topology with PV has been chosen to cover the impact of the larger charging request,
together with the intermittent power production throughout the day time hours of the
PV. Only the summer season for PV is included, since the simulation time to include
all seasons would exceed the time frame of this project. However, this will still serve as
a good indication for system sizes, since the scaling of the PV system was only slightly
influenced by seasonal biases, as seen in Figure 6.31. Two different EV distributions for

the 3S2C with PV for summer season are included in Figure 6.38 and 6.3922 for solutions
and economical aspects, as well as the battery degradation indications, respectively.

Figure 6.38. Solutions and economical comparison between EV distribution: [0.18 0.69 0.13] and
[0.05 0.69 0.26] for max. return rate at different EV penetration levels and PV during summer.
Solutions are found for EV penetration levels of 4 to 12 EVs/day for the original distribution,
EV dist 1. For EV dist 2 with the higher distribution of large EVs, solutions are only found
for 4 to 8 EVs/day. Larger power and energy are requested due to the larger distribution
of large EVs arriving. This will deplete the battery more than the original distribution.
This results in the system being more stressed. The EV dist 2 will therefore not be able
to find solution within the investigated ranges for the same EV penetration levels as EV
dist 1. Figure 6.38.a and .b show that the solutions for EV dist 2 are generally consisting
of a larger battery size and smaller inverter size, compared to the solutions for EV dist
1. The larger battery sizes are result of the higher energy demands from EVs in EV dist
2. The lower inverter sizes for EV dist 2 are chosen, as these present the best economical
solution that fulfill the customer satisfaction criteria. As seen in Figure 6.38.c, the system
cost is generally higher for the EV dist 2. This results in a system that is 37 kEUR and
19 kEUR more expensive for EV dist 2 for 4 and 8 EVs/day, respectively. The added
cost is a results of the larger string sizes required. On the other hand, as more large EVs
arrive, more energy is delivered to EVs/day for the EV dist 2 case, as seen in Figure 6.38.d.
This results in the ten year equivalent profit increases with 33 and 53 kEUR for 4 and 8
EVs/day, respectively. as seen in Figure 6.38.d. Conclusively, the return rate is found to
be equal or slightly higher for the EV dist 2 compared to EV dist 1. For 4 EVs/day the
return rate is the same for EV dist 1 and EV dist 2, whereas for 8 EVs/day it is 7%-points
22The numerical results can be found in Table L.3 in Appendix L
122
==PAGE:143==
6. Simulation Results
larger for EV dist 2. However, this is only the case for a low EV penetration level range
for the EV dist 2, since no solutions were found for higher EV penetration levels.
The lowest RES size in the investigated range is chosen for both the EV dist 1 and EV
dist 2 cases. The reason for this choice of RES is discussed in section 6.3.1. The exported
energy is lower for the EV dist 2 case compared to the EV dist 1 case. The daily exported
energy is 22 kWh/day lower for 4 EVs/day and 13 kWh/day lower for 8 EVs/day. This is a
result of the higher demand during RES production hours for the EV dist 2 case.

For the battery degradation metrics in Figure 6.39, it is seen that the system efficiency
differs slightly for the two cases. As a result of the higher battery strings, the system
efficiency for EV dist 2 is 0.4-1.3 % higher than the for EV dist 1. The equivalent cycles in
Figure 6.39.d are 269-1001 lower for the EV dist 2 case. This is also a result of the increased
battery size, since more energy is required to equal a full equivalent cycle. This potentially
increases the lifetime of the EV dist 2 system compare to the EV dist 1, considering the
solution achieving the maximum return rate.
All in all, increasing the amount of large EVs in the distribution stresses the system more,
resulting in fewer obtainable solutions. On the other hand, the higher profit for larger
energy demand in EV dist 2 outweighs the increased costs of investing in a system that is
more stressed, even obtaining more economic beneficial solution in one case. Conclusively,
this underlines the importance when designing a system of defining not only the EV
penetration level, but also the charging capabilities of the EV fleet arriving at the HPC
station.
6.4.3
Sensitivity: C vs. B customer
In this section the difference between installing HPC stations as C or B customer is
analysed. Essentially the difference between being a C and B customer is that for B
customers the HPC stations are installed directly on the low voltage side of 10/0.4 kV
transformer, whereas for C customers they are installed downstream the 400 V network
of a 10/0.4 kV transformer. This alters the distribution tariffs and grid connection cost,
as described in depth in section 3.4.5. The difference between sizing the HPC station as
123

B and C customers for the 3S2C and R3S2C configurations is shown in Figure 6.40 for
solutions and economical aspects.

Figure 6.40. Solutions and economical comparison between B and C customers for max. return

Only the system cost, equivalent profit, and return rate are included, as the solutions found
have the same component sizes for B and C customers. The numerical results can be found
in Table K.5 in Appendix L. There are no differences in the system components chosen, as
only the grid connection cost and price of importing energy are affected when comparing the
B and C customers. It can be seen from Figure 6.40.a that the system cost is marginally
smaller for the B customers compared to the C customers of approximately 1 KEUR
throughout all EV penetration levels. This difference is a direct result of the lower grid
connection cost for the B customers. An increase in profit can be seen for all configurations
in Figure 6.40.b This increase of 12-49 KEUR comes from the lower distribution tariff,
which essentially means importing energy from the grid is cheaper. Consequently, the
return rates for all configurations are 4-16%-points higher for B customers compared to C
customers. This highlights that if it is possible to connect the HPC stations directly on
the low voltage side of 10/0.4 kV transformer, this is a better economical solutions. It is
important to notice that this analysis is based on the values from section 3.4.5, which only
represent Danish prices. An investigation should be conducted for the region in which the
system is desired to be installed. This comparison indicates that changing the electricity
price results in noticeable differences in the overall system return rate.
6.5
Chapter summary
This chapter has presented how the Monte Carlo simulation results are processed in this
thesis and the findings have been analysed and discussed for the different investigated
configurations. The hot swap functionality has been found to be economical beneficial in
all investigated scenarios. The 2S1C setup has been compared to the 3S2C setup showing
that for lower EV penetration level, choosing a single 2S1C setup is most like beneficial.
However, if the EV penetration increases beyond a threshold for queuing chance and time,
the results points towards the 3S2C setup being the best solution. Also the results for
configurations including RES have been analysed. They show that the R3S2C setups
generally have be investigated with either a too narrow range of RES sizes, a too low
124
==PAGE:145==
6. Simulation Results
resolution or at an improper reference. Therefore, it has not been possible to conclude
which of the RES solutions that are most beneficial to incorporate. When comparing
R3S2C setups with the 3S2C setup, the return rate is generally lower for the system
including RES, but in investment cases where the environmental impact is important, it
might be beneficial to choose the 3S2C setup. To choose the best suited sizing solution in
this comparison, a weighted average for return rate and self-sufficiency could be considered
in future work.
This chapter has studied different sensitivity cases for the HPC station. First, the potential
impact of differences in prices between 2020 and 2030 were studied, showing that especially
PV systems might be more attractive in the future because of decreasing prices. Secondly,
it was investigated how changing the EV distribution to contain a smaller amount of small
EVs and a larger amount of large EV affect the system. Increasing the amount of large EVs
in the distribution stressed the system more, resulting in fewer obtainable solutions for the
same customer satisfaction criteria. On the other hand, the higher profit for larger energy
demand outweighs the increased costs of investing in a system that is more stressed, even
obtaining more economic beneficial solution in one case. This underlined the importance
of defining not only the EV penetration level, but also the charging capabilities of the EV
fleet arriving at the HPC station. Lastly, comparing the difference between connecting the
HPC stations as B or C customers showed that, if possible the system should be installed
as B customers, since this gives a higher return rate. This comparison also indicated that
changes in the electricity prices result in noticeable differences in the overall system return
rate.
Table 6.7 sums up the return rate for the investigated configurations to give an overview
of the economical feasibility of the different system configurations.

7
CONCLUSION
The objective of this thesis was to determine the optimal sizing of HPC system components
by sensitivity analysis for different configurations. As part of the H2020 INSULAE project
on Bornholm, the system deployed by NSS serves as the reference system for this thesis’
investigations. It consists of a NSS BESS of 311 kWh with reconfigurable battery cells, two
175 kW EV chargers, a 66 kW grid-connected inverter, and a 60 kW PV system.
An existing MATLAB Simulink model of the Bornholm system was extended in this thesis
to enable scaling and include new EMS functionalities. In the development of the model,
BESS sizing based on cell capacity was chosen as the best method to be able to adjust
the BESS size. To simplify the scaling, the thermal model was represented by a 3 kW
constant power buffer assumed to maintain optimal temperature. To compare the economic
performance of different configurations, investment and O&M costs of HPC and RES
systems, variable electricity prices and an average charging fee of 0.56 EUR/kWh were
included in the model. The EMS was upgraded to include power sharing between strings
for high power demands and a hot swap functionality enabling switching between strings
during EV charging.
The EV profile inputs were based on assumptions for arrival time, charging power
and battery sizes for EV, and the distribution of different EV sizes. The EV profiles
were generated based on developed probability distributions to reflect a large number of
predefined and controlled scenarios for the Monte Carlo simulation. The PV and WTG
power production inputs were determined based on one year PV 60 kW data from Bornholm
and one year WTG data from a 10 kW Gaia WTG located at DTU Risø Campus. The
WTG data were scaled to match the annual energy level of the PV data. The inverter
control was changed to charge the BESS with full power when needed. The EMS was
upgraded to share the power between strings if the EV power request was above the string
limit.
Due to the EV profiles being randomly generated, the Monte Carlo simulation was used to
include extreme scenarios. All solutions found were subject to defined customer satisfaction
criteria. These criteria have been chosen to represent conservative, yet realistic design
criteria considering 99% chance that 100% of the arriving EV are charged with more than
95% of their demand. The number of simulations required to get satisfactory results from
the Monte Carlo simulation was determined to be 10.000, aiming for less than 0.1% error
margin.
The results are based on a total of 85.5 million simulations. The extensive amount of data
was summarized clearly and concisely in six to nine metrics plotted for each configuration
for solutions and economical comparisons. Six metrics for monitoring battery degradation
were also included.
127
==PAGE:148==
7. Conclusion
The EV penetration level was concluded to have large impact on the system sizing and
estimated ten year return rate with up to 3.8 times higher return rate for 20 EVs/day
compared to 4 EVs/day. Generally systems with higher EV penetration levels required
larger component sizes increasing the system costs, but they resulted in higher return rate
as a result of higher revenues from charging EV. The hot swap functionality was found to
be economically beneficial in all investigated scenarios, resulting in 5-27%-points higher
return rate.
It was concluded from the results comparing the 2S1C and 3S2C configurations that the
return rate was 38-136%-points higher for the 2S1C setup compared to the 3S2C. The
ten year equivalent cycles for the 3S2C setup were 799-3091 cycles lower compared to the
2S1C setup, potentially prolonging the lifetime of the 3S2C setup.
An important customer aspect not covered in the defined customer satisfaction criteria is
queuing. The queuing time and chance were shown to be significantly larger for the 2S1C
setup than the 3S2C setup, resulting in two different customer experiences. Therefore, the
2S1C and the 3S2C setups were consequently compared with respect to chargers, meaning
one 3S2C and two 2S1C systems servicing the same amount of EVs/day. With a success
threshold for queuing defined as less than 20% chance of queuing for less than 20 minutes,
the 2S1C solutions achieved a 38-70%-points higher return rate for 4 and 8 EVs/day. EV
penetrations larger than 8 EVs/day exceeded the queuing threshold for a single 2S1C,
requiring two 2S1C to meet the queuing criteria. Therefore, the return rate of the 3S2C
solutions were 31-52%-points higher, resulting in more economically beneficial solutions for
the 3S2C for large EV penetration levels.
Results from R3S2C configurations showed solutions for EV penetration levels up to 12
EVs/day. The lower power rating of the RES compared to the inverter for the solutions
resulted in less energy imported to the system. The R3S2C solutions achieving the highest
return rate were consistently obtained with the lowest possible RES size in the investigated
range. Therefore, it was not possible to conclude whether PV or WTG is most beneficial
to incorporate as RES.
To investigate the influence on performance and profitability of including RES to the
system, a comparison was made between the R3S2C systems and the 3S2C system. This
comparison showed an overall lower return rate of 10-40%-points when including RES.
The R3S2C systems generally achieved approximately 20-40% self-sufficiency with green
energy, whereas the 3S2C system required all the energy imported from the grid. The
return rates for the R3S2C systems were close to the 3S2C for lower EV penetrations
(4-8EVs/day), with 10-20%-points difference. Therefore, if the environmental impact is
important in investment cases, the R3S2C presented an acceptable solution without severe
economical compromises, especially for lower EV penetrations.
Cost, EV distribution, and point of connection sensitivity cases for sizing the HPC stations
were conducted. First, the results from a comparison between actual prices for 2020 and
2030 cost projections were analysed to estimate the impact of future prices for system
components and RES. Since the 2030 cost projections for all components are lower, this
resulted in higher return rate for all configurations. Additionally the impact of the 2030
price tendencies resulted in PV systems most likely being more attractive than WTG.
128
==PAGE:149==
7. Conclusion
Secondly, the potential impact of changing the EV distribution of the randomly generated
EV profiles was investigated. A distribution with more large EVs arriving at the HPC
station obtained solutions for fewer EV penetration levels, but had up to 7%-points higher
return rate for the feasible solutions. This underlined the importance of defining not only
the EV penetration level, but also the charging capabilities of the EV fleet arriving at the
HPC station.
Finally, a comparison between C and B customers was included to examine the influence
of grid connection and distribution tariffs. The return rates for all configurations were
4-16%-points higher for B customers compared to C customers. This highlighted that if
it is possible to connect the HPC stations directly to the low voltage side of 10/0.4 kV
transformer, this is a better economical solution.
In conclusion, it was shown that EV penetration level has a large impact on the best
economical solutions. The 2S1C configuration was economically favorable for lower EV
penetration levels. If queuing was considered, the 3S2C configuration achieved the highest
return rate for higher EV penetration levels. In cases where the environmental impact is
important, the R3S2C presented an acceptable solution without high economical comprises,
especially for lower EV penetrations. Lastly, different sensitivity cases for the HPC station
showed that system cost estimations, the distribution of EVs, and installing the system
different places on the distribution grid impact the solutions. These should therefore be
considered when designing a HPC station. The model developed in this thesis provides a
platform for further development and can be changed to fit specific investigation needs for
various configurations.
129
==PAGE:150==
8
FUTURE WORK
The solutions found in this thesis open the door for a lot of further investigations. These
will be described in this section for the different topics, including the model, the economic
estimations, the EMS control, customer satisfaction criteria, and additional configurations.
Model
Sizing of the components investigated in the model is done with reference to the system
installed at Bornholm and the NSS HPC system found in Appendix B. Therefore, a broader
range of parameters could be investigated. Especially the RES size range and resolution
are important to be adjusted to find optimal solutions within the investigated range. To
limit the computing time this could be carried out by a combination of mathematical
optimization and sensitivity analysis. First simple assumptions could be considered in a
mathematical optimization model for a large range of parameter sweeps. From this an
optimum solution can be found, which then could be used as a reference to the Monte
Carlo simulation sensitivity analysis.
The system lifetime has not been included in the model. This could be implemented to get
an overview of how the system lifetime depends on operational metrics, such as loading
level, temperature etc. The lifetime estimation could be improved by implementing a more
detailed thermal model of the system, SOC and voltage dependent battery efficiency curves,
more specific voltage level request from different connected components, and inclusion of a
state of health model. This would indicate more accurately how the system control, scaling
and loading affects the system.
The performance of a HPC station with a BESS controlled by a conventional BMS could
be made to compare the strength and weaknesses of multiple system technologies.
The RES profiles used are based on one year data. If data for longer periods are obtained
more accurate estimations of RES profiles could be included. These could also be generated
in the same manner as the EV profiles, since RES generation might also be affected by
random distributions.
The EV profiles are generated based on the traffic flow on a specific highway in England.
Ideally the EV profiles used should come from actual HPC station measurements. Since
these measurements have been found to be business sensitive information for companies,
improvements of the method used to generate EV profiles could be made. For instance
sensitivity of the distributions in the EV profiles could be implemented to investigate
different EV profile behavior.
Economy
Generally the economic considerations in this thesis have only included the actual cost of
buying and operating the system, but not discount rates and inflation for the investment.
130
==PAGE:151==
8. Future work
This could be included to get a more realistic overview of the economic model. Furthermore,
the actual component specific cost and longer yearly projections could also be investigated
more in depth. Doing so would limit the uncertainties involved in estimating the system
cost, which has a significant impact on the investment.
The pricing scheme used in this project is focusing on variable electricity prices and fixed
charging prices for EV customers. Different pricing schemes, such as subscriptions for EV
customers could be considered too. This would give a perspective of how much the time
dependent income and expenses will impact the economical feasibility.
If the lifetime is more accurately estimated in the overall model, a weighted decision
towards which solutions to choose could be included. This would give a better perspective
of the lifetime investment of such a system. The lifetime is not limited to the operation of
the BESS, but all individual components of the system, meaning that changing components
with shorter lifetime than others in the system, will result in higher system cost. Effectively
a weighted average between design priorities could also be included, such as maximum
return vs. maximum self sufficiency, which enables finding multi-objective solutions.
In the Monte Carlo simulation a fixed energy or power specific step size is considered. This
step size could be adjusted to consider the economical impact of increasing the system
components. This should of course be a trade off between performance gains, economical
impacts, and available component sizes.
EMS
Parameters such as the SOC reference could be tuned to accommodate different EV
penetration levels or RES forecasts. Other EMSs prioritizing a mix of EV charging and
auxiliary services could also be investigated to highlight other possibilities of a multipurpose
BESS. Furthermore, the EMSs used in this thesis do not necessarily load the strings equally,
the impact of this could be investigated. For instance, if one string is loaded more than
another, then the lifetime of this string might be lower, which affects the investment of the
system.
Customer satisfaction
The MASR, MISR and chance thresholds are found to have direct impact on the return rate
of the system. Since decreasing the customer satisfaction is found to improve the return
rates significantly an investigation of how much EV customers are willing to compromise
is essential to proper justify how to define the customer satisfaction criteria. Furthermore,
the different priority when generating EV profiles for the one charger setup compared
to the two charger setup is found to have significant difference in chance of queuing and
time. Therefore, a queuing chance- and time threshold should be implemented to equally
compare the systems operated by these EV profiles.
Additional cases
Various configurations with different number of strings, chargers, and EMS controls can
be investigated in the future to determine best suited configuration for specific purposes.
Interesting configurations to investigate are, e.g. a three string one charger setup to proper
compare to the 2S1C setup. Then a RES could be included in both setups for further
comparison. Furthermore additional systems with higher complexity levels can be explored.
131

==PAGE:163==
B
BATTERY HIGH POWER CHARGER NSS
DATA SHEET
This appendix contains the data sheet on the B-HPC V1.6 from Nerve Smart
System.
143
==PAGE:164==
Nerve Smart Systems’ B-HPC (Battery-backed High-Power
Charger) facilitates high-power EV charging independent of grid
limitations. The charging session is handled with a minimum of
power conversion from battery to battery (B-HPC to EV battery).
 
The Nerve Switch   operated battery system inside the B-HPC
controls the DC output by controlling each individual battery cell
independently, enabling dynamic cell stack reconfiguration. 
The battery strings can be coupled in series or in parallel
enabling the system to deliver a variable output voltage (0-
920Vdc). Thereby, the system allows for ultra-fast charging with
up to 500kW.
The Nerve B-HPC is easy to move and install without the
challenges of civil works and grid connection complexity.
